Two Opinions on Time and the Deniers' Classification
Opening of a chapter presenting two views on time and the deniers' classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main opinions on the existence of time and the basic argument of those who deny it, including their classification of existents.

Core Idea
Time may be a mental construct; its concept doesn't require a separate entity.

This chapter opens with a classic theological debate: does time exist as a real entity or is it merely a mental concept? The author first presents two opposing views, then elaborates the position of those who deny time, categorizing existents into two types and arguing that the concept of time does not necessitate an additional existent. We'll explore this foundation before examining their proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening and note the two opinions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the two broad opinions on time: denial and affirmation. The author will focus on the deniers' view.
The chapter title indicates it is presenting proofs of those who deny time.
2
Understand the deniers' classification of existents
The deniers divide existing things into two types: those that persist through successive individuals, and those that are inherently continuous. Identify these two types in the passage.
The deniers argue that in both cases, the concept of time does not require an extra entity.
3
Recognize the beginning of the first proof
The passage introduces the first proof but cuts off. Notice the structure: if time existed, it would be either eternal or temporal. The text ends mid-sentence, so the proof is incomplete here.
This incompleteness is due to the source; later lessons will continue the argument.

The First Chapter: On Presenting the Proofs of Those Who Deny Time. People have two opinions regarding time: The first is the opinion of those who deny its existence. The second is the opinion of those who affirm its existence (1). As for those who deny its existence, they said: Existing things are of two kinds: among them are those whose persistence is due to their individuals and the succession of their units, and not by being successive and consecutive, each one of them exists after non-existence, then ceases to exist after existence, and this concept does not necessitate affirming an additional existent beyond those existents and non-existents. · And among them are those whose persistence is in the sense of being continuous and perpetual in themselves. And this concept [also (2)] does not necessitate affirming an additional existent. And what we have mentioned is supported by types of proofs. The first proof: This thing called time and duration, if it existed, then it would either be continuous in existence or perishable in existence. The first is impossible, otherwise...
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage opens the first chapter dealing with those who deny the existence of time. The author first states that there are two opinions on time: denial and affirmation. He then explains the position of the deniers. They argue that existents fall into two categories: (1) those whose persistence is through successive individuals that come into being and pass away, and (2) those that are inherently continuous and perpetual. In both cases, the concept does not require positing an extra entity called 'time' over and above the things themselves. The deniers claim that time is merely a mental construct. Then the author says that they support this with various proofs, and he begins to present the first proof: if time existed, it would have to be either continuous (i.e., eternal) or perishable (temporal). The first (continuous existence) is impossible because... but the text cuts off here. The argument is incomplete in this window. Note that the footnote markers (1) and (2) likely refer to manuscript variants.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage rejects the existence of time as a real entity.
Correction: The passage does not reject time outright; it merely presents the argument of those who deny it, as part of a larger refutation. The author is laying out the opposing view before responding to it.

Checkpoint
According to those who deny time, which of the following is true about the concept of time?
A) It is an additional existent beyond objects and non-existents.
B) It does not necessitate affirming an additional existent beyond objects and non-existents.
C) It is only found in continuous existents.
D) It is a necessary attribute of all existents.

Key Takeaway
The deniers of time argue that the concept of time does not require a separate existent beyond the objects themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the limited time you have in this life, do you treat time as a real container that is running out, or as a mere measure of the changes in your own existence? How would your sense of urgency in worship shift if you truly believed time has no independent reality but is only the succession of your own moments of being and non-being?
?
The deniers argue that the concept of time does not require an extra entity. If time is not a real thing, then what does that mean for your fear of 'wasting time'? Could it be that you are overly preoccupied with a mental construct rather than with the actual quality and continuity of your deeds before Allah?
?
Consider the things that persist through a succession of individuals—like your own body and the world around you. If their persistence does not need a separate 'time', then what is it that you are truly accountable for? Is it not the actual existence of your acts, not the time in which they occur? How can this understanding deepen your focus on the present moment of obedience?

The First Proof Against the Existence of Time
Razi presents the first proof against time's existence, preceded by an overview of the two opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof advanced by those who deny the existence of time, including how it reduces the hypothesis of time's existence to an absurdity.

Core Idea
If time existed, it would either be perpetual or transient—both lead to contradiction.

Before diving into the proof, note that Razi first sets up the debate: there are two camps regarding time—those who affirm its existence and those who deny it. The deniers classify all existents into two types, and they argue that neither type requires an additional entity called 'time'. To reinforce their position, they offer several proofs. The first proof, which we will examine in detail, shows that the very concept of time, if taken as an existing entity, leads to impossible consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow. Notice the structure: first the two opinions, then the classification of existents, then the first proof with its two horns.
Take your time to understand the logical steps.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The proof considers whether time would be perpetual (continuous in existence) or transient (perishing in existence). Understand what each scenario entails.
Perpetual means time is always the same entity; transient means it has successive parts.
3
Follow the reductio for the perpetual scenario
If time is perpetual, then today would be identical to the day of the Flood, making all events simultaneous—a contradiction. Also, it would require time to have a prior and subsequent existence, leading to infinite regress.
4
Follow the reductio for the transient scenario
If time is transient, its parts are successive. Each part is said to occur 'now', but that judgment would require another time as a container, leading to infinite regress of times.
5
Conclude the proof
Since both possibilities lead to absurdities, the existence of time as an entity is impossible. Thus, time is not an independent existing thing.
This proof is a classic example of reductio ad absurdum in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول في تقرير دلائل القائلين بنفي الزمان
للناس في الزمان قولان : الأول : قول من أنكر وجوده . والثاني : قول من أثبت وجوده
أما المنكرون لوجوده . فقالوا : الموجودات على قسمين : منها ما يكون بقاؤها بسبب أفرادها وتتالي آحادها ، وبغير كونها متعاقبة متتالية ، أن كل واحد منها وجد بعد العدم ، ثم عدم بعد الوجود، وهذا المفهوم لا يقتضي إثبات موجود زائد على تلك الموجودات ، والعدمات
·
ومنها ما يكون بقاؤها بمعنى كونها مستمرة دائمة بأعيانها . وهذا المفهوم أيضاً لا يقتضى إثبات موجود زائد. ويدل على ما ذكرناه وجوه من الدلائل : 
الحجة الأولى : هذا الشيء المسمى بالزمان والمدة ، لو كان موجوداً ، لكان إما أن يكون مستمر الوجود، أو منقضي الوجود ، والأول محال ، وإلا لوجب أن يكون هذا اليوم بعينه ، هو يوم الطوفان فيلزم على هذا التقدير أن يكون الحادث في هذا اليوم، حادثاً في يوم الطوفان ، بل قبله بل أزلاً . هذا خلف محال . وأيضاً : فبتقدير كونه دائم الوجود بعينه ، فلا بد وأن يصدق عليه أنه كان موجوداً قبل ، وسيبقى موجوداً بعد . فلو كان هذا المفهوم يقتضي إثبات مدة وزمان لذلك الشيء ، فحينئذ يلزم افتقار المدة والزمان إلى مدة أخرى ، وزمان آخر . ويلزم التسلسل وهو محال . والثاني أيضاً باطل ، لأنه يقتضي أن تكون الأجزاء المفترضة فيه متعاقبة متوالية ، وحينئذ يصح في كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء أن يقال : إنه حدث الآن ، لا قبل ولا بعد . فلو كان الحكم على الشيء بأنه حدث الآن ، أو كان حادثاً قبل ذلك ، أو سيحدث بعد ذلك ، يقتضي شيئاً آخر يكون ظرفاً له ، ويكون لأجل وقوع هذا الشيء في ذلك الظرف ، يصدق عليه بأنه حصل الآن ، أو في الماضي ، أو سيحصل في المستقبل، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون للزمان زمان آخر إلى غير النهاية ، وذلك محال
The first chapter: On establishing the proofs of those who deny time. People have two opinions regarding time: the first is the opinion of the one who denies its existence, and the second is the opinion of the one who affirms its existence. As for the deniers of its existence, they said: existents are of two kinds: among them are those whose persistence is by way of their individuals and the succession of their units, and by their being successive and consecutive, that each one of them exists after non-existence, then perishes after existence; and this concept does not entail the affirmation of an existent additional to those existents and non-existences. And among them are those whose persistence is in the sense of being continuous and perpetual in their essences. This concept also does not entail the affirmation of an additional existent. There indicate what we have mentioned several proofs. The first proof: This thing called time and duration, if it existed, it would either be continuous in existence or perishing in existence. The first is impossible. Otherwise it would be necessary that this very day is the day of the Flood; thus on this supposition it would follow that the event occurring on this day occurs on the day of the Flood, nay before it, nay eternally. This is a contradiction, impossible. Also, on the supposition that it is perpetual in existence itself, it must be true of it that it existed before and will continue to exist after. So if this concept necessitates the affirmation of a duration and a time for that thing, then it would entail that duration and time require another duration and another time, and an infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. The second [supposition] is also false, because it requires that the hypothetical parts in it be successive and sequential, and then it is valid for each one of those parts to be said: it occurs now, not before nor after. So if the judgment about a thing that it occurs now, or occurred before, or will occur after, requires something else that is a container for it, and because of the occurrence of this thing in that container it is true of it that it happened now, or in the past, or will happen in the future, then it would follow that time has another time ad infinitum, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents the first part of a discussion on the existence of time. It begins by stating that there are two views: one denying time's existence, the other affirming it. The deniers classify existents into two types: those that persist through successive individuals and those that are continuous in themselves. Neither type requires an additional existent called 'time'. Then the first proof (al-ḥujjah al-ūlā) is given: if time existed, it would either be perpetual or transient. If perpetual, then today would be the same as the day of Noah's Flood, making all events simultaneous—absurd. Also, if time were perpetual, it would require a prior and subsequent existence, leading to an infinite regress of times. If transient, its parts are successive, and each part is said to occur 'now', but that judgment would require another time as a container, again leading to infinite regress. Both possibilities are impossible, so time does not exist.

Key Terms
زمان (time)
مدة (duration)
مستمر الوجود (continuous in existence)
منقضي الوجود (perishing in existence)
أجزاء مفترضة (hypothetical parts)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
حادث (event/occurrence)
خلف (contradiction/absurdity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That time is an independent entity that can exist without relation to events.
Correction: The proof shows that if time were independent, it leads to contradictions such as the identification of all events or infinite regress. Therefore, time is not an independent entity but a relational concept.

Checkpoint
What is the first consequence if time were perpetual (continuous in existence)?
A) Time would have a beginning.
B) The present day would be identical to past events like the Flood.
C) Time would be composed of indivisible parts.
D) Time would be a substance.

Key Takeaway
The first proof against time's existence shows that if time were an entity, it would be either perpetual or transient; both possibilities entail contradictions, so time cannot be an existing thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is not a container for events, then your past sins are not stored in a 'time' that holds them. How does this change your view of Allah's forgiveness? Are you still bound by a past that does not exist as an independent reality?
?
The proof shows that assuming time as an entity leads to absurdities. What other assumptions do you hold about reality that might crumble under scrutiny? Are you willing to question them, even if they seem obvious?
?
If 'this very day' is not the same as any other day due to a continuous time, then your actions are not tied to a linear container. How does this affect your sense of urgency in worshipping Allah? Are you living as if moments are separate opportunities or as if they are trapped in an illusion of time?

The Reality of Time: Permanence and Succession
The concluding summary defining time positively.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that time, according to the author, is not an independent entity but the fact that some beings are eternal and others are successive.

Core Idea
Time is nothing but the order of eternal beings and changing events.

In this concluding passage, the author of al-Maṭālib al-ʿĀliyya summarizes the outcome of the twelve proofs he presented against the independent existence of time. Instead of leaving a negative conclusion, he offers a positive alternative: time is merely the distinction between what is permanent and what is successive. This lesson unpacks that definition and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic and the literal translation slowly. Note the phrase 'لا معنى لوجود الزمان إلا كون بعض الموجودات دائمة الوجود بأعيانها، وكون بعضها أموراً محدثة متوالية متعاقبة' – this is the core claim.
2
Identify the positive alternative
The author says time's meaning is reduced to two categories: eternally existing things and originated successive things. Consider how this avoids nihilism.
This is a reductive but constructive definition.
3
Reflect on the evaluation of the proofs
The author calls all twelve proofs 'good, strong, and known'. Ask yourself: Is he endorsing them as certain? What does 'known' mean here?
4
Connect to the broader debate
Why does it matter whether time is independent or relational? This definition aligns with a theological view that only Allah is eternal by essence.
This connects to Islamic theology (kalam).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا مجموع هذه الدلائل الاثني عشر، التي استنبطناها للقائلين بأنه لا معنى لوجود الزمان ، إلا كون بعض الموجودات دائمة الوجود بأعيانها ، وكون بعضها أموراً محدثة متوالية متعاقبة. وهي بأسرها حسنة قوية معلومة . وبالله التوفيق
This is the total of these twelve proofs, which we have derived for those who say that there is no meaning to the existence of time except that some existents are eternal in their identities, and some are originated matters successive and consecutive. They are all together good, strong, and known. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is the concluding summary of the twelve proofs that have been presented against the independent existence of time. The author states that these proofs were derived for the proponents of a specific positive view: that the reality of time is nothing more than the fact that some beings are eternal in their very identities (i.e., God and perhaps the heavens) and some beings are originated events that occur in succession. In other words, time is not an independent entity but a relational notion describing permanence and succession. The author affirms that all twelve proofs are good (حسنة), strong (قوية), and known (معلومة). The passage ends with a prayer: 'And with Allah is success.'

Key Terms
دلائل
وجود الزمان
دائمة الوجود
أمور محدثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That denying time as an independent entity leads to nihilism or meaninglessness.
Correction: The author shows that time can be positively redefined as the relation between eternal and successive existents.

Checkpoint
According to the conclusion, what is the meaning of the existence of time?
A) It is an independent substance
B) It is merely the permanence of some entities and the succession of others
C) It is an attribute of God
D) It is a measure of motion

Key Takeaway
Time is not an independent reality but the relational order of eternal beings and originated events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is merely the order of eternal and successive things, then your life is a series of originated moments. How does that change the weight you give to each passing moment? Will you live today as if it is a part of that succession leading to your meeting with the Eternal?
?
You often feel pressured by time—deadlines, aging, 'running out of time.' This lesson says time has no independent reality; it is just the order of created events. Does that free you from the fear of time? If not, what are you really fearing?
?
The lesson declares that only some beings are eternal (God). Everything else is successive and temporary. What in your life are you holding onto as if it were eternal? Are you ready to release it, knowing it is only a passing moment in the order of time?

The Intuitive Knowledge of Time: Al-Razi's First Proof
Al-Razi begins an intuitive proof for the existence of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim that knowledge of time is intuitive and the first thought experiment used to support it.

Core Idea
Time is directly perceived even when all motion is stilled.

This passage opens a key debate about time. Some thinkers argue that our awareness of time is immediate and does not rely on proofs. Al-Razi begins to formulate their strongest argument through a vivid thought experiment that isolates our perception of time from all external motion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
Read the first part of the passage to see how al-Razi categorizes those who affirm time: one group claims intuitive knowledge, the other seeks proof.
Note that Muhammad ibn Zakariyya al-Razi is mentioned as a proponent of the intuitive view.
2
Examine the first proof
Focus on the thought experiment of a blind person in a dark house, stilling all movements. Try to visualize the scenario.
The proof is incomplete in this passage, but the core idea is clear.
3
Understand the intuitive claim
Ask yourself: why does this thought experiment show that time is known intuitively? The passage says the person 'finds time as a flowing thing' regardless of external motion.
4
Reflect on the incomplete nature
Notice that the passage cuts off mid-sentence. The full argument would continue, but the essential premise is already presented.
This is the start of a longer discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في ثاني تقرير قول من يقول:
العلم يكون لـ يكون المعة والزمان موجودان علم بحيضي أملي. لا يحتاج فيه الى المحجة والدليل
اعلم أن المثبتين للمدة فريقان : منهم من يدعى أن العلم بوجوده علم بديهي ضروري، غني عن البيان والبرهان. ومنهم من حاول إثباته بالبينة والبرهان . أما الفريق الأول فمنهم : محمد بن زكريا الرازي ، وقوم آخرون
أنا (١) وإن كنت ما وجدت لهم إلا ادعاء البديهة والضرورة ، إلا أني أقرر تقريراً [ أحسن وأكمل ، مما ذكروه (۲)] . وأقول : لهم أن يحتجوا على صحة
قولهم بوجوه :
الحجة الأولى : إنا لو فرضنا شخصاً غافلاً عن وجود الأفلاك والكواكب وعن طلوعها وغروبها ، بأن كان أعمى، كان جالساً في بيت مظلم ، وقدرنا أنه قصد تسكين الحركات بأسرها حتى الطرف والنفس ، فإن هذا الإنسان يجد المدة أمراً سيالا (۳) يحدث ، ويمر دائماً بلا وقوف ولا انقضاء . والعلم بذلك ضروري حتى أنه إذا اعتبر هذه الحالة من البكرة إلى الضحوة، ثم اعتبرها من الضحوة إلى وقت الظهر، فإنه مع غفلته عن حركة الشمس والقمر وسائر الكواكب والافلاك ، يعلم بالبديهة : أن الذي انقضى من البكرة إلى الضحوة ، نصف ما
In the second report of the saying of those who say: knowledge of time and space exists as necessary knowledge, not needing proof and evidence. Know that those who affirm time are two groups: some claim that knowledge of its existence is intuitive and necessary, independent of explanation and demonstration. Others attempt to prove it with evidence and demonstration. As for the first group, among them are Muhammad ibn Zakariyya al-Razi and others. I (1), even though I have found for them only the claim of intuition and necessity, yet I present a better and more complete formulation (2) than what they mentioned. And I say: they may argue for the correctness of their statement with several proofs: The first proof: If we imagine a person oblivious to the existence of the celestial spheres and stars and their rising and setting, such as a blind person sitting in a dark house, and we suppose that he intends to still all movements including the eyelid and breath, then this person finds time (al-mudda) as a flowing thing (3) that occurs and passes continuously without stopping or ending. And knowledge of that is necessary, such that if he considers this state from dawn to forenoon, then considers it from forenoon to noon, then despite his ignorance of the movement of the sun, moon, and all other stars and spheres, he knows intuitively that what elapsed from dawn to forenoon is half of what
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new section: 'the second report' on the view that knowledge of time (al-mudda/al-zaman) is intuitive. The author, al-Razi, categorizes those who affirm time into two groups: those who assert intuitive knowledge (علم بديهي ضروري) and those who seek proof. He mentions Muhammad ibn Zakariyya al-Razi as a representative of the first group. Although he finds only the claim of intuition, he aims to present a better formulation. Then he begins the first proof (الحجة الأولى) which is a thought experiment: imagine a blind person in a dark house, stilling all movements; he still perceives time as a flowing continuum. This perception is necessary (ضروري) and independent of any observation of celestial motions. The proof is incomplete as the text cuts off. The key point is that time is directly perceived intuitively, not through inference.

Key Terms
العلم البديهي
المدة
الزمان
الضرورة
الحجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is only known through observation of celestial bodies like the sun and stars.
Correction: The thought experiment shows that even a blind person in a dark house, unaware of any celestial motions, still perceives time as a flowing continuum, indicating that time is known intuitively.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof in this passage, what does a blind person in a dark house still perceive?
A) The movement of the sun and stars
B) The passage of time
C) The existence of God
D) Nothing at all

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi argues that time is known intuitively through direct experience, independent of any external motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you sat in complete stillness, without distractions, and simply felt time passing? What did you experience, and how does that relate to al-Razi's claim that time is directly perceived?
?
Al-Razi argues that time is known intuitively even without any external motion. Yet, you often measure your days by events and appointments. Do you truly experience time as a flowing reality, or have you reduced it to a sequence of tasks? What would it take for you to become more aware of time itself?
?
If your knowledge of time does not ultimately depend on the sun or clocks, what does that say about your relationship with time as a gift from the Creator? How might you change your daily rhythm to honor that direct perception?

The Objection and the Threefold Benefit
Objection and summarized threefold answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the objection against the proof of time's existence and the summarized threefold benefit that each supposition provides.

Core Idea
Three suppositions about motion each prove a different aspect of time's reality.

Before diving into the detailed mechanics, we need to grasp the overall argument. An objector points out that the proof uses three separate hypothetical scenarios and asks what each contributes. The answer gives a concise roadmap: each supposition establishes a distinct feature of time. This lesson introduces that roadmap.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's summary of the three suppositions and the question about their purpose. Notice how the objector clearly states the conclusion drawn from each.
The objector is not denying the conclusions; he is asking why all three are needed.
2
Identify the three benefits
In the answer, find the three benefits stated at the beginning: existence of time, relation to motion, and distinctness from physical quantities. These are the core claims.
The answer uses the word 'ثبت' (established) for each benefit.
3
Understand the flow
Notice that the answer then says 'وعند هذا يحصل تمام المقصود' (and by this the complete intent is attained). This signals that the summary is the logical conclusion of the proof.
The detailed explanation follows as an elaboration.

من الإمكان الأول وإذا كان كذلك كان هذا الإمكان قابلا للزيادة والنقصان فوجب كونه أمراً موجوداً لا محالة. فإن قال قائل : إنكم في مشرع هذا الاستدلال فرضتم ثلاثة أشياء : أولها : الحركتان المتساويتان في السرعة والبطء ، المتساويتان في الأخذ والترك. وزعمتم : أنهما لا بد وأن يتساويا في مقدار المسافة. وثانيهما : الحركتان المتساويتان في السرعة والبطء ، المختلفتان في وقت الأخذ ، المتساويتان في وقت الترك. زعمتم : أن هذا السريع الثاني لا بد وأن يقطع مسافة أقل مما قطعه السريع الأولى. وثالثها : الحركتان المتساويتان في الأخذ والترك المختلفتان في السرعة والبطء . زعمتم : أن البطىء لا بد وأن يقطع أقل مما قطعه السريع . فما الفائدة في هذا البرهان من الوجوه الثلاثة من الفرض ؟ قلنا : الفائدة فيها : هي أن بالفرض الأول، ثبت وجود هذا الأمر الذي سميناه بالزمان ، وبالفرض الثاني ثبت أن هذا الإمكان مساوي لنفس الحركة ، ولنفس بطئها وسرعتها ، ولنفس مقدار المتحرك . وبالفرض الثالث ثبت أن هذا الإمكان أمر مغاير لمقدار المتحرك ، ولمقدار المسافة . وعند هذا يحصل تمام المقصود . وبيان هذا الكلام : أنا إذا فرضنا أولاً وجود حركتين متساويتين في البطء والسرعة، متساويتين في الأخذ والترك. وزعمنا : أنه متى كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب حصول التساوي في مقدار المسافة ، فعند هذا يظهر أنه حصل بين أخذ هذا السريع وتركه ، إمكان قطع مثل هذه المسافة بمثل هذه السرعة ، وأن في مثل هذا القدر من الإمكان ، وبمثل هذه السرعة ، لا يمكن أن يزاد على هذه المسافة ، ولا أن ينقص منها ، فهذا الإمكان له في نفسه خصوصية ، ولا يقبل حركة بهذا القدر من السرعة ، إلا في مثل هذا القدر من المسافة ، ولا يقبل الأزيد منها ، ولا بمثلي الأقل منها ، فهذا الإمكان له في حد ذاته خصوصية وحقيقة، باعتبارها حصل له هذا الامتياز والاختصاص . وأما الفرض الثاني وهو وجود حركتين متساويتين في السرعة والبطء ، مختلفتين في الأخذ ، متساويتين في الترك، فههنا السريع الثاني يقطع من المسافة أقل، وغرضنا منه أمران : الأول : إن الإمكان الذي يحصل فيه هذا السريع الثاني ، جزء من الإمكان الذي يحصل فيه السريع الأول ، فيكون هذا الإمكان قابلا للتطبيق وللزيادة والنقصان ، فوجب كونه موجوداً قابلاً للمساواة والمفاوتة . والثاني : إن هذا السريع الأصغر مشارك للسريع الأكبر في كونه حركة ، وفي كونها سريعة ، وفي مقدار المتحرك. وخالفها في مقدار هذا الإمكان . وما به المشاركة غير ما به المباينة، فيثبت أن هذا الإمكان يجب أن يكون أمراً مغايراً لنفس ماهية الحركة، ومغايراً لبطء الحركة ولسرعتها ، ومغايراً لمقدار جوهر المتحرك . وأما الفرض الثالث وهو وجود حركتين مختلفتين في السرعة والبطء ، متساويتين في الأخذ والترك . فاعلم : أن غرضنا من هذا الفرض : بيان أن هذا الإمكان مغاير لمقدار المتحرك، ولمقدار المسافة ، وذلك لأنه ظهر بهذا
From the first possibility. And if it is such, then this possibility is susceptible to increase and decrease, so it must be an existent thing necessarily. For if a sayer says: In the path of this inference you have supposed three things: first: two motions equal in speed and slowness, equal in starting and stopping. You claim that they must be equal in the amount of distance. Second: two motions equal in speed and slowness, different in the time of starting, equal in the time of stopping. You claim that this second quick one must traverse less distance than the first quick one. Third: two motions equal in starting and stopping, different in speed and slowness. You claim that the slow one must traverse less than the quick one. So what is the benefit in this proof of the three aspects of the supposition? We said: The benefit in them is that by the first supposition, the existence of this thing which we have named time is proven; by the second supposition, it is proven that this possibility is equal to the motion itself, to its slowness and speed, and to the amount of the moving object; and by the third supposition, it is proven that this possibility is something different from the amount of the moving object and from the amount of distance. And by this, the complete intent is attained. And the explanation of this statement: When we suppose first the existence of two motions equal in slowness and speed, equal in starting and stopping, and we claim that whenever it is such, equality in the amount of distance must occur, then it appears that there occurs between the start and stop of this quick one the possibility of traversing such a distance with such speed, and that in such measure of possibility and with such speed, it is not possible to increase or decrease from this distance. So this possibility has in itself a particularity, and it does not accept a motion with this measure of speed except in such measure of distance, and it does not accept more than it, nor the double of less than it. So this possibility has in its very essence particularity and reality, by virtue of which this distinction and specialization occurred. As for the second supposition, i.e., the existence of two motions equal in speed and slowness, different in starting, equal in stopping, here the second quick one traverses less distance. Our purpose from it is two things: First: that the possibility which occurs in this second quick one is a part of the possibility which occurs in the first quick one, so this possibility is susceptible to division and to increase and decrease, so it must be existent, susceptible to equality and difference. Second: that this smaller quick one shares with the larger quick one in being motion, in being quick, and in the amount of the moving object, and differs from it in the amount of this possibility. And that by which there is sharing is other than that by which there is difference. So it is proven that this possibility must be something different from the very essence of motion, different from the slowness of motion and its speed, and different from the amount of the substance of the moving object. And as for the third supposition, i.e., the existence of two motions different in speed and slowness, equal in starting and stopping. Know that our purpose from this supposition is to show that this possibility is different from the amount of the moving object and from the amount of distance, because by this it appears
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure within a proof for the existence of time (al-zaman) as a distinct entity. The objector points out that the argument uses three separate suppositions (furud) about motions and asks what the point of each is. The answer explains the specific benefit of each supposition.

First, the objector summarizes the three suppositions: (1) two motions equal in speed and duration; (2) two motions equal in speed but with different starting times; (3) two motions with different speeds but equal duration. For each, the objector notes the conclusion drawn (equal distances, less distance for delayed start, less distance for slower motion). Then the objector asks: what is the point of these three aspects of the proof?

The answer gives the purpose of each: (1) the first supposition establishes the existence of the thing called time; (2) the second supposition shows that this possibility (imkan) is equal to the motion itself, its speed and slowness, and the amount of the moving object; (3) the third supposition shows that this possibility is distinct from the amount of the moving object and the distance. The answer then elaborates on each.

For the first supposition (equal motions), the author explains that when we suppose two equal motions, there arises a specific possibility (imkan) of covering exactly that distance at that speed, which cannot be increased or decreased. This possibility has a particularity in itself, showing it is a real attribute.

For the second supposition (delayed start), there are two aims. The first aim: the possibility in the second motion is part of the possibility in the first, so possibility is divisible and subject to increase/decrease, hence it must be existent and capable of equality and difference. The second aim: the smaller quick motion shares with the larger in being motion, speed, and amount of moving object, but differs in the amount of possibility. This shows possibility is distinct from motion, speed, slowness, and the substance of the moving object.

For the third supposition (different speeds), the purpose is to show that possibility is different from the amount of the moving object and the distance, as becomes apparent from this supposition.

The passage thus uses empirical assumptions about motion to argue for a distinct entity underlying temporal variation, namely time itself.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what does the second supposition prove?
A) The existence of time
B) That possibility is equal to the motion itself, its speed/slowness, and the moving object's amount
C) That possibility is different from the moving object and distance
D) That motion is impossible without time

Key Takeaway
The three suppositions are not arbitrary; each establishes a distinct property of time: its existence, its relation to motion, and its distinctness from physical objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is a real entity distinct from motion and objects, how does your daily perception of time as a passing of moments affect your awareness of being alive? Do you treat time as something you own or as a created reality that owns you? How would your actions tomorrow change if you truly believed each moment is a distinct gift from the Creator?
?
The third supposition proves that time is different from your physical progress and achievements. In what areas of your life do you still measure your worth by how far you've moved or how much you've accomplished? Could your anxiety about 'wasting time' be rooted in equating time with worldly gain? What would it look like to revere time as a sign of Allah's creation, not just a resource to exploit?
?
This proof uses three suppositions to establish time's reality, but do you live as though time is an illusion or merely a convenience? When you are honest with yourself, do you act as if time is just a backdrop for your plans or as a divine decree that shapes every moment? What commitment can you make today to honor time as a real, God-given trust?

The First Supposition: Establishing Time's Existence
Detailed explanation of the first supposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first supposition of two equal motions reveals a fixed potential that cannot be altered, thereby proving the existence of a real thing called time.

Core Idea
Equal motions show that the possibility of covering a distance is fixed, indicating a real attribute.

The first supposition involves two motions identical in speed and duration. The argument shows that such a motion can only cover exactly a certain distance—no more, no less. This fixedness points to a real underlying attribute that determines the motion's scope. In this lesson, we trace the reasoning that leads to the affirmation of time's existence.

Guided Path
1
Set up the supposition
Imagine two motions equal in speed and slowness, starting and stopping at the same times. They must traverse equal distances. Consider the 'possibility' (imkan) inherent in one such motion.
Think of possibility as the potential for motion to cover a distance.
2
See the fixedness of possibility
For a given motion of this speed and duration, the distance is fixed: it cannot be increased or decreased. This shows that the possibility has a specific measure and particularity.
This particularity is a property of the possibility itself, not of the moving object or distance.
3
Conclude that possibility is real
Because this possibility has a real particularity and cannot be altered, it must be an existent thing. This is the existence of time.
The text says: 'فهذا الإمكان له في حد ذاته خصوصية وحقيقة'.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure within a proof for the existence of time (al-zaman) as a distinct entity. The objector points out that the argument uses three separate suppositions (furud) about motions and asks what the point of each is. The answer explains the specific benefit of each supposition.

First, the objector summarizes the three suppositions: (1) two motions equal in speed and duration; (2) two motions equal in speed but with different starting times; (3) two motions with different speeds but equal duration. For each, the objector notes the conclusion drawn (equal distances, less distance for delayed start, less distance for slower motion). Then the objector asks: what is the point of these three aspects of the proof?

The answer gives the purpose of each: (1) the first supposition establishes the existence of the thing called time; (2) the second supposition shows that this possibility (imkan) is equal to the motion itself, its speed and slowness, and the amount of the moving object; (3) the third supposition shows that this possibility is distinct from the amount of the moving object and the distance. The answer then elaborates on each.

For the first supposition (equal motions), the author explains that when we suppose two equal motions, there arises a specific possibility (imkan) of covering exactly that distance at that speed, which cannot be increased or decreased. This possibility has a particularity in itself, showing it is a real attribute.

For the second supposition (delayed start), there are two aims. The first aim: the possibility in the second motion is part of the possibility in the first, so possibility is divisible and subject to increase/decrease, hence it must be existent and capable of equality and difference. The second aim: the smaller quick motion shares with the larger in being motion, speed, and amount of moving object, but differs in the amount of possibility. This shows possibility is distinct from motion, speed, slowness, and the substance of the moving object.

For the third supposition (different speeds), the purpose is to show that possibility is different from the amount of the moving object and the distance, as becomes apparent from this supposition.

The passage thus uses empirical assumptions about motion to argue for a distinct entity underlying temporal variation, namely time itself.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the fixedness of distance is due to the moving object or the motion itself, not a separate entity.
Correction: The argument shows that the fixedness is a property of the possibility (imkan) that lies between the motion's start and stop, which is neither the motion nor the object nor the distance.

Checkpoint
According to the first supposition, what does the fixed nature of the possibility indicate?
A) That the motion is perfectly regular
B) That the possibility has a real particularity and is an existent thing
C) That the moving object must be uniform
D) That distance and speed are identical

Key Takeaway
The first supposition shows that the possibility inherent in motion has a fixed measure, proving it is a real entity—this is time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every possibility in your life has a fixed measure set by Allah, how does that challenge the way you use your time? In the next 24 hours, what specific step will you take to align your possibilities with the purpose for which you were created?
?
The passage teaches that the possibility of a motion is a real, fixed attribute. Your life’s span is a set of possibilities. Do you treat each moment as a real, irreplaceable gift from Allah, or do you waste it as if it were infinite? What change must you make today?

The Second Supposition: Divisibility and Distinctness of Possibility
Detailed explanation of the second supposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second supposition (delayed start) proves that possibility is divisible, subject to increase/decrease, and distinct from motion, speed, and the moving object.

Core Idea
A delayed start shows that possibility can be divided, proving it is a real attribute separate from motion.

The second supposition compares two motions of equal speed and slowness, one starting later but both stopping simultaneously. The later motion covers less distance. This allows us to analyze possibility in terms of parts and differences, revealing that possibility is not identical to motion or its qualities. This lesson unpacks the two key conclusions drawn from this supposition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Two motions equal in speed, but the second starts later and stops at the same time as the first. The second covers less distance. Identify the possibility in each.
The second motion's possibility is a portion of the first's possibility.
2
First aim: divisibility
The possibility in the second is part of the possibility in the first. This shows possibility can be divided and is subject to increase and decrease, proving it is an existent attribute capable of equality and difference.
If possibility were not real, it couldn't have parts or degrees.
3
Second aim: distinctness from motion
Both motions share being motion, speed, and moving object amount, but differ in the amount of possibility. Therefore, possibility is distinct from motion, its speed/slowness, and the substance of the moving object.
The shared features are identical, only the possibility differs—so possibility is not those shared features.

من الإمكان الأول وإذا كان كذلك كان هذا الإمكان قابلا للزيادة والنقصان فوجب كونه أمراً موجوداً لا محالة. فإن قال قائل : إنكم في مشرع هذا الاستدلال فرضتم ثلاثة أشياء : أولها : الحركتان المتساويتان في السرعة والبطء ، المتساويتان في الأخذ والترك. وزعمتم : أنهما لا بد وأن يتساويا في مقدار المسافة. وثانيهما : الحركتان المتساويتان في السرعة والبطء ، المختلفتان في وقت الأخذ ، المتساويتان في وقت الترك. زعمتم : أن هذا السريع الثاني لا بد وأن يقطع مسافة أقل مما قطعه السريع الأولى. وثالثها : الحركتان المتساويتان في الأخذ والترك المختلفتان في السرعة والبطء . زعمتم : أن البطىء لا بد وأن يقطع أقل مما قطعه السريع . فما الفائدة في هذا البرهان من الوجوه الثلاثة من الفرض ؟ قلنا : الفائدة فيها : هي أن بالفرض الأول، ثبت وجود هذا الأمر الذي سميناه بالزمان ، وبالفرض الثاني ثبت أن هذا الإمكان مساوي لنفس الحركة ، ولنفس بطئها وسرعتها ، ولنفس مقدار المتحرك . وبالفرض الثالث ثبت أن هذا الإمكان أمر مغاير لمقدار المتحرك ، ولمقدار المسافة . وعند هذا يحصل تمام المقصود . وبيان هذا الكلام : أنا إذا فرضنا أولاً وجود حركتين متساويتين في البطء والسرعة، متساويتين في الأخذ والترك. وزعمنا : أنه متى كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب حصول التساوي في مقدار المسافة ، فعند هذا يظهر أنه حصل بين أخذ هذا السريع وتركه ، إمكان قطع مثل هذه المسافة بمثل هذه السرعة ، وأن في مثل هذا القدر من الإمكان ، وبمثل هذه السرعة ، لا يمكن أن يزاد على هذه المسافة ، ولا أن ينقص منها ، فهذا الإمكان له في نفسه خصوصية ، ولا يقبل حركة بهذا القدر من السرعة ، إلا في مثل هذا القدر من المسافة ، ولا يقبل الأزيد منها ، ولا بمثلي الأقل منها ، فهذا الإمكان له في حد ذاته خصوصية وحقيقة، باعتبارها حصل له هذا الامتياز والاختصاص . وأما الفرض الثاني وهو وجود حركتين متساويتين في السرعة والبطء ، مختلفتين في الأخذ ، متساويتين في الترك، فههنا السريع الثاني يقطع من المسافة أقل، وغرضنا منه أمران : الأول : إن الإمكان الذي يحصل فيه هذا السريع الثاني ، جزء من الإمكان الذي يحصل فيه السريع الأول ، فيكون هذا الإمكان قابلا للتطبيق وللزيادة والنقصان ، فوجب كونه موجوداً قابلاً للمساواة والمفاوتة . والثاني : إن هذا السريع الأصغر مشارك للسريع الأكبر في كونه حركة ، وفي كونها سريعة ، وفي مقدار المتحرك. وخالفها في مقدار هذا الإمكان . وما به المشاركة غير ما به المباينة، فيثبت أن هذا الإمكان يجب أن يكون أمراً مغايراً لنفس ماهية الحركة، ومغايراً لبطء الحركة ولسرعتها ، ومغايراً لمقدار جوهر المتحرك . وأما الفرض الثالث وهو وجود حركتين مختلفتين في السرعة والبطء ، متساويتين في الأخذ والترك . فاعلم : أن غرضنا من هذا الفرض : بيان أن هذا الإمكان مغاير لمقدار المتحرك، ولمقدار المسافة ، وذلك لأنه ظهر بهذا
From the first possibility. And if it is such, then this possibility is susceptible to increase and decrease, so it must be an existent thing necessarily. For if a sayer says: In the path of this inference you have supposed three things: first: two motions equal in speed and slowness, equal in starting and stopping. You claim that they must be equal in the amount of distance. Second: two motions equal in speed and slowness, different in the time of starting, equal in the time of stopping. You claim that this second quick one must traverse less distance than the first quick one. Third: two motions equal in starting and stopping, different in speed and slowness. You claim that the slow one must traverse less than the quick one. So what is the benefit in this proof of the three aspects of the supposition? We said: The benefit in them is that by the first supposition, the existence of this thing which we have named time is proven; by the second supposition, it is proven that this possibility is equal to the motion itself, to its slowness and speed, and to the amount of the moving object; and by the third supposition, it is proven that this possibility is something different from the amount of the moving object and from the amount of distance. And by this, the complete intent is attained. And the explanation of this statement: When we suppose first the existence of two motions equal in slowness and speed, equal in starting and stopping, and we claim that whenever it is such, equality in the amount of distance must occur, then it appears that there occurs between the start and stop of this quick one the possibility of traversing such a distance with such speed, and that in such measure of possibility and with such speed, it is not possible to increase or decrease from this distance. So this possibility has in itself a particularity, and it does not accept a motion with this measure of speed except in such measure of distance, and it does not accept more than it, nor the double of less than it. So this possibility has in its very essence particularity and reality, by virtue of which this distinction and specialization occurred. As for the second supposition, i.e., the existence of two motions equal in speed and slowness, different in starting, equal in stopping, here the second quick one traverses less distance. Our purpose from it is two things: First: that the possibility which occurs in this second quick one is a part of the possibility which occurs in the first quick one, so this possibility is susceptible to division and to increase and decrease, so it must be existent, susceptible to equality and difference. Second: that this smaller quick one shares with the larger quick one in being motion, in being quick, and in the amount of the moving object, and differs from it in the amount of this possibility. And that by which there is sharing is other than that by which there is difference. So it is proven that this possibility must be something different from the very essence of motion, different from the slowness of motion and its speed, and different from the amount of the substance of the moving object. And as for the third supposition, i.e., the existence of two motions different in speed and slowness, equal in starting and stopping. Know that our purpose from this supposition is to show that this possibility is different from the amount of the moving object and from the amount of distance, because by this it appears
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure within a proof for the existence of time (al-zaman) as a distinct entity. The objector points out that the argument uses three separate suppositions (furud) about motions and asks what the point of each is. The answer explains the specific benefit of each supposition.

First, the objector summarizes the three suppositions: (1) two motions equal in speed and duration; (2) two motions equal in speed but with different starting times; (3) two motions with different speeds but equal duration. For each, the objector notes the conclusion drawn (equal distances, less distance for delayed start, less distance for slower motion). Then the objector asks: what is the point of these three aspects of the proof?

The answer gives the purpose of each: (1) the first supposition establishes the existence of the thing called time; (2) the second supposition shows that this possibility (imkan) is equal to the motion itself, its speed and slowness, and the amount of the moving object; (3) the third supposition shows that this possibility is distinct from the amount of the moving object and the distance. The answer then elaborates on each.

For the first supposition (equal motions), the author explains that when we suppose two equal motions, there arises a specific possibility (imkan) of covering exactly that distance at that speed, which cannot be increased or decreased. This possibility has a particularity in itself, showing it is a real attribute.

For the second supposition (delayed start), there are two aims. The first aim: the possibility in the second motion is part of the possibility in the first, so possibility is divisible and subject to increase/decrease, hence it must be existent and capable of equality and difference. The second aim: the smaller quick motion shares with the larger in being motion, speed, and amount of moving object, but differs in the amount of possibility. This shows possibility is distinct from motion, speed, slowness, and the substance of the moving object.

For the third supposition (different speeds), the purpose is to show that possibility is different from the amount of the moving object and the distance, as becomes apparent from this supposition.

The passage thus uses empirical assumptions about motion to argue for a distinct entity underlying temporal variation, namely time itself.

Checkpoint
What does the second aim of the second supposition prove about possibility?
A) That it is divisible
B) That it is the same as motion
C) That it is distinct from the essence of motion, speed, and the moving object's substance
D) That it is identical to distance

Key Takeaway
The second supposition proves that possibility is a real, divisible attribute distinct from motion, speed, and the moving object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If possibility is a real attribute that can be divided, then the possibilities in your life are not vague ideas but real, measurable capacities. What is one possibility you have been treating as unreal or unimportant? Will you commit to recognizing it as real and acting on it this week?
?
The passage distinguishes possibility from motion and speed. In your own life, where have you confused mere activity (motion) with actual capacity (possibility)? Identify one area where you have mistaken busyness for progress, and resolve to separate the two.
?
The second motion's possibility is a part of the first's. When you compare your own possibilities to others, do you fall into envy or despair? How can you humbly accept the portion of possibility Allah has given you without comparison? Write down a specific change in your mindset.

The Third Supposition: Distinguishing Possibility from Physical Quantities
Partial explanation of the third supposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third supposition (different speeds, same duration) shows that possibility is different from the amount of the moving object and from the distance traversed.

Core Idea
Different speeds with the same duration reveal that possibility is independent of distance and moving object.

The third supposition considers two motions with different speeds but equal start and stop times. The slower motion covers less distance. This simple fact is used to argue that the underlying possibility (time) is not the same as the distance or the moving object's magnitude. In this lesson, we examine the reasoning, though the passage cuts off mid-explanation.

Guided Path
1
Review the third supposition
Two motions equal in start and stop, but different in speed. The slower traverses less distance. The claim is that this shows possibility is different from the moving object's magnitude and distance.
The text explicitly states the purpose: 'بيان أن هذا الإمكان مغاير لمقدار المتحرك، ولمقدار المسافة'.
2
Understand the intended logic
By varying speed while keeping duration fixed, the distance changes. Since distance varies while the moving object's magnitude could be the same, possibility (time) cannot be identical to either.
The full explanation is incomplete in the source, but the goal is clear.
3
Connect to the overall proof
This supposition finalizes the distinctness of time from all physical quantities, completing the threefold proof.
Together with the first two suppositions, we have existence, relation to motion, and distinctness from physical quantities.

Permanent and Non-Permanent Existence: Defining Time's Nature
Defines permanent/non-permanent and proves time non-permanent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between permanent and non-permanent existence and how it proves that time is a distinct non-permanent entity.

Core Idea
Time's parts cannot coexist, so it is non-permanent, unlike bodily things.

In classical Islamic metaphysics, understanding the nature of time is crucial. Before engaging with the proof, the author establishes two fundamental categories of existence: permanent (where parts are together) and non-permanent (where parts are sequential). This passage defines these categories and then applies them to time, showing that time must be non-permanent and distinct from physical bodies and their attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the two types of existence: permanent (qār al-wujūd) and non-permanent (ghayr qār al-wujūd). Note that permanent means all parts exist simultaneously.
The Arabic terms are technical; pay attention to the wording.
2
Understand the proof for time
The author uses intuitive reason (badīhat al-ʿaql) to argue that two parts of time (e.g., two years) cannot exist together. This shows time is non-permanent.
The proof relies on the impossibility of simultaneous existence of any two temporal parts.
3
Apply the conclusion
Since body and its accidents are permanent, and time is non-permanent, the author concludes that time is a distinct entity from them.
This is a foundational argument for the reality of time as a separate substance in kalam.
4
Reflect on the method
Notice how the argument combines definition, intuitive judgment, and comparison to establish a metaphysical point.

الوجود ، ومنها ما لا يكون كذلك ، والمراد من قولنا : فإن الوجود : هو الذي تكون أجزاؤه حاصلة معاً . والمراد من قولنا : غير قار الوجود : ما لا يكون كذلك . فنقول : هذا الشيء الذي يمكن تقسيمه إلى السنين ، والشهور والأيام والساعات ليس من الموجودات التي تكون قارة الوجود ، بدليل أن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأنه لا يوجد جزءان منه معاً فلا يمكن حصول سنتين معاً ولا حصول شهرين ، ولا حصول يومين ، ولا حصول ساعتين معاً . ولا يمكن أيضاً أن يجتمع في الوجود منها جزءان ، وإن كانا أصغر الأجزاء ، بل كل اثنين يفرضان ، فإنه لا بد وأن يكون أحدهما متقدماً على الآخر ، وإنه لا بد عند حصول أحدهما ، أن لا يكون الآخر حاصلاً ، وكل ذلك معلوم في البديهة ، بشرط حصول التأمل الصافي، واستحضار مفهومات هذه الألفاظ في الذهن. فيثبت : أن هذا الشيء المسمى بالمدة والزمان موجود ليس قار الوجود، لكن ثبت أن الجسم وكمه وكيفه وأينه ووضعه ومضافه وسائر مقولاته ، أمور قارة ، فوجب القطع بأن هذا الشيء المسمى بالمدة والزمان أمر مغاير لهذه الأشياء بأسرها.
Existence, and among them some are not like that. And what is meant by our saying: 'permanent existence' is that whose parts are obtained together. And what is meant by our saying: 'non-permanent existence' is what is not like that. So we say: This thing which can be divided into years, months, days, and hours is not among the existents that have permanent existence, as evidenced by the fact that the natural intellect judges that two parts of it do not exist together. So it is impossible to have two years together, nor two months, nor two days, nor two hours together. Nor can two parts of it, even the smallest, come together in existence. Rather, any two that are supposed, one must precede the other, and when one occurs, the other must not exist. All of that is known intuitively, provided one has pure reflection and brings to mind the concepts of these words. Thus it is established that this thing called duration and time is an existent that is non-permanent. But it is established that body and its quantity, quality, location, position, relation, and the rest of its categories are permanent matters. So it is necessary to conclude that this thing called duration and time is something other than all these things.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage first defines two types of existence: permanent (qār al-wujūd) and non-permanent (ghayr qār al-wujūd). Permanent existence means that all parts of the existent are present simultaneously, like a solid object. Non-permanent existence means the opposite, i.e., its parts cannot coexist. The author then applies this to time: time can be divided into years, months, days, hours. By intuitive reason (badīhat al-ʿaql), we know that two years or two hours cannot exist together; one must precede the other. This proves time is non-permanent. But body and its accidents (quantity, quality, etc.) are permanent. Therefore time is distinct from all those things. This is a foundational argument in kalam metaphysics to establish the reality of time as a separate entity.

غير قار الوجود

بديهة العقل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is a permanent substance whose parts exist together.
Correction: The passage argues that time's parts cannot coexist, so time is non-permanent, not permanent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the criterion for an existent to be qār al-wujūd (permanent existence)?
A) It cannot be divided into parts.
B) Its parts exist together simultaneously.
C) It is independent of time.
D) It is mental.

Key Takeaway
Time is an existent that is non-permanent because its parts cannot coexist, and it is distinct from permanent bodies and their attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You live your life in time, yet this lesson proves that each moment is unique and cannot coexist with another. Are you truly aware that the moment you just read is gone forever? How will you use this awareness to make each remaining moment count for what lasts eternally?

If time is non-permanent and distinct from your body and its attributes, what does that mean for your attachment to fleeting worldly gains? Will you continue to chase what passes, or will you invest your time in what is permanent—your faith and character?
?
The argument here relies on pure reflection to see the truth. When was the last time you sincerely reflected on the nature of your own existence, separate from the distractions of daily life? Commit now to a regular practice of deep reflection (tafakkur) on the signs of Allah, starting with the nature of time itself.

Proof that Time is Not Motion
Argument that time cannot be identical to motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that demonstrates why time cannot be identical to motion.

Core Idea
Motion depends on time; time does not depend on motion.

In this passage, the author sets out a clear argument to show that time is fundamentally distinct from motion. He first lays out the logical possibilities for what time could be, then systematically eliminates the idea that time is motion. By analyzing the nature of motion and its dependence on successive times, he establishes that time must be something other than motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author begins with a tripartite division.
2
Identify the argument structure
The author sets up three possibilities for what time could be: motion, a property of motion, or something else. He dismisses the first as false and then provides a new argument.
The refutation of the first option is based on earlier proofs.
3
Understand the key concepts
Grasp what 'successive occurrences' and 'consecutive times' mean. Motion is defined as a series of occurrences in places that are adjacent, and their succession requires consecutive times.
4
Explore the dependency relation
Motion depends on time because its essence involves successive times. But time does not depend on motion because we can conceive a duration without any motion or change.
This asymmetry proves that time is not motion.
5
Draw the conclusion
The dependency argument forces the conclusion that time must be something other than motion. Reflect on why this matters for understanding the nature of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : هذا الشيء إما أن يكون هو الحركة أو صفة من صفات الحركة ، أو شيء مغاير للحركة ولجميع صفات الحركة . والأول باطل للوجوه الكثيرة التي شرحناها وقررناها في الفصل المتقدم . والذي نزيده الآن أن نقول : لا معنى للحركة إلا حصولات متعاقبة في أحياز متعاقبة متلاصقة ، ولا معنى لتعاقب تلك الحصولات ، إلا حصول بعضها عقيب البعض في الأزمنة المتوالية ، لكن تواليها في تلك الأزمنة إضافة بينها وبين تلك الأزمنة والإضافة بينها وبين الأزمنة متأخرة عن تقرر حصول تلك الأزمنة فثبت : أن الحركة مفتقرة في تحقق ماهيتها إلى الزمان ، وأما الزمان فإنه غني في ذاته عن الحركة ، لأنه يمكننا فرض مدة خالية عن جميع الحركات وجميع التغيرات ، وهذا يوجب القطع بأن الزمان أمر مغاير للحركة
Then we say: This thing is either motion or a property of motion, or something other than motion and all its properties. The first is false for many reasons we have explained and established in the previous chapter. What we now add is to say: There is no meaning of motion except successive occurrences in successive contiguous places, and there is no meaning of the succession of those occurrences except the occurrence of some after others in consecutive times. But their consecutiveness in those times is a relation between them and those times, and the relation between them and the times is posterior to the establishment of those times. So it is established that motion, in the realization of its essence, is dependent on time. As for time, it is independent in itself of motion, because we can conceive a duration empty of all motions and all changes. This necessitates the conclusion that time is something other than motion.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage argues that time cannot be identical to motion. The author first presents three possibilities: time is motion, or a property of motion, or something else. He dismisses the first as false, referring to earlier proofs. Then he gives a new argument: motion is defined as successive occurrences in places, and succession requires consecutive times. The relation between motion and time is such that motion depends on time for its existence, but time does not depend on motion (we can imagine time without motion). Therefore time is distinct from motion.

Misconception: One might think time is just motion.
Correction: The argument shows that motion depends on time for its definition, whereas time can exist without motion, so they cannot be the same.

B) Because motion requires successive times for its occurrence.
C) Because time is measured by motion.
D) Because motion and time are identical.

Key Takeaway
Time is not motion because motion requires time to exist, but time can exist without motion.

Proof that Time is Not a Property of Motion
Demonstrating that time cannot be a property of motion using circularity and independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why time cannot be a property of motion, using two arguments: circularity and independence.

Core Idea
Time is an independent container for motion, not a property of it.

The author now addresses the possibility that time might be a property of motion. He presents two clear arguments: one based on circularity (dawr) and one based on independence. This passage firmly establishes time as a distinct entity that serves as a container for all changes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the use of 'فلو' (if) and 'يلزم' (it follows) which signal logical steps.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage contains two separate proofs: one based on circularity and one based on independence. Find where each begins.
The first argument starts with 'فلو كان الزمان...' and the second with 'وأيضاً...'.
3
Understand the circularity argument
If time were a property of motion, time would depend on motion. But motion depends on time (already proven). This creates a circular dependency, which is logically impossible.
Circularity (dawr) is a fundamental logical error.
4
Understand the independence argument
Time is independent of motion (proven earlier). But every property of motion depends on motion. Therefore, time cannot be a property of motion.
This argument is simpler: it uses the principle that a property depends on its subject.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that time is a non-permanent existent, a container (zarf/wi'a) for motions and changes. This is a key concept in kalam cosmology.
Contrast this view with modern physics: time here is absolute, not relative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الزمان عبارة عن صفة من صفات الحركة : فهو أن صفة الحركة مفتقرة في تحققها إلى حصول الحركة ، فلو كان الزمان عبارة عن صفة من صفات الحركة ، لكان الزمان مفتقراً في تحقق ماهيته إلى تحقق الحركة. لكنا قد بينا : أن الحركة مفتقرة في تحقق ماهيتها إلى تحقق الزمان ، فيلزم الدور وهو محال . وأيضاً : فقد بينا : أن الزمان غني في تحقق ماهيته عن الحركة ، لكن كل ما كان من صفات الحركة ، فهو مفتقر في تحقق حقيقته إلى الحركة. ينتج : أن الزمان والمدة يمتنع أن يكون عبارة عن صفة من صفات الحركة . فثبت : أن الزمان موجود غير قار الذات وثبت : أنه ليس بحركة ولا صفة من صفات الحركة ، بل هو كالظرف والوعاء للحركات والتغيرات. ولا نريد بالزمان الذي حاولنا إثباته إلا ذلك
As for the demonstration that it is not permissible for time to be a property of motion: it is that the property of motion is dependent in its realization on the occurrence of motion. So if time were a property of motion, then time would be dependent in the realization of its essence on the realization of motion. But we have shown that motion is dependent in the realization of its essence on the realization of time. Thus circularity follows, which is impossible. Also, we have shown that time is independent in the realization of its essence of motion, but everything that is a property of motion is dependent in the realization of its reality on motion. This yields that time and duration cannot be a property of motion. So it is established that time is an existent that is non-permanent in essence, and it is established that it is neither motion nor a property of motion, but rather it is like a container and receptacle for motions and changes. And we do not mean by 'time' which we have attempted to prove except that.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage continues the previous argument by eliminating the second possibility: that time is a property of motion. The author uses two proofs. First, if time were a property of motion, then time would depend on motion. But we already proved motion depends on time. This would create a circular dependency (dawr), which is logically impossible. Second, we proved time is independent of motion, but every property of motion depends on motion. Therefore time cannot be a property of motion. The conclusion is that time is a distinct entity, non-permanent, serving as a container for motions and changes. This is a classic kalam argument for the reality of time.

Correction: The passage proves that time cannot be a property of motion because that would lead to circularity or contradiction with time's independence.

Key Takeaway
Time is an independent, non-permanent existent that serves as a container for motion and change, not a property of motion.

?

?
Time is real and non-permanent; it does not wait for you. This lesson strips away the excuse that 'I need to move first to have time.' Refutation of the Infinite Regress Objection
Response to the objection about infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection of infinite regress does not apply to this particular proof for the existence of time as a distinct entity.

Core Idea
The intuitive intellect rejects that time needs a container, so no regress.

The author has just established a proof for the existence of time by showing it is neither a body nor an accident. Now he anticipates a logical objection: doesn't this proof lead to an infinite regress of times? In this passage, he clarifies that the objection only holds if our reason naturally assumes time has a container, but our primary intuitions actually reject that notion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get its flow, then read the English translation. Notice the author's confident tone in dismissing the objection.
The passage is a single continuous argument, not a list.
2
Identify the objection stated
The author refers to an earlier charge: that his previous two proofs forced time to need another time ad infinitum. Now he addresses this objection.
This is not a new objection but a response to an earlier criticism.
3
Understand the refutation's core
The author argues that this proof only necessitates a container for time if the intellect naturally judges that time has a container. Since it does not, the objection fails.
The key phrase is 'فطرة العقل' (natural disposition of the intellect).
4
Reflect on the role of intuition
Contrast this use of intuition with other theological arguments. Here, the author relies on the absence of an intuitive judgment to block the regress.
In other contexts, intuition is used affirmatively; here it is used negatively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنا كنا الزمنا على الدليلين الأوليين افتقار الزمان إلى زمان آخر إلى غير النهاية ، فيجب أن يعلم ههنا : أن ذلك السؤال غير لازم على هذا الدليل . وذلك لأنا ههنا ادعينا حصول العلم الضروري ، بحصول شيء يقسم إلى السنين والشهور والأيام والساعات ، ثم بينا أن ذلك الشيء ليس كذا ولا كذا ، فثبت أنه شيء آخر وهذا الكلام إنما يلزم عليه إثبات زمان للزمان ، لو حكمت فطرة العقل بأن الشيء الذي سميناه بالزمان له ظرف آخر ، ولما لم يحصل هذا المعنى في بدائه العقول، بل بديهة العقل دافعة لذلك، علمنا : أن هذا السؤال غير لازم على هذا الوجه
And know that we had imposed on the first two proofs that time needs another time ad infinitum. So it must be known here that this objection does not apply to this proof. That is because we here claimed the attainment of necessary knowledge of the existence of something that is divided into years, months, days, and hours. Then we showed that that thing is not such and such, so it is established that it is something else. This argument would only entail establishing a time for time if the natural disposition of the intellect judged that the thing we call time has another container. But since this meaning is not found in the primary intuitions of the intellects—rather the natural intellect repels it—we know that this objection does not apply to this line of reasoning.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection: that the proof for time itself requires another time (infinite regress). The author responds that this objection does not apply to the current proof because it does not assume time has a container. The proof merely establishes the existence of something divisible into time units, and then shows it is not a body or attribute, thus proving it is something else. To demand another time would require that our concept of time implies a container, which the intuitive intellect does not accept. Therefore the objection is invalid.

Misconception: One might think that proving time inevitably leads to an infinite regress of times, making the proof self-defeating.
Correction: The proof does not assume time has a container; it only establishes time as something divisible and not a body/accident. The regress would only follow if the intellect naturally required a container for time, which it does not.

Key Takeaway
