Six Definitions of Time: A Doxographical Survey
Six definitions of time enumerated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand six distinct philosophical definitions of time as presented by Islamic philosophers.

Core Idea
Time is defined in radically different ways by different philosophical schools.

Islamic philosophers debated the nature of time, proposing definitions ranging from a measure of motion to an eternal substance. This passage presents six views, starting with Aristotle and ending with the ancient philosophers. As you read, note how each definition reflects a different metaphysical commitment.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without translation. Let the flow of definitions sink in. Observe the connectors 'وقال' (and said) as markers for each new definition.
The passage is a series of reported views, not the author's own argument.
2
Translate and identify each definition
Using the literal translation, identify all six definitions. List them in order: (1) Aristotle & followers, (2) Abu al-Barakat, (3) another group, (4) others, (5) another group, (6) ancient philosophers.
The fifth definition uses an analogy of the point and the 'now'.
3
Analyze the analogy in the fifth definition
The author compares the 'now' (instant) to a point: as a point moving generates a line, the 'now' moving generates time. Explore how this connects and separates moments.
This is a sophisticated analogy from atomistic physics.
4
Note the incomplete ending
The passage ends mid-sentence after the sixth definition. Consider what might follow: likely a refutation or critique of these views.
This suggests the author is not merely reporting but using these definitions as a basis for his own stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في البحث عن ساحية الزمان الذي ذهب إليه أرسطاطاليس وارتضاه المعتبرون من أتباعه كأبي نصر الفارابي وأبي علي بن سينا أنه مقدار حركة الفلك الأعظم. وقال الشيخ أبو البركات البغدادي صاحب المعتبر إنه مقدار امتداد الوجود. وقال قوم آخرون إنه عبارة عن نفس حركة الفلك الأعظم. وقال آخرون إنه لا معنى للزمان إلا مجرد التوقيت على ما فسرناه وكشفنا عن معناه. وقال آخرون كما أن النقطة تفعل بحركتها الخط فكذلك الآن يفعل بحركته الزمان. وكما أن النقطة إذا فعلت بحركتها الخط كانت واصلة لأحد قسمي ذلك الخط بالقسم الثاني وإذا وقفت تلك النقطة كانت فاصلة لذلك الخط وقاطعة له فكذلك الآن إذا فعل بحركته الزمان كان آناً واصلاً وإذا فرض حدوثه في الزمان المتصل كان آناً فاصلاً. وقالت طائفة عظيمة من قدماء الحكماء الزمان جوهر أزلي واجب الوجود لذاته ولا تعلق
The fourth chapter: on investigating the reality of time, which Aristotle held and the esteemed ones among his followers approved, such as Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Abu Ali ibn Sina, that it is the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere. And the Shaykh Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi, author of al-Mu'tabar, said that it is the measure of the extent of existence. And another group said that it is nothing but the motion itself of the outermost sphere. And others said that there is no meaning for time except mere temporal determination as we explained and disclosed its meaning. And others said: just as the point by its motion generates the line, so likewise the 'now' by its motion generates time. And just as the point, when it moves, produces the line, is connecting one part of that line to the second part, and when that point stops, it is separating and cutting that line, so likewise the 'now', when it moves, produces time, is a connecting moment, and when it is assumed to occur in continuous time, it is a separating moment. And a great group of ancient philosophers said: time is an eternal substance, necessary of existence by itself, and it has no connection...
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a doxographical survey of definitions of time. The author presents six distinct philosophical views in sequence. First, the view of Aristotle and his followers (such as al-Farabi and Ibn Sina): time is the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere, i.e., the celestial sphere. Second, Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi defines time as the extent of existence, a broader metaphysical notion. Third, another group identifies time directly with the motion itself of the outermost sphere. Fourth, a group reduces time to mere temporal determination, i.e., a relational concept of before and after, which the author says he has already explained elsewhere. Fifth, an analogy: just as a point moving produces a line, the 'now' (instant) by its motion produces time; the 'now' functions both as a connecting link (like a moving point joining two parts of a line) and as a separator (when it stops, it cuts the line); similarly, the 'now' in its motion connects moments, and when it is considered as occurring in continuous time, it separates. Sixth, the ancient philosophers held time to be an eternal substance, necessary of existence in itself. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing with a refutation or further discussion. Each definition reflects a distinct metaphysical commitment: time as measure, as motion, as relation, as product of the instant, or as independent substance.

Key Terms
زمان
حركة
فلك أعظم
آن
نقطة
جوهر
أزلي
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is a single, self-evident concept with a universal definition.
Correction: This passage shows that philosophers proposed radically different definitions of time, from quantitative measure to eternal substance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which philosopher or school defined time as the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere?
A) Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi
B) Aristotle and his followers
C) The ancient philosophers
D) The group that equated time with motion

Key Takeaway
Time has been defined in multiple ways by Islamic philosophers, each reflecting different metaphysical commitments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers disagree on what time is, but you live each moment as if it's self-evident. Are you living in the illusion that time is neutral, or are you aware that your understanding of time shapes how you see your purpose before God? What would change if you truly believed time is merely the measure of your fleeting movements?
?
If the 'now' can either connect or separate, is your present moment connecting your past deeds to your future accountability, or cutting you off from repentance? What specific action will you take this 'now' to ensure it is a connecting moment?
?
The ancient philosophers considered time an eternal substance necessary in itself — a view that challenges the uniqueness of God's necessity. Have you ever unconsciously treated time as if it has its own power, independent of God, rushing you or holding you? How will you reclaim your submission to the Creator of time instead of being enslaved to time itself?

The Aristotelian Definition of Time
First view: time as measure of celestial motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical Aristotelian definition of time as the measure of the motion of the outermost celestial sphere.

Core Idea
Time is the measure of celestial motion.

This lesson introduces the first and most influential philosophical definition of time, attributed to Aristotle and endorsed by his major Muslim followers. The passage presents this view as a foundation for the subsequent discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the key terms: زمان, حركة, فلك.
The definition is concise: time is a measure of motion.
2
Identify the proponents
Note that this view is held by Aristotle and his followers, specifically al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. This gives it significant authority.
These are major figures in Islamic philosophy.
3
Reflect on the definition
Consider why time is tied to the motion of the outermost sphere. What does 'measure' imply?
This definition connects time to the physical universe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في البحث عن ماهية الزمان الذي ذهب إليه أرسطاطاليس، وارتضاه المعتبرون من أتباعه كأبي نصر الفارابي، وأبي علي بن سينا: أنه مقدار حركة الفلك الأعظم. وقال الشيخ أبو البركات البغدادي صاحب المعتبر: إنه مقدار امتداد الوجود. وقال قوم آخرون: إنه عبارة عن نفس حركة الفلك الأعظم. وقال آخرون: إنه لا معنى للزمان إلا مجرد التوقيت على ما فسرناه وكشفنا عن معناه. وقال آخرون: كما أن النقطة تفعل بحركتها الخط فكذلك الآن يفعل بحركته الزمان. وكما أن النقطة إذا فعلت بحركتها الخط كانت واصلة لأحد قسمي ذلك الخط بالقسم الثاني، وإذا وقفت تلك النقطة كانت فاصلة لذلك الخط وقاطعة له، فكذلك الآن، إذا فعل بحركته الزمان، كان آناً واصلاً وإذا فرض حدوثه في الزمان المتصل كان آناً فاصلاً. وقالت طائفة عظيمة من قدماء الحكماء: الزمان جوهر أزلي واجب الوجود لذاته، ولا تعلق له في ذاته، ولا في وجوده، لا بالفلك ولا بالحركة، وإنما الفلك بحركته يقدر أجزاؤه كما أن الفنجانة تقدر بسبب أحوالها المختلفة أجزاء الليل والنهار. ثم قالوا: هذا الجوهر القائم بالنفس، إن حصل فيه شيء من الحركات، ويقدر امتداد دوامه بسبب تلك الحركات سمي زماناً، وأما إن خلى عن مقارنة الحركات ولم يحصل فيه شيء من التغيرات فهو المسمى بالدهر والأزل والسرمد. فهذا تفصيل مذاهب الناس في هذا الباب
The fourth chapter: On the investigation of the nature of time (zaman), which was held by Aristotle, and approved by the esteemed among his followers, like Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Abu Ali ibn Sina: that it is the measure of the motion of the outermost celestial sphere. And Shaykh Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi, the author of al-Mu'tabar, said: it is the measure of the extension of existence. And some others said: it is the very motion of the outermost celestial sphere. And others said: there is no meaning of time except mere timing (tawqit), according to what we have explained and disclosed its meaning. And others said: just as the point (nuqtah) produces the line by its motion, so the 'now' (an) produces time by its motion. And just as the point, when it produces the line by its motion, connects one of the two parts of that line with the second part, and when that point stops, it separates and cuts that line, so the 'now', when it produces time by its motion, is a connecting now, and if it is assumed to occur in continuous time, it is a separating now. And a great group of ancient philosophers said: time is an eternal substance, necessary of existence in itself, having no relation in itself nor in its existence, neither to the sphere nor to motion; rather the sphere by its motion measures its parts, just as a cup (fanjanah) measures the parts of night and day by its varying states. Then they said: this substance subsisting in itself, if some motions occur in it and the extension of its duration is measured by those motions, it is called time (zaman); but if it is devoid of association with motions and no change occurs in it, it is called dahr, azal, and sarmad. This is a detailed account of the doctrines of people on this subject.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the different opinions on the nature of time. The author begins by listing the view of Aristotle and his followers, including al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, that time is the measure of the motion of the outermost celestial sphere. Then Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi says it is the measure of the extension of existence. Some say it is the motion itself. Others say time is mere timing, as previously explained. Another group draws an analogy: just as a point generates a line by its motion, so the 'now' generates time by its motion, and the 'now' can be either a connector (connecting two parts of time) or a divider (when it is taken as an instant in continuous time). Then a great group of ancient philosophers say time is an eternal substance, necessary in itself, having no relation to the sphere or motion; rather, the sphere by its motion measures its parts, analogous to a cup that measures parts of night and day by its changing states. They further distinguish between time (zaman) when associated with motion, and three terms for eternity (dahr, azal, sarmad) when free from motion. The author then concludes that this is a detailed exposition of the different doctrines on this topic.

Key Terms
زمان
حركة
فلك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Aristotelian definition of time?
A) The measure of the motion of the outermost celestial sphere.
B) The extension of existence.
C) The motion itself.
D) An eternal substance.

Key Takeaway
Time, in the Aristotelian tradition, is fundamentally the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is merely the measure of celestial motion, then your entire life's timeline is anchored to the turning of the heavens. Does this make you feel like a small part of a grand cosmic order, or does it leave you feeling detached? How do you respond to that feeling?
?
You have likely never thought of your daily schedule as being linked to the movement of the outermost sphere. Starting now, try to see each hour as a marker of that cosmic motion. Would that change how you prioritize your time? If so, how?
?
The Aristotelian view ties time to a physical reality outside yourself. But you often feel time as personal and internal. Which of these two—external cosmic time or internal subjective time—actually governs your actions? Be honest: which one do you truly live by?

Three Alternative Definitions of Time
Three alternative views on the nature of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be familiar with three alternative definitions of time: as the extension of existence, as motion itself, and as mere relativistic numbering.

Core Idea
Time is not just one thing; philosophers disagree on its essence.

After the Aristotelian definition, the author presents three distinct alternatives. Each proposes a different fundamental nature of time, ranging from a metaphysical extension to a pure conventional measure.

Guided Path
1
Identify each view
Read the passage and pick out the three views: 1) Abu al-Barakat's extension of existence, 2) time as motion itself, 3) time as mere timing.
The third view is a reference to a previous explanation.
2
Compare the definitions
Notice how each view emphasizes a different aspect: metaphysical, physical, or conventional.
This shows the diversity of opinions.
3
Reflect on the third view
The author says 'as we have explained'—this indicates the view is not new but a recapitulation.
Understanding context helps.

The Point and Now Analogy
Analogy: point to line as now to time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogy that likens time to a line generated by the motion of the 'now', similar to how a line is generated by a moving point.

Core Idea
The 'now' generates time the way a point generates a line.

Some thinkers attempted to illustrate the relationship between the present instant and time itself using a geometric analogy. This lesson unpacks that comparison.

Guided Path
1
Understand the point-line analogy
A point moving creates a line. The point connects or divides the line depending on whether it moves or stops.
This is a geometric concept.
2
Apply it to the now and time
Similarly, the 'now' (instant) moving produces time. A moving now connects continuous time; a static now divides it.
The now is analogous to the point.
3
Distinguish connector vs separator
When the now moves, it connects past and future; when it is considered as a discrete instant, it separates time into past and future.
This dual role is crucial.

Time as an Eternal Substance
View: time as eternal substance, with distinction between time and eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the view that time is an eternal self-existing substance, and how it relates to motion and the concepts of dahr, azal, and sarmad.

Core Idea
Time is an eternal substance, not dependent on motion.

The final and most radical view presented is that time is a necessary being, independent of the celestial sphere. This view also distinguishes between time and timeless eternity.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the claim
Time is an eternal substance, necessary in itself, unrelated to sphere or motion. The sphere merely measures time's parts.
This is a Platonist view.
2
Understand the measuring cup analogy
Just as a cup measures night and day, the sphere measures time's parts. Time itself exists independently.
The cup does not create the night; it measures it.
3
Distinguish zaman from dahr/azal/sarmad
When associated with motion, this substance is called time (zaman). Without motion, it is called dahr, azal, or sarmad (eternity).
These terms denote different aspects of timelessness.

الفصل الرابع في البحث عن ماهية الزمان الذي ذهب إليه أرسطاطاليس، وارتضاه المعتبرون من أتباعه كأبي نصر الفارابي، وأبي علي بن سينا: أنه مقدار حركة الفلك الأعظم. وقال الشيخ أبو البركات البغدادي صاحب المعتبر: إنه مقدار امتداد الوجود. وقال قوم آخرون: إنه عبارة عن نفس حركة الفلك الأعظم. وقال آخرون: إنه لا معنى للزمان إلا مجرد التوقيت على ما فسرناه وكشفنا عن معناه. وقال آخرون: كما أن النقطة تفعل بحركتها الخط فكذلك الآن يفعل بحركته الزمان. وكما أن النقطة إذا فعلت بحركتها الخط كانت واصلة لأحد قسمي ذلك الخط بالقسم الثاني، وإذا وقفت تلك النقطة كانت فاصلة لذلك الخط وقاطعة له، فكذلك الآن، إذا فعل بحركته الزمان، كان آناً واصلاً وإذا فرض حدوثه في الزمان المتصل كان آناً فاصلاً. وقالت طائفة عظيمة من قدماء الحكماء: الزمان جوهر أزلي واجب الوجود لذاته، ولا تعلق له في ذاته، ولا في وجوده، لا بالفلك ولا بالحركة، وإنما الفلك بحركته يقدر أجزاؤه كما أن الفنجانة تقدر بسبب أحوالها المختلفة أجزاء الليل والنهار. ثم قالوا: هذا الجوهر القائم بالنفس، إن حصل فيه شيء من الحركات، ويقدر امتداد دوامه بسبب تلك الحركات سمي زماناً، وأما إن خلى عن مقارنة الحركات ولم يحصل فيه شيء من التغيرات فهو المسمى بالدهر والأزل والسرمد. فهذا تفصيل مذاهب الناس في هذا الباب
The fourth chapter: On the investigation of the nature of time (zaman), which was held by Aristotle, and approved by the esteemed among his followers, like Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Abu Ali ibn Sina: that it is the measure of the motion of the outermost celestial sphere. And Shaykh Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi, the author of al-Mu'tabar, said: it is the measure of the extension of existence. And some others said: it is the very motion of the outermost celestial sphere. And others said: there is no meaning of time except mere timing (tawqit), according to what we have explained and disclosed its meaning. And others said: just as the point (nuqtah) produces the line by its motion, so the 'now' (an) produces time by its motion. And just as the point, when it produces the line by its motion, connects one of the two parts of that line with the second part, and when that point stops, it separates and cuts that line, so the 'now', when it produces time by its motion, is a connecting now, and if it is assumed to occur in continuous time, it is a separating now. And a great group of ancient philosophers said: time is an eternal substance, necessary of existence in itself, having no relation in itself nor in its existence, neither to the sphere nor to motion; rather the sphere by its motion measures its parts, just as a cup (fanjanah) measures the parts of night and day by its varying states. Then they said: this substance subsisting in itself, if some motions occur in it and the extension of its duration is measured by those motions, it is called time (zaman); but if it is devoid of association with motions and no change occurs in it, it is called dahr, azal, and sarmad. This is a detailed account of the doctrines of people on this subject.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the different opinions on the nature of time. The author begins by listing the view of Aristotle and his followers, including al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, that time is the measure of the motion of the outermost celestial sphere. Then Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi says it is the measure of the extension of existence. Some say it is the motion itself. Others say time is mere timing, as previously explained. Another group draws an analogy: just as a point generates a line by its motion, so the 'now' generates time by its motion, and the 'now' can be either a connector (connecting two parts of time) or a divider (when it is taken as an instant in continuous time). Then a great group of ancient philosophers say time is an eternal substance, necessary in itself, having no relation to the sphere or motion; rather, the sphere by its motion measures its parts, analogous to a cup that measures parts of night and day by its changing states. They further distinguish between time (zaman) when associated with motion, and three terms for eternity (dahr, azal, sarmad) when free from motion. The author then concludes that this is a detailed exposition of the different doctrines on this topic.

Key Takeaway
Time is an eternal substance that, when associated with motion, becomes zaman; otherwise it is called dahr, azal, or sarmad.

?

Two Arguments Against Distinct Times for Motions
The author refutes the first possibility with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the idea that each motion has its own time is false, through two rational arguments that show its absurd consequences.

Core Idea
The unity of time is known by necessity; splitting it leads to contradiction.

In the previous lesson, the possibility was raised that each motion could have its own separate time. Here, the author refutes this possibility with two powerful arguments. The first argument relies on our intuitive knowledge that the present hour is a single unit, while the second argument shows that multiple simultaneous times would require an impossible encompassing time. Let's examine these arguments in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the author begins by stating the first division is invalid from two aspects.
2
Examine the first argument
Focus on the first argument: if each motion had its own time, the same hour would be many hours. Why does the author say this is known necessarily? Think about your own experience of time.
3
Examine the second argument
The second argument involves a logical analysis: times existing together must be encompassed by another time, leading to either infinite regress or a contradiction. Trace the reasoning step by step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسم الأول باطل من وجهين أحدها إن على هذا التقدير يلزم أن لا تكون هذه الساعة الواحدة ساعة واحدة بل تكون ساعات كثيرة بعد الحركات الموجودة في العالم الأعلى والعالم الأسفل ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل فإنا نعلم بالضرورة أن هذه الساعة ليست إلا ساعة واحدة والثاني إن تلك الأزمنة لما وجدت معاً فمعيتها لا بد وأن تكون لزمان آخر محيط بها وذلك محال لأنه يؤدي إلى التسلسل وأيضاً فبتقدير تسليم جواز التسلسل فالمحال لازم لأن الأمر الذي لأجله حصل اجتماع كل الأزمنة لا بد وأن يكون محيطاً بجميعها والمحيط بجميع الأزمنة وجب أن لا يكون زماناً لأنا قد حصرنا الأزمنة بأسرها في ذلك المجموع فالمحيط بها الخارج عنها لا بد وأن يكون زماناً لكن قد ثبت أن الشيء الذي يقتضي المعية والقبلية والبعدية هو الزمان فذلك الخارج يجب أن يكون زماناً وأن لا يكون زماناً وهذا خلف محال
And the first division is invalid from two aspects: First, that on this estimation it would follow that this one hour is not one hour, but rather many hours corresponding to the motions existing in the higher world and the lower world, and it is known that that is false, for we know necessarily that this hour is only one hour. Second, that when those times exist together, their togetherness must be due to another time encompassing them, and that is impossible because it leads to infinite regress. Also, if one grants the possibility of infinite regress, the absurdity is necessary, because that for which the gathering of all times occurred must encompass all of them, and that which encompasses all times must not be time, for we have encompassed all times in that aggregate, so that which encompasses them and is external to them must be time, but it has been established that the thing that entails togetherness, beforeness, and afterness is time, so that external thing must be time and must not be time, and this is an impossible contradiction.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage refutes the first possibility from the previous lesson: that each motion has its own distinct time. The author gives two arguments. First, if each motion had its own time, then the current hour would be multiple (one for each motion), which is absurd because we know intuitively that there is only one hour. Second, if multiple times exist simultaneously, they must be encompassed by a greater time, leading to two problems: either an infinite regress of encompassing times, or a contradiction where the encompassing time must both be time (to provide togetherness) and not be time (since it's outside the aggregate). Both are impossible.

Correction: This leads to the absurdity of the same hour being many hours and to logical contradictions.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The unity and continuity of time is rationally necessary; attempts to assign distinct times to each motion lead to absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Impossibility of a Single Time for All Motions
Author refutes second possibility with two arguments, first complete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against a single time for all motions: the impossibility of a single accident inhering in multiple loci.

Core Idea
A single accident cannot be in two places at once, not even time.

After refuting the first possibility, the author turns to the second: that time might be a single entity shared by all motions. He declares this impossible and presents two arguments. The first is a fundamental kalam principle: a single accident cannot inhere in multiple subjects. The following passage states this argument and begins a second, though the text is incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get the gist. Note that the author is refuting the second possibility with two arguments.
The second argument is cut off; focus on the first.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage lists two proofs: 'الأول' and 'الثاني'. The first states a principle; the second gives a conditional reasoning that leads to a contradiction.
3
Understand the first argument
The first argument is that a single accident (time) cannot inhere in many loci (motions). This is because an accident's existence requires a specific subject; it cannot be simultaneously in multiple subjects. This principle is key in kalam.
This argument alone suffices to refute the second possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يحصل زمان واحد قائم بجميع الحركات فهذا أيضاً محال ويدل عليه وجوه الأول إن حلول العرض الواحد في المحال الكثيرة محال والثاني إنه إذا عدمت حركة فقد عدم مقدارها والحركة الثانية التي هي باقية موجودة يكون مقدارها أيضاً موجوداً فلو كان مقدار جميع الحركات
As for the second division, that a single time subsisting in all motions obtains, this is also impossible. And evidence for it is given by aspects: The first: That a single accident's inherence in many loci is impossible. The second: That if a motion ceases, its measure has ceased, and the second motion which is remaining exists, its measure also exists, so if the measure of all motions...
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage begins the refutation of the second possibility (one time for all motions). It presents two arguments, though the second is incomplete due to missing text. The first argument states that a single accident (time) cannot inhere in multiple distinct loci (motions). The second argues that if a motion ceases, its measure (time) ceases, yet other motions continue; the shared measure would have to both cease and persist, a contradiction. The argument is incomplete.

Correction: The principle of inherence prevents a single accident from existing in multiple subjects. Time, as an accident, cannot be in more than one motion at once.

The Argument for Time as a Continuous Quantity and Its Refutation
Presents the argument for time as continuous quantity and its refutation by reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument presented for time being a continuous quantity and how the author begins to refute it by showing its absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Time's continuity is argued via quantity classification, but leads to atomism.

In this passage, the author presents the reasoning of those who claim time is a continuous quantity. They argue that since time admits equality and inequality, it must be a quantity, and every quantity is either continuous or discrete. They then attempt to show that time cannot be discrete, because that would force motion and body to be composed of indivisible parts—a position they aim to reject. However, the passage is cut off mid-argument, leaving the full refutation incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This is a refutation chapter. The author first presents the opponents' argument before dismantling it.
The text is from a kalam work on time.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the logical flow: time is a quantity, quantity is continuous or discrete, not discrete, so continuous.
Watch for the conditional 'if... then' structure.
3
Identify the reductio ad absurdum
The argument shows that if time were discrete (composed of indivisible instants), then motion would be composed of indivisible parts, leading to atomism. This is meant to be an absurd consequence.
The argument is cut off, but we see the direction.
4
Answer the checkpoint
Test your understanding by answering the question about what follows if time were composed of successive instants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس
التخصص عما قيل من أن الزمان كم متصل. وبيان أن ذلك ليس بحق
احتج القائلون بأن الزمان كم متصل بأن قالوا : قد ذكرنا أن الزمان قابل للمساواة والمفاوتة، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو كم ، فالزمان كم ، وكل كم فهو إما متصل أو منفصل ، لا جائز أن يكون الزمان كماً منفصلاً ، لأن كل كم منفصل فهو مركب من الوحدات ، فلو كان الزمان كماً منفصلاً ، لكان مركباً من آنات متلاصقة ، ودفعات متعاقبة ، ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لزم كون الحركة مركبة من أمور متتالية ، كل واحد منها لا يقبل القسمة ، لأن الواقع من الحركة في الآن الواحد ، إن كان منقسماً كان وقوع النصف الأول من تلك الحركة ، متقدماً على وقوع النصف الثاني منها ، فحينئذ ينتصف ذلك الآن ، لكنا فرضنا أن ذلك الآن غير منقسم هذا خلف . فثبت أن القدر الحاصل من الحركة في الآن الواحد غير منقسم فلو كان الزمان مركباً من الآنات المتتالية ، لكانت الحركة مركبة من أمور غير منقسمة ، ولو كان الأمر كذلك لكان الجسم مركباً من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ ، لأن المقدار من المسافة الذي يتحرك عليه في الآن ، الذي لا ينقسم، بالجزء الذي لا يتجزأ من الحركة ، إن
Chapter Five: Refutation of what was said that time is a continuous quantity, and showing that this is not correct. Those who say that time is a continuous quantity argued: We have mentioned that time admits of equality and inequality, and everything that admits this is a quantity, so time is a quantity, and every quantity is either continuous or discrete. It is impossible for time to be a discrete quantity, because every discrete quantity is composed of units. If time were a discrete quantity, it would be composed of contiguous instants and successive moments. If that were the case, motion would necessarily be composed of successive things, each of which is indivisible. For what occurs of motion in a single instant, if divisible, then the occurrence of the first half of that motion would precede the occurrence of the second half, so that instant would be divided, but we assumed that instant is indivisible—this is a contradiction. So it is established that the amount of motion occurring in a single instant is indivisible. If time were composed of successive instants, then motion would be composed of indivisible things. If that were the case, then body would be composed of parts that are indivisible, because the distance traversed in the indivisible instant is (traversed) by the indivisible part of motion...
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a proof and refutation concerning the nature of time. The author presents the argument of those who claim that time is a continuous quantity. The argument proceeds as follows: Time admits of equality and inequality, so it is a quantity. Every quantity is either continuous or discrete. It cannot be discrete, because a discrete quantity is composed of units. If time were discrete, it would be composed of consecutive instants and successive moments. This would lead to motion being composed of successive indivisible parts. The reasoning is that motion occurring in a single instant must be indivisible, otherwise the instant would be divisible, contradicting the assumption that it is an indivisible instant. Therefore, if time were composed of such instants, motion would be made of indivisible parts. Consequently, body would be composed of indivisible parts (atoms), because the distance moved in an indivisible instant would have to be by an indivisible part of motion. The argument is cut off here, but the intent is to show that this consequence is absurd, thus refuting the claim that time is continuous. The full refutation would continue on the next page.

Key Terms
الزمان
الكم
المتصل
المنفصل
الآن
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this passage is proving atomism or that atomism is the conclusion.
Correction: Actually, the argument is a reductio ad absurdum: it shows that if time were discrete, then atomism would follow, but atomism is considered false, so time cannot be discrete. The passage is part of a refutation of the continuity claim, not an endorsement of atomism.

Checkpoint
According to the argument presented, if time were composed of successive instants, what would necessarily follow?
A) Motion would be continuous and infinitely divisible.
B) Motion would be composed of indivisible parts.
C) Time would be a continuous quantity.
D) The body would be infinitely divisible.

Key Takeaway
The argument attempts to prove time is continuous by showing that assuming it is discrete leads to the indivisible parts (atomism), which is considered absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read a deep philosophical debate about time. But the fact remains: your time is limited and every instant is a trust from Allah. How many of your 'instants' today were spent in heedlessness? Will you account for each one?
?
The argument suggests that if time were discrete, each instant would be a unique, unrepeatable moment. Regardless of its nature, you only get one chance per instant. What is your most common excuse for wasting time? Write it down, and then commit to abandoning it tomorrow.
?
These scholars debated time's essence while most people never give it a second thought. But you now have a greater awareness. What specific habit will you change starting today to ensure your remaining moments are used in obedience to Allah, not in vain? Be concrete.

Refuting the View that Time is the Measure of Existence
The author refutes three views on time: celestial motion, its measure, and measure of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of Abu al-Barakat's view that time is the measure of existence, and why it leads to an absurd multiplicity of times.

Core Idea
Time cannot be the measure of existence because it would multiply times absurdly.

The author continues his investigation into the nature of time by examining three remaining opinions. After dismissing the first two views in his earlier works, he focuses on Abu al-Barakat's claim that time is the measure of existence. He interprets this claim and then demonstrates its logical impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
Read the passage and note the three opinions on time: celestial motion, its measure, and the measure of existence.
The first two are only mentioned in passing.
2
Observe the author’s treatment of the first two
Notice that the author claims the first two views have been refuted elsewhere and does not repeat the arguments here.
3
Analyze the third opinion
Focus on Abu al-Barakat's view and the author's interpretation of it as 'the measure of the extent of existence'.
The author finds the original phrasing ambiguous.
4
Understand the refutation
Follow the logical steps: the extent of existence is persistence, each thing has its own persistence, so if time were this measure, there would be many times in one hour—which is impossible.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Conclude that the view is false because it leads to an impossible consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس في تتبع سائر المذاهب والأقوال في ماهية الزمان

أما مذهب من يقول : الزمان عبارة عن الحركة الفلكية . فقد بالغنا في تقرير الوجوه الدالة على فساده. وأما مذهب من يقول : إنه عبارة عن مقدار الحركة الفلكية فقد كشفنا عن فساده كشفاً لا يبقى للعاقل فيه شبهة . وأما قول أبي البركات إن الزمان عبارة عن مقدار الوجود . فهذا كلام مبهم مجمل وأحسن أقواله أن يقال : المراد منه : أن الزمان مقدار امتداد الوجود . فإن كان المراد ذلك فهو باطل . وذلك لأن امتداد الوجود عبارة عن بقاء الشيء ودوامه واستمراره، وهذا البقاء والدوام إما أن لا يكون أمراً زائداً على ذات الباقي . أو إن كان زائداً عليه، لكن بقاء كل شيء ودوامه صفة قائمة به. وأيضاً : فبقاء كل شيء ودوامه غير بقاء الآخر ، وغير دوامه . فلو كان الزمان عبارة عن هذا المعنى لزم أن يكون عدد الأزمنة المجتمعة في هذه الساعة الواحدة ، بحسب عدد الأشياء الباقية في هذه الساعة . لكنا قد دللنا على أن القول باجتماع الأزمنة الكثيرة دفعة واحدة : محال .
Chapter Six: On Following Up the Rest of the Schools and Sayings Concerning the Essence of Time. As for the school of whoever says: time is an expression for celestial motion—we have exaggerated in presenting the aspects indicating its corruption. And as for the school of whoever says: it is an expression for the measure of celestial motion—we have uncovered its corruption with a disclosure that leaves no doubt for the rational person. And as for the saying of Abu al-Barakat that time is an expression for the measure of existence—this is ambiguous and vague speech. The best of its interpretations is that it be said: what is meant by it is that time is the measure of the extent of existence. If that is meant, then it is false. That is because the extent of existence is an expression for the persistence of a thing and its continuance and its duration. This persistence and continuance either is not an additional matter over the enduring essence, or if it is additional, yet the persistence and continuance of each thing is a quality subsisting in it. Also, the persistence and continuance of each thing is other than the persistence and continuance of another. So if time were an expression for this meaning, it would necessarily follow that the number of times gathered in this single hour would be according to the number of enduring things in this hour. But we have demonstrated that the saying about the gathering of many times simultaneously is impossible.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation of three opinions on the essence (mahiyya) of time. The author, in chapter six, examines the remaining views. First, the opinion that time is celestial motion: he claims to have already sufficiently shown its corruption. Second, the opinion that time is the measure (miqdar) of celestial motion: he says he has exposed its corruption beyond any doubt for the rational person. Third, the opinion of Abu al-Barakat that time is the measure of existence: the author finds this ambiguous. The best interpretation is that time is the measure of the extent (imtidad) of existence. He then refutes this interpretation. The extent of existence is persistence (baqa'), continuance (dawam), and duration (istimrar). This persistence either is not additional to the enduring thing's essence, or if additional, it is a quality subsisting in the thing. Moreover, each thing's persistence is distinct. If time were this, then in a single hour (the same temporal moment) there would be as many distinct 'times' as there are enduring things, because each thing has its own persistence. But the author states he has already proven that it is impossible for multiple times to coexist simultaneously. Hence Abu al-Barakat's view leads to an absurdity. The author thus concludes that this view is false.

Key Terms
مذهب
زمان
حركة فلكية
مقدار
وجود
امتداد
بقاء
دوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author dismisses Abu al-Barakat's view as vague without proper refutation.
Correction: The author carefully interprets the view in its best light before refuting it logically.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the interpretation that time is the measure of the extent of existence?
A) Because time would then depend on the existence of things, which is circular.
B) Because it would entail multiple distinct times coexisting in the same moment, which is impossible.
C) Because existence is not measurable.
D) Because the extent of existence is not real.

Key Takeaway
The view that time is the measure of existence entails an absurd multiplicity of times, thus it is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If each thing's duration is unique and cannot be measured by a single time, how does that challenge your tendency to compare the length or significance of your life to others'? Will you stop measuring yourself against others and instead recognize the uniqueness of your own existence before Allah?
?
The passage argues that multiple times cannot coexist; every moment you experience is singular. How does this truth affect your awareness in salah? Will you commit to being fully present in each moment of worship, rather than allowing your mind to wander to past regrets or future worries?
?
Since time is not a universal measure, your persistence is a direct gift from Allah, not a common commodity. Are you using the unique duration of your life to draw closer to Him, or are you wasting it on distractions? What concrete change will you make today to honor the time Allah has given you?

Defending Time as Substance: Al-Razi's Refutation
Al-Razi refutes later philosophers' argument against time as a substance and endorses Plato's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi defends the ancient view of time as a self-subsistent substance by refuting the later philosophers' objection using an analogy to God's immutable essence.

Core Idea
Time's substance can be eternal; only its relations change.

This passage from Al-Razi's Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a defense of Plato's view of time as a substance. Al-Razi first states the later philosophers' objection that time cannot be a substance because it is flowing, then provides a response arguing that the flow is merely relational, not essential, drawing an analogy to God's unchanging essence. The passage concludes with an endorsement of Plato over Aristotle.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the flow from objection to response.
2
Identify the objection
Locate the later philosophers' claim and their reasoning. They argue that because time is flowing and constantly renewing, it cannot be a self-subsistent substance.
3
Examine the response
See how Al-Razi (or the responder) denies that time is essentially flowing. He proposes instead that time may be a permanent substance whose changes are merely relational, occurring in its relations to events, not in its essence.
4
Analyze the analogy to God
The responder draws an analogy to God's essence: God's relations to things change (before, with, after) without any change in His essence. Similarly, time's substance can remain unchanged while its relations change.
This is the key argument that undermines the objection.
5
Understand the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that the later philosophers' argument is weak and invalid, and he endorses the ancient view (Plato's) as the most correct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قول قدماء الحكماء، وهو أنه جوهر قائم بنفسه، مستقل بذاته، فالمتأخرون أبطلوا ذلك بأن قالوا: الزمان شيء سيال متجدد الوجود. وما يكون كذلك، فإنه يمتنع أن يكون جوهراً قائماً بذاته، مستقلاً بنفسه. هذا غاية الإلزام في إبطال هذا المذهب. ولمجيب أن يجيب عنه فيقول: لا نسلم أنه في ذاته وماهيته سيال متبدل منقض. ولم لا يجوز [ أن يقال : ] إنه جوهر باقي أزلي أبدي إلا أنه إذا حدثت الحوادث، صارت تلك الحوادث المتعاقبة مقارنة له، وحينئذ يلزم من وقوع التغير والتبدل، وقوع التغير والتبدل في نسب ذلك الجوهر إلى تلك الحوادث. والحاصل: أن السيلان والتبدل ما وقعا في ذات الزمان، وفي جوهره. بل وقعا في نسبته إلى الحوادث المتعاقبة. ومما يدل عليه: أن ذات واجب الوجود لذاته، تكون بالنسبة إلى كل شيء، إما قبله أو معه أو بعده. ثم إنه بحسب تغير المتغيرات، تتغير تلك النسب المسماه بالقبلية والمعية والبعدية في ذات واجب الوجود، ولم يلزم من تبدل تلك النسب والإضافات وقوع التبدل والتغير في ذاته وفي صفاته الحقيقية فإنه واجب الوجود لذاته، و واجب الوجود من جميع جهاته. فإذا عقل هذا المعنى في حق واجب الوجود، فلم لا يعقل مثله في ذات الزمان وجوهره؟ فظهر أن هذه الحجة التي ذكرها المتأخرون في إبطال مذهب القدماء في ماهية الزمان: حجة ضعيفة ساقطة. بل عندي: أن هذا القول أقرب الأقوال المذكورة في ماهية الزمان وفي حقيقته. وهو مذهب الإمام أفلاطون. وظهر بهذه المباحث الغامضة التي أوردناها، والبينات الكاملة التامة التي قررناها: أن الحق في حقيقة المكان والزمان ما قاله أفلاطون الإنمي لا ما اختاره أرسطاطاليس المنطقي.
As for the saying of the ancient philosophers, which is that it (time) is a self-subsistent substance, independent in its essence, the later philosophers invalidated that by saying: time is a flowing thing, continually renewing in existence. And what is like that, then it is impossible for it to be a self-subsistent substance, independent in its essence. This is the ultimate obligation in invalidating this doctrine. And a responder may respond about it and say: We do not accept that in its essence and quiddity it is flowing, changing, passing away. And why is it not permissible [that it be said:] that it is a permanent, eternal, everlasting substance, except that when events occur, those successive events become concomitant to it, and then from the occurrence of change and alteration, there follows the occurrence of change and alteration in the relations of that substance to those events. The upshot is: flowing and alteration did not occur in the essence of time, nor in its substance. Rather, they occurred in its relation to successive events. And what indicates that is: the essence of the Necessary Existence (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi) is, in relation to everything, either before it or with it or after it. Then, according to the change of changing things, those relations called 'beforeness', 'withness', and 'afterness' change in the essence of the Necessary Existence, and from the change of those relations and attributions there does not follow the occurrence of change and alteration in His essence or in His real attributes, for He is necessary in existence by His essence, and necessary in existence from all His aspects. So if this meaning is understood in the case of the Necessary Existence, then why is not the like of it understood in the essence of time and its substance? So it became clear that this argument which the later philosophers mentioned in invalidating the doctrine of the ancients regarding the quiddity of time is a weak, invalid argument. Rather, in my view: this saying is the closest of the mentioned sayings regarding the quiddity of time and its reality. And it is the doctrine of the master Plato. And it became clear through these profound investigations which we have presented, and the complete perfect evidences which we have established: that the truth regarding the reality of space and time is what Plato the Divine said, not what Aristotle the Logician chose.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the later philosophers' argument against the ancient view that time is a substance, followed by an endorsement of Plato's position. The author first states the later philosophers' objection: they say time is flowing and constantly renewing, so it cannot be a self-subsistent substance. Then he presents a response: one can deny that time is essentially flowing; it may be an eternal substance whose changes are merely relative to events. He draws an analogy with God (wajib al-wujud): God's relations to things (before, with, after) change without any change in His essence. Similarly, time's substance can remain unchanged while its relations to events change. Therefore, the later philosophers' argument is weak. The author then states that in his view, the ancient view is the closest to the truth, and that Plato's doctrine is correct, not Aristotle's.

جوهر قائم بنفسه
سيال
نسبة
إضافة
واجب الوجود لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If time is flowing and changing, it cannot be a substance.
Correction: The flow and change may be in the relations of time to events, not in its essence, just as God's relations change without change in His essence.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to refute the later philosophers' argument that time cannot be a substance because it is flowing?
A) The analogy of a river that is constantly changing.
B) The analogy of God's essence, whose relations change but essence does not.
C) The analogy of a human body that changes over time.
D) The analogy of a number that remains the same.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi defends the ancient view of time as a substance by arguing that its flow is relational, not essential, analogous to God's unchanging nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that time changes everything, but Al-Razi argues that time itself is unchanging—only its relation to events changes. If time's essence is steadfast, then what does that say about your own feeling of being swept away by life's changes? Are you confusing the flow of events with a change in your own core?
?
The analogy with God's unchanging essence is used to defend time's constancy. But if even God's relations to you change (He is 'before' your sin, 'after' your repentance), yet He remains the same, then how should this transform your view of repentance—not as altering God, but as altering your relation to the Eternal? Will you begin to see your moments not as lost, but as repositioned relative to the Unchanging?
?
You live as if each moment is a new self, but the lesson suggests that your core may be as enduring as time itself. If that is true, then what excuse do you have for not turning every relation—every event—toward permanence? What one relationship in your life needs to be reoriented today, not by changing your essence, but by changing its connection to the Eternal?

Two Groups on Time's Necessity and Two Proofs for Its Necessity
Al-Razi classifies two groups and gives two proofs for time's necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's classification of two groups regarding time's ontological status and the two proofs he presents for the necessity of time in itself.

Core Idea
Time's nonexistence leads to contradiction, proving its necessity.

Al-Razi engages with a contentious issue in Islamic philosophy: is time a necessary substance? He first distinguishes two camps among those who affirm time's independence, then provides two compelling arguments that time is necessary in itself. These proofs rely on the principle that a necessary being is one whose nonexistence entails a contradiction, and they apply this principle to time itself.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification
Read the opening where Al-Razi divides those who hold time as a self-subsistent substance into two groups: one deems it possible in itself, the other necessary in itself. Note the key terms used.
This sets up the debate.
2
Examine the first proof
Follow the first proof: defining necessary existence and applying it to time. Notice how the supposition of time's nonexistence leads to its existence, forming a contradiction.
This proof relies on the concept of 'after existence' implying time.
3
Examine the second proof
Read the second proof, which argues that any attempt to deny time uses temporal relations (before, with, after), thereby affirming time's existence. Note that the text ends mid-sentence but the argument is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما تلخص هذا الكلام فنقول: القائلون بأن الزمان جوهر قائم بنفسه مستقل بذاته فريقان: منهم من قال: إنه وإن كان كذلك لكنه ممكن بذاته واجب بغيره، للدلائل الدالة على أن واجب الوجود لذاته ليس إلا الواحد. ومنهم من قال: بل الزمان جوهر واجب الوجود لذاته ممتنع العدم لعينه. وهؤلاء احتجوا على صحة قولهم بوجوه: الحجة الأولى: إنا إذا أردنا أن نعرف أن واجب الوجود لذاته ما هو؟ قلنا: إنه الذي يلزم من مجرد فرض عدمه محال، سواء كان ذلك اللزوم مما يعرف ببديهة العقل، أو بنظر العقل. لكن [ من الظاهر أنه لو كان لزوم المحال عليه معلوماً بالبديهة، كان وجوب الاعتراف ] بكونه واجب الوجود لذاته أولى [ لكن أولى ] الأشياء بهذا المعنى هو الزمان، لأنك لو فرضت له عدماً، لوجب أن يكون عدمه بعد وجوده، وكون عدمه بعد وجوده ليس إلا بالزمان. فههنا لزم من مجرد فرض عدم الزمان، فرض وجوده، لزوماً بديهياً أولياً. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، كان الأمر الذي هو أظهر خواص واجب الوجود لذاته، وأقوى آثاره، وأكمل لوازمه، لم يحصل إلا في الزمان. فكيف يعقل أن يقال: إنه ليس واجب الوجود لذاته؟. الحجة الثانية: إن العقل كلما حاول أن يحكم بارتفاع الزمان وبعدمه، فإنه لا بد مع ذلك [ وأن يحكم ] بوجوده. لأنه إنما يرفعه إما قبل شيء، أو مع شيء، أو بعد شيء. فحصول تلك القبلية والمعية والبعدية شاهد على كذب من نفاه، وعلى فساد دعوى من أبطله. ومناد بإثبات الزمان، وناطق بكونه موجوداً بريئاً عن قبول العدم والزوال. وكل ذلك من خواص واجب
When this discussion is summarized, we say: Those who say that time is a self-subsistent substance independent in its essence are two groups: some of them said that although it is like that, yet it is possible in itself and necessary through another, because of the evidences indicating that the Necessary Existence in itself is only the One. And some of them said: Rather, time is a substance necessary in existence in itself, impossible of nonexistence in itself. And these adduced for the correctness of their saying proofs: The first proof: When we want to know what the Necessary Existence in itself is, we say: It is that from the mere supposition of whose nonexistence an impossibility follows, whether that following is known by the intuition of the intellect or by the reasoning of the intellect. But [ it is apparent that if the following of impossibility upon it were known by intuition, then the necessity of acknowledging ] that it is necessary in existence in itself would be more primary. [ But the most primary ] of things in this meaning is time, because if you suppose nonexistence for it, it must be that its nonexistence is after its existence, and its nonexistence after its existence is only through time. So here, from the mere supposition of the nonexistence of time, the supposition of its existence follows, with an intuitive, primary following. And if the matter is such, then the thing which is the most apparent of the properties of the Necessary Existence in itself, and the strongest of its effects, and the most perfect of its concomitants, obtains only in time. So how can it be said that it is not necessary in existence in itself? The second proof: Whenever the intellect tries to judge that time is removed and nonexistent, it must necessarily [and judge] its existence. Because it removes it only either before something, or with something, or after something. So the occurrence of those 'beforeness', 'withness', and 'afterness' is a witness to the falsehood of the one who denies it, and to the corruption of the claim of the one who invalidates it. And it is a proclaimer of the affirmation of time, and a speaker of its being existent, free from accepting nonexistence and passing away. And all that is among the properties of the Necessary
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage classifies two groups regarding time's ontological status and presents two proofs for the view that time is necessary in itself. The first group holds that time is a possible substance dependent on another for necessity. The second group holds that time is necessary in itself. The author then gives two proofs for the second view. The first proof defines the Necessary Existence as that whose nonexistence would entail an impossibility; applying this to time, if we suppose time's nonexistence, it must be after its existence, which itself presupposes time, so the supposition leads to a contradiction. Thus time has the most apparent property of the Necessary Existence. The second proof argues that any attempt to deny time's existence inevitably uses temporal relations (before, with, after), which affirm time's existence. Hence time is necessary and eternal. The text ends mid-sentence but the argument is clear.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only God can be necessary in existence; time cannot be necessary.
Correction: The arguments show that time has a kind of necessity (its nonexistence is impossible) but this does not equate it to God; it is a distinct necessary property.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Razi shows that denying time leads to contradiction. But have you ever denied time's reality in your own heart by living as if the afterlife is distant? How does this lesson confront your procrastination in preparing for what is truly necessary?
?
The passage claims time possesses a property of the Necessary Existence. Does that make you uncomfortable? Examine your heart: do you subconsciously fear that recognizing time's necessity diminishes God's uniqueness? Are you ready to accept truth even if it challenges your assumptions?

Plato's Substantialist View of Time
Author states Plato's definition of time and argues it is correct over Aristotle's.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Plato's conception of time as a self-subsistent substance that acquires successive relations to events, and why the author considers this superior to Aristotle's relational view.

Core Idea
Time is a real substance, not merely a relation.

In this concise passage, the author presents Plato's definition of time as a self-subsistent substance that then acquires successive relations to events. This stands in direct opposition to Aristotle's view that time is an accident or relation. The author decisively sides with Plato, affirming that this is the correct understanding of time and duration. This lesson unpacks that claim and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: first Plato's view, then the author's judgment.
The passage is brief but packs a decisive theological-philosophical stance.
2
Identify the two components of Plato's view
Plato's definition has two parts: (1) time is a self-subsistent substance, and (2) it acquires successive relations to events. Understand each part separately.
A self-subsistent substance exists independently; relations are secondary.
3
Grasp the author's conclusion
The author states that this view is an admission of the truth about duration and time, and explicitly rejects Aristotle's position. Ask yourself why the author might favor a substantialist definition.
This connects to broader debates about the reality of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذهب أفلاطون من أن الزمان جوهر قائم بذاته ، ثم إنه تحصل له نسب متعاقبة متوالية إلى الحوادث. فيكون هذا اعترافاً بأن الحق في تقرير المدة والزمان : ما ذكره أفلاطون . لا ما ذكره أرسطاطاليس
The doctrine of Plato is that time is a self-subsistent substance, then it acquires successive, consecutive relations to events. So this is an admission that the truth in determining duration and time is what Plato mentioned, not what Aristotle mentioned.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents a concise argument about the nature of time. The author states Plato's view that time (al-zaman) is a self-subsistent substance (jawhar qā'im bi-dhātihi) that subsequently acquires a series of successive relations to events. The author then concludes that this view constitutes an admission that the correct position on the determination of duration (al-midda) and time is Plato's, not Aristotle's. The argument is brief but definitive: it aligns the truth with Plato's substantialist conception of time, implicitly rejecting Aristotle's relational or accidental view.

Avicenna's Threefold Distinction of Time, Perpetuity, and Eternity
Al-Razi recounts Avicenna's definitions of zaman, dahr, and sarmad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Avicenna's definitions of zaman, dahr, and sarmad, and how they differ based on their relation to change.

Core Idea
Time, perpetuity, and eternity are distinguished by what they consider: change, unchanging with change, or unchanging alone.

In this passage, al-Razi reports Avicenna's concise definitions of three temporal concepts: time (zaman), perpetuity (dahr), and eternity (sarmad). Each definition hinges on the perspective taken toward change and unchanging things. The passage sets the stage for further discussion by noting that Avicenna provided no further elaboration.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice that there are three distinct definitions presented.
Pay attention to the phrase 'اعتبار أحوال' (consideration of the states) which appears in each definition.
2
Identify the key distinction
Compare the three definitions. The key difference lies in what is being considered: changes with changing things, unchanging things with changing things, or unchanging things alone.
This distinction is based on the presence or absence of change and the relation between unchanging and changing entities.
3
Reflect on the missing explanation
Note al-Razi's remark that Avicenna gave no further clarification. Think about why these definitions might require more explanation and what questions arise from them.
This reflection prepares you for the subsequent discussion in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل
تحقيق الكلام في الدهر والسرمد. والفرق بينهما وبين الزمان

ذكر الشيخ الرئيس في أكثر كتبه : أن اعتبار أحوال التغيرات مع المتغيرات هو الزمان ، واعتبار أحوال الأشياء الثابتة مع الأشياء المتغيرة هو الدهر ، واعتبار أحوال الأشياء الثابتة هو السرمد . وهذا هو الذي رأيناه في كتبه ، وما وجدنا له مزيد بيان ، وشرح لهذه الأقسام .
Chapter
Establishing the discourse on al-dahr (perpetuity) and al-sarmad (eternity), and the difference between them and al-zaman (time).

The Chief Master (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) mentioned in most of his books: that the consideration of the states of changes together with the changing things is al-zaman; and the consideration of the states of unchanging things together with the changing things is al-dahr; and the consideration of the states of unchanging things is al-sarmad. This is what we have seen in his books, and we have not found for him any further clarification or explanation of these divisions.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage introduces Avicenna's (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) threefold distinction of temporal concepts. The passage is a definition and classification. First, al-zaman (time) is defined as the consideration of the states of changes together with the changing things. Second, al-dahr (perpetuity) is the consideration of the states of unchanging things together with the changing things. Third, al-sarmad (eternity) is the consideration of the states of unchanging things alone. Al-Razi notes that this is all he found in Avicenna's works, and that Avicenna gave no further explanation or commentary on these divisions. The passage sets up the need for the two questions that follow in the subsequent lessons.

Key Terms

الدهر
السرمد
الشيخ الرئيس
التغيرات
المتغيرات
الأشياء الثابتة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that al-dahr and al-sarmad are synonyms for time or each other.
Correction: Avicenna distinguishes them based on whether they consider the state of changing things alone, unchanging things with changing, or unchanging things alone.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is al-dahr (perpetuity) considered to be?
A) The consideration of the states of changes together with the changing things
B) The consideration of the states of unchanging things together with the changing things
C) The consideration of the states of unchanging things alone
D) The same as al-zaman

Key Takeaway
Avicenna defines time, perpetuity, and eternity by what they consider: change, unchanging with change, or unchanging alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you often experience change. How do you, like Avicenna's definition of zaman, consider your own changing states as mere time? Could you instead, through this distinction, practice considering unchanging truths (like Allah's attributes) together with your changing circumstances (dahr), and thereby elevate your experience? Will you commit to intentionally shifting your perspective from pure time to perpetuity when faced with difficulties?
?
The definition of sarmad is the consideration of unchanging things alone, without reference to change. When have you last set aside all thoughts of change and focused solely on the eternal? If your worship is always tied to temporal concerns, are you truly engaging with the Eternal? This week, can you dedicate a portion of your worship to contemplate Allah's attributes as if nothing else exists?
?
Al-Razi notes that Avicenna gave no further explanation of these divisions. Are you satisfied with a theoretical understanding, or does your soul crave a deeper existential grasp? How can you move from intellectual knowledge to lived reality in your own life?

Al-Razi's First Question: Are Dahr and Sarmad Relations or Entities?
Al-Razi presents a dilemma on the ontological status of dahr and sarmad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's objection that Avicenna failed to clarify whether dahr and sarmad are merely relations or separate existents, and why this distinction is crucial for the metaphysics of time and eternity.

Core Idea
Dahr and sarmad cannot be meaningful without ontological classification.

Al-Razi challenges Avicenna by posing a crucial dilemma: are dahr and sarmad merely the specific relations of priority, posteriority, and togetherness, or are they separate entities that cause these relations? Without a clear answer, any talk of eternity remains vague. This passage highlights the need to classify such concepts into substances or accidents, corporeal or spiritual, to make them intelligible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the full English translation. Let Al-Razi's probing tone sink in before you interpret.
Notice the parallel structure: 'If it is the first... If it is the second...'
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Al-Razi presents two possibilities: either dahr and sarmad are themselves the specific relations, or they are something else that causes these relations. Find the exact Arabic phrasing for each.
The key phrase is 'هل هو نفس هذه النسب المخصوصة أو هو أمر آخر يقتضي حصول هذه النسب'
3
Understand the demand for classification
Al-Razi insists that if dahr and sarmad are separate existents, they must be categorized as substance or accident, and further specified. Trace his sub-questions: corporeal or spiritual? Which genus of accidents?
Note the implied criticism: Avicenna's terms are unclear without this analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب البحث ههنا عن سؤالين :

السؤال الأول : أن الشيخ لم يبين أن هذا الذي سماه بـالدهر والسرمد، هل هو نفس هذه النسب المخصوصة أو هو أمر آخر يقتضي حصول هذه النسب ؟ فإن كان الأول ، فلم لا نقول في الزمان بمثل هذا القول ؟ وهو أنه لا معنى للزمان إلا نفس هذه القبليات والبعديات والمعيات من غير إثبات أمر آخر ؟ ولم زعم : أن الزمان موجود يقتضي حصول هذه النسب ، ولم يقل : الدهر موجود يقتضي حصول هذه النسبة المخصوصة ؟ وما الفرق بين البابين ؟ وإن كان الثاني وهو أن يقال : إن المسمى بالدهر والسرمد موجود مخصوص ، يقتضي حصول هذه النسب . فكان من الواجب عليه أن يبين أن ذلك الشيء جوهر أو عرض ؟ وإن كان جوهراً ، فهو من الجواهر الجسمانية أو من الجواهر الروحانية المجردة ؟ وإن كان عرضاً فهو من أي أجناس الأعراض ؟ فإن هذه الكلمات لا تصير معلومة مفهومة ، إلا بهذا الطريق .
And it is necessary to investigate here concerning two questions:

The first question: that the Shaykh did not clarify whether that which he called al-dahr and al-sarmad is itself these specific relations or is another thing that necessitates the occurrence of these relations? If it is the first, then why do we not say concerning al-zaman something similar? That is, that there is no meaning to al-zaman except these very priornesses, posteriornesses, and togethernesses without positing another thing? And why did he claim that al-zaman is an existent that necessitates the occurrence of these relations, and did not say that al-dahr is an existent that necessitates the occurrence of this specific relation? And what is the difference between the two cases? And if it is the second, that it is said: that that which is called al-dahr and al-sarmad is a specific existent that necessitates the occurrence of these relations. Then it would have been obligatory upon him to clarify whether that thing is a jawhar (substance) or an 'arad (accident)? If it is a jawhar, is it from the corporeal substances or from the spiritual abstract substances? And if it is an 'arad, then from which genera of accidents is it? For these words do not become known and understood except by this way.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two questions that Al-Razi considers necessary to investigate regarding Avicenna's definitions. The passage is an objection-and-argument structure. Al-Razi argues that Avicenna did not clarify whether dahr and sarmad are merely the specific relations themselves or whether they are separate entities that cause these relations. If they are just the relations, then why not apply the same reductionist view to zaman? That is, why claim zaman is a separate existent that causes relations when one could simply say time is nothing but priornesses, posteriornesses, and togethernesses? If they are separate existents, then Avicenna should have specified whether they are substances or accidents, and if substance, whether corporeal or spiritual; if accident, which category. Without this, the terms remain unclear.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that dahr and sarmad are self-explanatory terms that need no further analysis.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that without specifying whether they are relations, substances, or accidents, and without further classification, these terms remain meaningless.

Checkpoint
According to the first question, what did Avicenna fail to clarify about dahr and sarmad?
A) Whether they are corporeal or spiritual
B) Whether they are the same as zaman

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi demands that any concept like dahr and sarmad must be classified as either mere relations, or as a substance (corporeal or spiritual) or an accident (with genus specification), otherwise they remain unintelligible.

Will you commit to questioning one abstract spiritual concept you take for granted this week?
?
Al-Razi insists that without specifying whether dahr and sarmad are substances or accidents, they remain unintelligible. What is one concept in your faith that you have accepted without examining its ontological status? Are you willing to investigate it deeper, even if it leads to uncomfortable questions about your assumptions?

The Dilemma of Dahr: Static or Flowing?
Al-Razi presents a dilemma on the nature of dahr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's dilemma against Avicenna's definition of dahr: whether dahr is static or changing leads to contradictions in its causal role.

Core Idea
Al-Razi traps Avicenna: either dahr is static and cannot cause change, or it is changing and redundant.

Al-Razi now turns to the nature of dahr itself. He presses Avicenna with a dilemma: if dahr is static, it cannot produce changing relations; if it is changing, then it is no different from time and unnecessary. This passage shows Al-Razi's skill in exposing internal contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once, then the translation, to grasp the overall structure of the dilemma.
Notice that Al-Razi uses conditional statements to explore both possibilities.
2
Identify the two branches
The passage presents two main branches: assuming dahr is static, and assuming dahr is changing. Find where each branch begins.
The static branch starts with 'فإن كان ثابتاً' and the changing branch with 'وأما ان المسمى بالدهر أمراً متغيراً'.
3
Follow the implications for static dahr
If dahr is static, can it cause changing relations? Al-Razi offers two sub-options: yes leads to Plato's view, no leads to impossibility. Understand why both are problematic.
4
Follow the implications for changing dahr
If dahr is changing, can it relate to unchanging things? If yes, then zaman suffices; if no, then dahr fails. Understand why both are problematic.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Neither horn of the dilemma works, so Avicenna's definition of dahr is undermined. Consider why this is a powerful critique.
The dilemma forces Avicenna to either accept an absurdity or abandon the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : أن يقال : إنك زعمت : أن نسبة الثابت إلى المتغير هو الدهر. فنقول : هذا الذي سميته بالدهر ، هل هو في نفسه ثابت مستقر ، أو هو في نفسه متغير سيال ؟ فإن كان ثابتاً فالثابت هل يجوز جعله سبباً لحصول النسب المتغيرة أو لا يجوز ؟ فإن جاز فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : المقتضى لحصول نسب بعض المتغيرات إلى بعض شيء ثابت في ذاته ، كما هو قول أفلاطون ، وإن لم يجز هذا ، فكيف جعلتم هذا الشيء المسمى بالدهر مع كونه ثابتاً سبباً لحصول النسب الواقعة بين الأشياء الثابتة، وبين الأشياء المتغيرة ؟ وذلك لان هذه النسب تكون متغيرة لا محالة فإذا كان الدهر ثابتاً ، والثابت لا يجوز جعله سبباً للنسب المتغيرة ، لزم امتناع كون الدهر سبباً لحصول الأشياء المتغيرة ؟ وذلك لأن هذه النسب تكون متغيرة لا محالة فإذا كان الدهر ثابتاً، والثابت لا يجوز جعله سبباً للنسب المتغيرة، لزم امتناع كون الدهر سبباً لحصول هذه النسب المتغيرة ، وأما ان المسمى بالدهر أمراً متغيراً في ذاته ، فالشيء المتغير في ذاته ، هل يمكن جعله سبباً لحصول النسبة مع الأشياء الثابتة ، أو لا يمكن ؟ فإن أمكن فلم لا يكون الزمان كافياً في ذلك حتى لا يحتاج إلى إثبات هذا الأمر المسمى بالدهر ، وإن لم يمكن فكيف جعلتم الدهر المتغير في ذاته ، سبباً لحصول النسبة إلى الأشياء الثابتة ؟
The second question: that it is said: You claimed that the relation of the unchanging to the changing is al-dahr. Then we say: this which you have called al-dahr, is it in itself static and stable, or is it in itself changing and flowing? If it is static, then is it permissible to make the static a cause for the occurrence of changing relations or not? If it is permissible, then why is it not permissible to say: the necessitator for the occurrence of the relations of some changing things to each other is a thing static in itself, as is the saying of Plato. And if this is not permissible, then how have you made this thing called al-dahr, despite it being static, a cause for the occurrence of the relations that occur between unchanging things and changing things? That is because these relations are necessarily changing; so if al-dahr is static, and the static is not permissible to be made a cause for changing relations, then the impossibility of al-dahr being a cause for the occurrence of changing things necessarily follows. And as for if what is called al-dahr is a thing changing in itself, then a thing changing in itself, can it be made a cause for the occurrence of relation with unchanging things or not? If it is possible, then why is not al-zaman sufficient for that so that there is no need to posit this thing called al-dahr? And if it is not possible, then how have you made al-dahr, changing in itself, a cause for the occurrence of relation to unchanging things?
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents the second question, structured as a dilemma. Al-Razi considers two possibilities for the nature of dahr: either it is static (thabit mustaqirr) or changing (mutaghayyir sayyal). If static, can it be the cause of changing relations? If yes, then why not accept Plato's view that a static thing causes relations among changing things? If no, then dahr cannot cause the changing relations between unchanging and changing things. If dahr is changing, then can it cause a relation with unchanging things? If yes, then why is zaman (which is also changing) not sufficient, eliminating the need for dahr? If no, then dahr cannot cause the relation to unchanging things. Either way, Avicenna's definition faces a problem.

مستقر
متغير
