سبب
نسب متغيرة
أفلاطون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming dahr is simply 'eternity' without considering its causal role.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the nature of dahr directly affects whether it can serve as the cause of relations; it is not a static label but an explanatory concept.

Checkpoint
What is the main dilemma Al-Razi presents in this passage?
A) Whether dahr is substance or accident
B) Whether dahr is static or changing, and whether either can serve as a cause
C) Whether dahr is prior or posterior
D) Whether dahr is known or unknown

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi argues that whether dahr is static or changing, it fails to serve the causal role Avicenna assigns to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi forces a choice: dahr must be either static or flowing. When you reflect on God's attribute of being eternal (al-azali), do you tend to think of it as a static state or a flowing duration? Which horn of this dilemma does your conception fall into, and can you defend it from the contradictions Al-Razi exposes? If not, what does that say about the intellectual foundations of your faith?
?
The dilemma shows that a concept we might use to relate God to time can be logically problematic. Are there any beliefs you hold about God's relationship to creation that you have never subjected to logical scrutiny? Will you be willing to abandon or revise them if you find they lead to similar contradictions?
?
Al-Razi uses this argument to challenge a grand philosophical system. Are you more willing to accept the conclusions of revelation even when they seem philosophically paradoxical, or do you trust human reasoning to resolve such dilemmas? How does your answer affect your submission to God's word?

Time as an Independent Substance: The Platonic View
The author explains the Platonic conception of time, eternity, and timelessness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Platonic conception of time as an independent substance, distinct from Aristotle's measure-of-motion view, and how the author defines sarmad, dahr, and zaman based on relations.

Core Idea
Time is an independent substance, not just a measure.

This lesson explores a key philosophical debate about the nature of time. The author presents the Platonic view, arguing that time is a real, self-subsisting entity that relates to change in different ways. By examining the concepts of sarmad, dahr, and zaman, we see how this view differs from Aristotle's and why the author prefers it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a citation from Ibn Sina.
2
Identify the three key terms
Find the terms sarmad, dahr, and zaman in the passage. See how the author defines each one in relation to the same underlying substance.
These terms are not synonyms but reflect different relations.
3
Understand the argument for time as substance
The author argues that time is a substance (jawhar) subsisting by itself. Notice how he uses Ibn Sina's statement to lead into Plato's view.
Aristotle's view is that time is a measure of motion.
4
Reflect on the threefold definition
The author gives three definitions based on what time is related to: unchanging essences, pre-motion, or concurrent change. Ask yourself why this distinction matters.
This shows time is not just a concept but something real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ في كتاب عيون الحكمة : ( الدهر في ذاته من السرمد ، وهو بالقياس إلى الزمان دهر ، وأقول : معناه : إن الدهر في ذاته شيء ثابت غير متغير إلا أنه إذا نسب إلى الزمان الذي هو موجود متغير في ذاته ، سمي دهراً . وهذا تصريح بأن الدهر ثابت في نفسه وذاته، إلا أنه مع كونه كذلك ، فإنه يقتضي حصول هذه النسب المتغيرة .
وفي هذا اعتراف بأن الشيء قد يكون ثابتاً في ذاته ، ومع ذلك فإنه يقتضي تقدير الأحوال المتغيرة بالمقادير المخصوصة . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إن هذا عين مذهب أفلاطون ، وهو أن الزمان جوهر قائم بنفسه مستقل بذاته ، إلا أنه يقتضي تقدير هذه الأحوال المختلفة ، وقد ثبت أن الناصرين لمذهب أرسطاطاليس في أن الزمان مقدار الحركة ، لا يمكنهم التوغل في شيء من مضايق المباحث المتعلقة بالزمان ، إلا عند الرجوع إلى قول أفلاطون .
وأقول : قد ذكرنا : أن الأقرب عندنا في المدة والزمان هو مذهب أفلاطون . وهو أنه موجود قائم بنفسه مستقل بذاته، فإن اعتبرنا نسبة ذاته إلى ذوات الموجودات القائمة المبرأة عن التغير، سميناه بالسرمد ، وإن اعتبرنا نسبة ذاته إلى ما قبل حصول الحركات والتغيرات ، فذاك هو الدهر الداهر ، وإن اعتبرنا نسبة ذاته إلى كون المتغيرات مقارنة له حاصلة معه فذاك هو الزمان وبالله التوفيق .
The Shaykh said in the book 'Uyun al-Hikmah: 'Timelessness (al-dahr) in its essence is from eternity (al-sarmad), and it is, in relation to time (al-zaman), timelessness (dahr).' I say: Its meaning is that timelessness in its essence is something fixed, unchanging, except that when it is related to time, which is an existent changing in its essence, it is called timelessness. This is an explicit statement that timelessness is fixed in itself and its essence, except that despite being thus, it necessitates the occurrence of these changing relations. And in this is an acknowledgment that a thing may be fixed in its essence, and yet it necessitates the estimation of changing states by specific measures. If you understand this, we say: This is exactly the doctrine of Plato, namely that time is a substance subsisting by itself, independent in its essence, except that it necessitates the estimation of these different states. And it has been established that the supporters of Aristotle's doctrine that time is a measure of motion cannot delve into any of the narrow investigations related to time except by returning to the statement of Plato. And I say: We have mentioned that the closest to us concerning duration and time is the doctrine of Plato. It is that it is an existent subsisting by itself, independent in its essence. If we consider the relation of its essence to the essences of existing things [which are] free from change, we name it eternity (al-sarmad). If we consider the relation of its essence to what is before the occurrence of motions and changes, that is persistent timelessness (al-dahr al-dahir). If we consider the relation of its essence to the fact that changing things are concurrent with it, occurring with it, that is time (al-zaman). And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical analysis of the concepts of eternity (sarmad), timelessness (dahr), and time (zaman), with the author defending the Platonic view that time is an independent substance rather than merely a measure of motion. The passage begins by citing a statement from Ibn Sina (referred to as 'the Shaykh') in his work 'Uyun al-Hikmah, which says that timelessness (dahr) in its essence is derived from eternity (sarmad), but when considered in relation to time (zaman), it is called timelessness. The author then explains that timelessness is inherently fixed and unchanging, but when related to the changing reality of time, it acquires the name 'dahr'. This implies that something can be inherently stable yet still necessitate the occurrence of variable relations. The author interprets this as an acknowledgement that an entity may be fixed in its essence while still requiring the estimation of changing states by specific measures. He then declares that this is precisely the doctrine of Plato: time is a substance (jawhar) subsisting by itself, independent, yet it necessitates the estimation of diverse states. The author further asserts that the supporters of Aristotle, who define time as a measure of motion, cannot properly address the intricate problems of time without ultimately resorting to Plato's view. The passage concludes with the author stating his own preference for Plato's position regarding 'duration' (al-mudda) and time. He provides three distinct definitions based on relation: if we relate the essence of this substance to immutable existents, we call it eternity (sarmad); if we relate it to the period prior to motion and change, we call it persistent timelessness (al-dahr al-dahir); and if we relate it to the concurrence of changing things with it, we call it time (zaman). The entire discussion is concerned with establishing time as a real, independent entity rather than a mere conceptual measure.

Key Terms
الدهر
السرمد
الزمان
أفلاطون
أرسطاطاليس
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author entirely adopts Ibn Sina's view on time.
Correction: The author uses Ibn Sina's statement as a starting point but explicitly affirms that the closest doctrine to his own is Plato's, which holds time to be an independent substance.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the closest view to his own regarding the nature of time and duration?
A) The view of Aristotle that time is a measure of motion
B) The view of Plato that time is a substance subsisting by itself
C) The view of the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) that timelessness is from eternity
D) The view that time is an illusion

Key Takeaway
Time is an independent substance whose different names (sarmad, dahr, zaman) depend on its relation to changing or unchanging entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that your daily experience of time is rooted in a substance that also relates to the unchanging (sarmad), does this change how you view the constancy of Allah's attributes? How can this truth deepen your reliance on Him amidst life's changes?
?
The passage distinguishes three relations of the same time substance. Which of these three—eternity, timelessness, or time—do you most need to meditate on in your current life situation, and what concrete action will you take to align your heart with that aspect?

The Order of Past, Present, and Future
Defines the terms past, present, future and provides a proof of their temporal order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the natural temporal order of events and the descriptive order of past, present, and future, along with the proof for the latter.

Core Idea
In description, future comes first, then present, then past.

Time is often thought of as moving from past to present to future. But when we describe events, the order flips: what is anticipated comes first in thought, then experienced, then remembered. This lesson unpacks that reversal through a clear definition and logical proof from the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that two different orders are mentioned: the natural order (past→present→future) and the descriptive order (future→present→past).
The passage uses the word 'ثم' (then) to sequence the orders.
2
Identify the two orders
The passage first states the natural order (past, then present, then future). Then it says 'if we consider the description' the order reverses. Locate these two statements and understand the contrast.
The descriptive order is about the attributes of being past, present, or future.
3
Study the proof
The proof uses an example of a thing that does not yet exist but is anticipated. Trace the steps: it is future, then it becomes present, then it becomes past. This shows the descriptive order.
The proof demonstrates why the descriptive order is future→present→past.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Think about how these two orders apply to everyday life. Why might understanding this distinction be important for deeper discussions about time?
This builds a foundation for further study of time in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو الماضي، ثم الحال ثم المستقبل. وأما إن اعتبرنا وصف كونه ماضياً وحاضراً ومستقبلاً فههنا ينعكس الأمر، فالمتقدم هو وصف كونه مستقبلاً، ثم يتلوه صيرورته حاضراً، ثم يتلوه كونه ماضياً. والدليل عليه: وهو أن الشيء الذي لم يوجد، وكان بفرضية أنه سيوجد، فعند كونه كذلك يكون مستقبلاً، فإذا حصل صار حاضراً، فإذا انقضى وانقرض فإنه يصير ماضياً، فيثبت أن الشيء في المرتبة الأولى يكون مستقبلاً ثم يصير حالاً ثم بالأخيرة يصير ماضياً.
It is the past, then the present, then the future. But if we consider the description of being past, present, and future, then the order reverses: what is prior is the description of being future, then its becoming present follows, then its being past follows. The proof for that: that a thing which did not exist and was assumed to exist, when it is thus it is future, then when it occurs it becomes present, then when it passes and ends it becomes past. So it is established that the thing in the first rank is future, then it becomes present, then lastly it becomes past.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage defines the three temporal states—past, present, future—and provides a demonstration of their order. The author begins by stating the natural order: past, then present, then future. However, when considering the descriptions of being past, present, or future, the order is reversed. The description of being future is first because the thing is anticipated; then it becomes present, then finally past. The proof uses an example of a thing that does not yet exist but is anticipated: initially it is future, upon its occurrence it becomes present, and after its passing it becomes past. This establishes the logical order: future first, then present, then past.

The Irreversible Direction of Time
Explains the second property of time: future becomes present then past, but past cannot become present or future.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the irreversible transformations of temporal states according to the second property of time.

Core Idea
Time flows only forward: future → present → past, never backward.

Time has a fixed direction. The second property of time explains how each temporal state — future, present, and past — can or cannot transform into others. This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah clarifies these transformations, showing that the future becomes present first, then past, while the past never returns. Let’s examine the text to grasp this essential concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the author systematically covers each state.
The passage is short but dense. Read carefully.
2
Identify the three temporal states
List the three states: future, present, past. For each, note which transformations are possible and which are not according to the passage.
Focus on the verbs: 'becomes' (يصير) and 'cannot become' (لا يصير).
3
Understand the direction of change
Observe that only the future can become present and then past. The present can only become past, never future. The past cannot change at all.
This establishes an irreversible arrow of time.
4
Address a common misunderstanding
Some might think the present can become future, but the passage explicitly says it cannot. Reflect on why this makes sense in a linear concept of time.
Time moves forward, not backward.
5
Test yourself with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to confirm your understanding.
Choose the option that matches the passage exactly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخاصية الثانية: المستقبل يصير حالاً أولاً، ثم يصير ماضياً، وأما الماضي فإنه لا يصير حالاً البتة ولا مستقبلاً، وأما الحال فإنه يمكن أن يصير ماضياً لكنه لا يمكن أن يصير مستقبلاً. وأما المستقبل فإنه يصير حالاً أولاً، ثم يصير ماضياً ثانياً.
The second property: The future becomes present first, then becomes past. As for the past, it never becomes present nor future. As for the present, it can become past but cannot become future. As for the future, it becomes present first, then becomes past secondly.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage states the second property of time: the temporal transformations. The future is the only state that can become present and then past. The past is fixed and cannot revert to present or future. The present can only move forward to the past, not backward to the future. This establishes the irreversible direction of time.

Third Property: Past Recedes, Future Approaches
States the third property of time with a poetic example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third property of time according to Islamic cosmology: that the past continually recedes and the future continually approaches, and how this is both intuitively self-evident and poetically expressed.

Core Idea
Time is dynamic: the past slides away, the future draws near, every moment.

This lesson explores a fascinating property of time recognized in Islamic thought: the past is not static but constantly becoming more distant, while the future is constantly approaching. The author asserts that this knowledge is self-evident, and then illustrates it with a beautiful poetic supplication. Let's see how this property is presented.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

The Arabic uses simple terms: الماضي (past) and المستقبل (future).
2
Reflect on the intuitive nature
The author says knowledge of this property is بديهي (self-evident). Think about your own experience: doesn't the past always seem to be slipping further away?
This property is a foundational observation about the flow of time.
3
Analyze the poetic illustration
Read the line of poetry. Notice how the poet uses the dynamic nature of time to make a supplication: may what you desire be closer than tomorrow, and what you fear be farther than yesterday.
The poet leverages the natural progression of time to express a wish.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخاصية الثالثة: إن الماضي يصير في كل وقت أبعد مما كان قبله، والمستقبل يصير في كل وقت أقرب مما كان قبله، والعلم بذلك بديهي. ونظم بعض الشعراء هذا المعنى في مدح واحد فقال: فلا زال ما تهواه أقرب من غد ولا زال ما تخشاه أبعد من أمس
The third property: The past becomes every moment farther than it was before, and the future becomes every moment nearer than it was before. Knowledge of that is self-evident. This passage presents the third property: the dynamic nature of time. The past keeps receding; each moment the past is farther than it was before. The future keeps approaching; each moment it is nearer. The author states that this is intuitively obvious and then gives a poetic example: a line of poetry expressing the wish that what one desires be closer than tomorrow (i.e., very near) and what one fears be more distant than yesterday (i.e., very far). This uses the properties of time to make a supplication.

The Fourth Property: Priority in Past and Future
Explains that earlier events in the past are farther from the past, while earlier events in the future are closer to the present.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth property of time: the asymmetry of priority in past and future events.

Core Idea
Earlier past events are farther back; earlier future events are closer to now.

This lesson introduces the fourth property of time, which describes how priority works differently in the past and future. Understanding this asymmetry is essential for grasping the directional nature of time. The passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah succinctly states this property.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage attentively
Read the Arabic passage once, then read its literal translation. Notice the parallel structure between past and future statements.
2
Identify the two parts of the property
The passage first states the rule for the past, then for the future. Compare the two: in the past, earlier events are farther; in the future, earlier events are nearer.
3
Reflect on the direction of time
Think about why this property holds: the past extends backward from the present, so earlier events are more remote. The future extends forward, so earlier events are closer to the present.
This highlights the symmetry-breaking nature of time.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check that you understand the future part of the property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخاصية الرابعة: إن كل ما كان متقدماً في الماضي، فهو أبعد عن الماضي مما كان متأخراً. وفي المستقبل بالعكس، لأن كل ما كان متقدماً في المستقبل، فإنه أقرب إلى الحال مما كان متأخراً.
The fourth property: Every thing that was prior in the past is farther from the past than what was later. And in the future it is the opposite, because everything that was prior in the future is nearer to the present than what was later.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage states the fourth property: within the past, events that occurred earlier are more distant from the past than those that occurred later. In other words, the earlier something happened in the past, the further back it is. Conversely, in the future, events that are earlier (i.e., closer to the present) are nearer to the present than later events. This property highlights the directional asymmetry of time: the past extends backward, the future extends forward.

Key Terms
التقدم
التأخر
الماضي
المستقبل
القرب
البعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that earlier events in the past are closer to the present than later events, because 'earlier' intuitively means nearer to the starting point of time.
Correction: Actually, earlier events in the past are farther from the present because they occurred further back. The property clarifies that priority has opposite implications in the past versus the future.

Checkpoint
In the future, which events are nearer to the present?
A) Later events
B) Prior events
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The fourth property establishes that temporal priority has opposite effects in past and future: earlier past events are more remote, while earlier future events are more proximate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Fifth Property: Stronger Relation Between Present and Future
Argues that the present has a stronger relation to the future than to the past because the future has potential existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the present has a stronger relation to the future than to the past, based on the concept of potential existence.

Core Idea
The future has potential existence; the past has none, so the present is more connected to the future.

This lesson explores the fifth property discussed in the text: the asymmetry in temporal relations. The author argues that the present is more strongly linked to the future than to the past, using the notions of actual and potential existence. This insight is then reflected in the Arabic language itself, where the same verb form (mudāri') is used for both present and future, but not for the past.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the author's main claim and the reasoning.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the existence distinctions
The author distinguishes actual existence (present), potential existence (future), and non-existence (past). Grasp why potential existence makes the future more connected.
The phrase 'especially if we say that the restoration of the nonexistent is impossible' is key.
3
Consider the linguistic evidence
The Arabic mudāri' tense covers both present and future, but not past. Reflect on how this supports the property.
This shows language mirrors ontological relations.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about the broader principle: the impossibility of restoring the nonexistent reinforces the asymmetry.
This connects to theological discussions about resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخاصية الخامسة: أن نقول: المناسبة بين الحال وبين المستقبل، أشد من المناسبة بين الحال وبين الماضي. وذلك لأن الحال موجود بالفعل، والمستقبل وإن كان غير موجود بالفعل إلا أنه موجود بالقوة. أما الماضي فإنه غير موجود لا بالفعل ولا بالقوة، لا سيما إذا قلنا: إعادة المعدوم ممتنعة. ولهذه الدقيقة وضع أهل اللغة العربية لفظ المضارع مشتركاً بين الحال والاستقبال، وما أثبتوا هذا الاشتراك بين الحال وبين الماضي.
The fifth property: We say: The relation between the present and the future is stronger than the relation between the present and the past. That is because the present exists in actuality, and the future, even though it does not exist in actuality, yet it exists in potentiality. As for the past, it does not exist either in actuality or in potentiality, especially if we say that the restoration of the nonexistent is impossible. For this subtle reason, the Arabic language experts placed the term 'mudāri' (present-future tense) as shared between the present and the future, and they did not establish this sharing between the present and the past.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage explains the fifth property: the relationship between the present and the future is stronger than that between the present and the past. The reasoning is that the present is actual existence. The future, though not actual, has potential existence—it can come into being. The past, however, has no existence at all, neither actual nor potential, especially if we hold that what has ceased to exist cannot be restored. This explains why Arabic grammar uses a single tense (the mudāri') for both present and future, but not for the past, reflecting the stronger connection between present and future.

Key Terms
العلاقة
الحال
المستقبل
الماضي
الوجود بالقوة
المضارع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the past has some lingering existence, but the author denies even potential existence for the past.
Correction: The author states that the past does not exist either in actuality or in potentiality, especially given the impossibility of restoring the nonexistent.

Checkpoint
Why is the relation between the present and the future stronger than that between the present and the past?
A) Because the future is closer in time
B) Because the future has potential existence while the past has none
C) Because the past is longer
D) Because the future is actual

Key Takeaway
The present's stronger relation to the future than to the past is grounded in the ontological difference between potential existence (future) and non-existence (past), and this is mirrored in Arabic grammar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Time's Nobility Over Space
States that time is moving and space stationary, making time nobler.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why time is considered more noble than space in this philosophical tradition, based on the attributes of motion and stillness.

Core Idea
Time's constant motion elevates it above stationary space.

This lesson examines the eighth property discussed by the author: a comparison between time and space. The author asserts that time is perpetually in motion while space is eternally still, and since motion is nobler than stillness, time is more noble. A quote from a shaykh reinforces this hierarchy by stating that space follows time in existence. Understanding this property helps clarify the metaphysical status of time relative to space.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the concise style and the quote.
The passage is short, so read carefully.
2
Identify key attributes of time and space

3
Understand the logical argument
The argument is: movement is more noble than stillness; time moves, space is still; therefore, time is more noble than space. Reflect on why movement might be considered nobler.
This is a simple syllogism.
4
Reflect on the shaykh's quote
Read the quote: 'Space follows time in existence, and finds it the first causes of time in determination.' Consider how this supports the property.
The quote emphasizes the priority of time over space.

Source Text

Since motion is more noble than stillness, time is more noble than space. The author then quotes a shaykh (likely a reference to a philosopher or theologian) who says that space follows time in existence and that space finds time as the first causes of time in determination. This suggests a hierarchy where time precedes space.

The First Analogy: Point and Line, and Its Critique
Author presents and critiques the first analogy for time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first analogy for understanding time—the point moving to make a line—and the author's critique that the now is not a sensible object, making its motion inconceivable.

Core Idea
Time cannot be explained by the movement of instants because instants are intellectual, not physical.

The author begins presenting analogies to help conceptualize time and duration. The first analogy compares the now to a point moving to create a line. However, he raises several problems: the now is not a sensible entity, so how can it move? And onto what does it move? This critique shows the limits of using physical analogies for metaphysical concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author first states the analogy, then critiques it.
2
Identify the analogy and its components
The analogy compares the point moving to make a line to the nows extending to make time. Identify the key elements: point, line, now, time.
The point is sensible, the now is not.
3
Understand the critique
The author finds the analogy problematic because the now is not sensible—it is an intellectual concept—so its motion is inconceivable. He also questions the direction of motion.
This critique challenges the analogy's validity.
4
Consider the Aristotelian perspective
The author notes that this analogy conflicts with Aristotelian philosophy, which does not accept time as composed of successive nows.
This shows the analogy's incompatibility with a major school of thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنختم هذا الفصل بذكر تمثيلات ذكرها الناس لتقريب ماهية المدة والزمان من الأفهام. وهي أربعة:
فالأول: قالوا: النقطة إذا امتدت، فعلت بحركتها الخط، وكذلك الآنات التي لا تنقسم إذا امتدت وسالت، فعلت بامتدادها وسيلانها: الزمان. وهذا التشبيه فيه بعض الصعوبة، لأن حركة النقطة على السطح أمر مشاهد. وأما حركة الآن فغير معقولة. لأن الآن ليس موجوداً مشاراً إليه بحسب الحس، بل هو أمر معقول. فكيف يعقل كونه متحركاً؟ وبتقدير أن يتحرك، فعل أي شيء ينحرك؟ أعلى جرم من أجرام الأفلاك أم على العناصر؟ فهذا أمر غير معلوم التصور.
واعلم أن هذا الكلام لا يليق بقول أصحاب أرسطاطاليس، لأن ذلك يقتضي حصول الزمان من الآنات المتتالية وهم لا يقبلون به. وأيضاً فيرجع البحث الأول وهو أن الآن كيف تحرك مع أنه ليس من الموجودات ذوات الوضع والإشارة؟ وعلى أي شي؟ أعلى الأفلاك أم على العناصر؟
الوجه الثالث: شبهوا ذلك بخيط ألقي على طرف حد السيف، ثم جر، فإنه يلاقي ذلك الخيط حد السيف جزءاً فجزءاً ثم يحصل منه أمر ممتد مستمر على ذلك التجدد الدائم.
الوجه الرابع في التمثيل: شبهوه بالماء السيال الذي يتوالى جزءاً فجزءاً، على طرف الأنبوبة.
فهذا مبلغ ما حصلناه من علم المدة والزمان. والله ولي الإحسان والغفران.
Let us conclude this chapter by mentioning analogies that people have mentioned to bring the essence of duration (al-muddah) and time (al-zamān) closer to understanding. They are four: The first: they said: The point (al-nuqṭah) when it extends, it makes the line (al-khaṭṭ) by its movement, and likewise the indivisible nows (al-ānāt) when they extend and flow, they make time by their extension and flow. This analogy has some difficulty, because the movement of the point on a surface is observable. As for the movement of the now, it is inconceivable, because the now is not an existent pointed to by sense, but rather an intellectual thing. So how can it be conceived as moving? And assuming it moves, onto what thing does it move? Is it onto a body of the celestial spheres or onto the elements? So this is a matter whose conception is unknown. And know that this statement does not befit the saying of the followers of Aristotle, because that entails time coming from successive nows, and they do not accept that. And also the first inquiry returns, which is: how can the now move given that it is not among the existents having position and indication? And upon what thing? Is it upon the celestial spheres or upon the elements? The third way: they likened that to a thread placed on the edge of a sword, then pulled; then that thread meets the edge of the sword part by part, and then from it arises an extended continuous matter upon that perpetual renewal. The fourth way of analogy: they likened it to flowing water which follows part by part at the tip of a tube. So this is the extent of what we have obtained from the knowledge of duration and time. And God is the possessor of beneficence and forgiveness.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of a chapter on time and duration, presenting four analogies used by scholars to help conceptualize these metaphysical entities. The author states that he will conclude with these analogies. The first analogy compares the point (nuqṭah) moving to draw a line to the indivisible nows (ānāt) extending to form time. The author then critiques it: while a point's motion is observable, the now is not a sensible object—it is an intellectual concept—so its motion is inconceivable. He also questions the direction of such motion (onto a celestial body or the elements) and notes that this analogy conflicts with Aristotelian philosophy, which does not accept time as composed of successive nows. The third analogy (the second is missing from the text) likens time to a thread drawn over a sword's edge, contacting part by part to produce a continuous extension. The fourth analogy compares time to flowing water emerging from a tube part by part. The author concludes by stating that this is the sum of what he has obtained of the knowledge of duration and time, and ends with a prayer. The missing second analogy is a lacuna in the available text, but the author's enumeration of 'four' is clear.

Key Terms
المدة (duration)
الزمان (time)
الآن (now/instant)
النقطة (point)
الخط (line)
الآنات التي لا تنقسم (indivisible nows)
أصحاب أرسطاطاليس (followers of Aristotle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is composed of discrete, self-moving instants that string together like a line from points.
Correction: The author argues that instants are not physical objects; they have no position and cannot move, so the analogy fails. Moreover, Aristotelian philosophy denies that time is composed of successive nows.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author finds the first analogy difficult?
A) The point and line analogy is too abstract.
B) The movement of the now is inconceivable because the now is not a sensible object.
C) The analogy contradicts the Qur'an.
D) The analogy was invented by Aristotle.

Key Takeaway
The first analogy fails because the now is an intellectual entity, not a physical one, making its motion inconceivable and the analogy unsound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You habitually think of time as a line or a flowing river. After reading this lesson, how has your mental picture of the 'now' changed? Will you continue to treat the present moment as if it moves?
?
The now is intellectual, not sensible. When you are absorbed in worldly concerns, do you live as if the 'now' is merely a fleeting point to be used up, or do you recognize its deeper reality? What one practice can you adopt today to honor the true nature of the present moment?
?
If you cannot even conceive how the now moves, how can you be sure that time itself is real in the way you assume? This lesson calls you to question your most basic assumptions. Are you willing to remain uncertain about the nature of time, or will you accept a comfortable analogy without critique?

The Third and Fourth Analogies: Thread and Flowing Water
Author presents two analogies for time and concludes the chapter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third and fourth analogies for time: a thread drawn over a sword and flowing water through a tube, both emphasizing continuous renewal part by part.

Core Idea
Time is like a thread contacting a blade part by part, or water flowing through a tube, revealing its continuous yet partite nature.

After critiquing the first analogy, the author presents two more analogies for time: a thread drawn over a sword's edge and flowing water through a tube. These analogies emphasize time's continuous extension that is renewed part by part, providing a more satisfactory model that avoids the problems of the first analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the third analogy
Read the description of the thread on the sword. The thread meets the edge part by part, producing a continuous extended matter.
Notice the emphasis on 'part by part' and 'perpetual renewal'.
2
Read the fourth analogy
Read the analogy of flowing water at the tip of a tube. Water flows part by part, continuously.
This analogy also uses the idea of parts following in succession.
3
Compare the two analogies
Both analogies illustrate a process where a continuous whole arises from successive, interconnected parts. Consider how this differs from the first analogy.
Unlike the first analogy, these do not require the motion of an abstract now.
4
Reflect on the author's conclusion
The author ends by saying this is what he has obtained of the knowledge of duration and time, concluding with a prayer.
This signals the end of the chapter and his satisfaction with these analogies.

Plato's Symbolic Notion of Place: Hylic and Form
Explains and corrects the interpretation of Plato's statements about place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of Plato's statements about place as hylic and form, and why literal readings lead to false contradictions.

Core Idea
Plato's 'place' as hylic and form is symbolic, not literal.

Plato's remarks on place have long been debated. Some took his words literally and accused him of contradiction. This passage clarifies that when Plato called place 'hylic' or 'form,' he was speaking symbolically—referring to space's receptivity and its dimensional structure. Let's explore how this resolves the apparent paradox.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Start by reading the source passage in Arabic and then its literal English translation. Pay attention to the two interpretations Plato offered for 'place' and the author's resolution.
The passage is dense; read slowly and note the terms 'hylic' (الهيولى) and 'form' (الصورة).
2
Understand the two symbolic meanings
The author explains that Plato used 'hylic' to emphasize space's receptivity (like prime matter), and 'form' to highlight the three dimensions that distinguish body from abstract substance. These are not literal descriptions of place's essence but metaphorical comparisons.
3
Grasp the refutation of literal critics
The author points out that critics who took Plato's words literally and argued that a body moving would leave its hylic and formal parts behind miss the point. The statements are symbolic; attacking them literally is unreasonable.
Notice how the author defends Plato by invoking the intended symbolic (رمز) nature of the speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصفات المتلاحقة. وههنا هذا الفضاء أمر واقف لا يتغير ولا يتبدل البتة إنه تتوارد عليه الأجسام، وهذا الفضاء يقبلها، فكأن هذا الفضاء مشابهاً للهيولى من هذا الوجه، فلا جرم سموا هذا الفضاء بالهيولى بحسب هذا التأويل. وإنما سموه بالصورة، وذلك لأن الجوهر الجسماني إنما امتاز عن الجواهر المجردة العقلية لأجل كونه قابلاً لهذه الأبعاد الثلاثة، فهذه الأبعاد الثلاثة هي كالجزء الصوري لماهية الجسم. فلما كان هذا الفضاء عبارة عن هذه الأبعاد المجردة الواقعة سموه بالصورة بحسب هذا التأويل. فهذا هو التفسير الصحيح لما نقل عن أفلاطون، أنه كان تارة يقول: المكان هو الهيولى، وتارة كان يقول: المكان هو الصورة. ثم إن جماعة ممن أرادوا تقبيح قوله في أعين الناس، نقلوا عنه: أنه يقول: المكان عبارة عن الهيولي أو عن الصورة، ثم أخذوا يحتجون على إبطاله بأن الجسم إذا انتقل من مكان إلى مكان، فهو إنما انتقل بمجموع أجزائه التي هي الهيولى والصورة، فكيف يمكن أن يقال المكان هو الهيولى والصورة؟ إلا أنه على الوجه الذي لخصناه، ظهر أنه إنما أطلق اسم الهيولى تارة، واسم الصورة أخرى على المكان بناء على التأويل الذي شرحناه والتفسير الذي لخصناه. فأما إجراء الكلام الذي قصد به الرمز على ظاهره، ثم الاشتغال بالطعن فيه، فذلك مما لا يليق بالعقلاء الكاملين.
And the [other] connected attributes. And here, this space is a fixed matter that does not change or alter at all; bodies come upon it successively, and this space receives them. So this space resembles hylic (al-hayula) from this aspect, thus no wonder they named this space 'hylic' according to this interpretation. And they named it 'form' (al-sura) because the corporeal substance (al-jawhar al-jismani) is distinguished from abstract intellectual substances (al-jawahir al-mujarrada al-'aqliyya) only by its being receptive to these three dimensions; thus these three dimensions are like the formal part of the essence of body. So when this space consists of these abstract fixed dimensions, they named it 'form' according to this interpretation. This is the correct interpretation of what was transmitted from Plato—that he sometimes said 'place is hylic' and sometimes said 'place is form'. Then a group of people who wanted to discredit his statement in people's eyes transmitted from him that he says 'place is hylic or form', and then they undertook to refute it by saying that when a body moves from one place to another, it moves with all its parts which are hylic and form; so how can it be said that place is hylic and form? However, according to the way we summarized, it became clear that he only applied the name 'hylic' sometimes and the name 'form' other times to place, based on the interpretation we explained and the explanation we summarized. As for taking the speech that was intended as a symbol (ramz) at face value and then engaging in criticizing it, that is not befitting for complete rational people.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a correction of the interpretation of Plato's statements about place. The author explains that when Plato called place 'hylic' (hayula), he meant that space is receptive like prime matter; when he called it 'form' (sura), he meant that space consists of the three abstract dimensions that give form to bodies. The author then refutes critics who took Plato's words literally and pointed out an alleged contradiction: if place is both hylic and form, how can a body move leaving its parts behind? The answer is that Plato was using symbolic language, not literal descriptions. The author concludes that attacking such symbolic speech literally is unworthy of rational people.

Key Terms
الفضاء
الهيولى
الصورة
المكان
الأبعاد الثلاثة
الرمز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Plato's statements that place is hylic and place is form are contradictory and cannot both be true.
Correction: They are not contradictory because they are symbolic: 'hylic' refers to space's receptivity, 'form' to its dimensional structure.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did Plato sometimes call place 'hylic' (hayula) and sometimes 'form' (sura)?
A) He was inconsistent and confused.
B) He used 'hylic' to refer to space's receptivity and 'form' to refer to the three dimensions.
C) He changed his view over time.
D) He meant that space is both matter and form in a literal sense.

Key Takeaway
Plato's descriptions of place as hylic and form are compatible metaphorical references to space's receptivity and dimensional structure.

?

?
When you encounter a difficult concept in kalam or philosophy, do you rush to criticize or do you seek the intended meaning? How does this passage challenge your approach?
?
Imagine you are one of those who transmitted Plato's words with the intention to discredit him. What does that teach you about the responsibility of transmitting knowledge and the harm of taking things out of context? How will you change your own speech about others' statements starting today?

Classification of Theories on the Reality of Place
Classifies the main theories of place into space/void and surface, with subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main doctrines on the reality of place (space/void vs. surface) and their subdivisions among theologians and philosophers.

Core Idea
The reality of place reduces to either space/void or the containing surface.

The author has previously argued that the valid opinions on the reality of place are only two: space/void or surface. Now he provides a detailed classification of who holds each view and how they subdivide. This lesson maps out the schools and their internal differences, showing that even within the same tradition there is disagreement—for example, some philosophers allow empty space while others deny it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of classification. Then read the English translation. Notice how the author introduces the two main categories and then subdivides each.
The passage is a single continuous classification.
2
Identify the two main theories
The author states that the valid statement reduces to two: place is either space (al-fada') or surface (al-sath). Find these two in the text.
Space is also called void (al-khala').
3
Examine the first theory: space/void
The author splits the proponents of space/void into two groups: the theologians (mutakallimun) and the philosophers. Note their different positions—theologians deny any existence to void, while philosophers affirm it as existing dimensions.
The philosophers are further divided on whether empty space is possible.
4
Examine the second theory: surface
The second theory is that place is only the containing surface. This is attributed to Aristotle and his followers. Identify the names given.
This view is simpler—no subdivision is mentioned.
5
Reflect on the classification
Consider the overall structure: a two-branch classification with further subdivisions. How does this help in understanding the debate about place?
This passage sets the stage for the author's own view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة: فقد ظهر بالبيان الذي لخصناه: أن القول المعتبر في حقيقة المكان هو أنه إما الفضاء وإما السطح. أما القائلون بأن المكان هو الفضاء والخلاء فهم فرقان: أحدهما المتكلمون. فإنهم يقولون: هذا الفضاء وهذا الخلاء محض وليس له وجود البتة. والفرقة الثانية: الفلاسفة. وهم يقولون: هذا الخلاء أبعاد موجودة قائمة بأنفسها، وهي أمكنة للأجسام. وهذا القول هو اختيار أفلاطون وأكثر من تقدمه من الحكماء المعتبرين. وهؤلاء فريقان: منهم من يقول: لا امتناع في بقاء هذا الفضاء خالياً عن الأجسام، ومنهم من يقول: إن ذلك ممتنع. وأما القائلون بأن المكان هو السطح الحاوي فقط. فهذا قول أرسطاطاليس وجمهور أتباعه كأبي نصر الفارابي، وأبي علي بن سينا. فهذا تفصيل المذاهب في هذا الباب.
And in summary, it has become clear by the explanation we summarized that the valid statement concerning the reality of place is that it is either space (al-fada') or surface (al-sath). As for those who say that place is space and void (al-khala'), they are two groups: one of them is the mutakallimun (theologians). They say: this space and this void are pure [nothing] and have no existence at all. The second group is the philosophers (al-falasifa). They say: this void is dimensions (ab'ad) existing by themselves (qa'ima bi-anfusiha), and they are places for bodies. This statement is the choice of Plato and most of the earlier respected sages. And these [philosophers] are two factions: some of them say: there is no impossibility in this space remaining empty of bodies, and some of them say: that is impossible. And as for those who say that place is only the containing surface (al-sath al-hawi), that is the statement of Aristotle (Aristatalis) and the majority of his followers, like Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Abu Ali Ibn Sina. This is the detailing of the doctrines in this matter.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the two main theories about the reality of place (al-makan). The author states that the valid opinion reduces to two possibilities: place is either space/void or surface. He then subdivides the first theory into two groups: the mutakallimun (Islamic theologians) who deny any existence to void, considering it pure nothing, and the philosophers (falasifa) who affirm void as existing dimensions that serve as places for bodies. The philosophers are further divided: some allow empty space (no impossibility in it being devoid of bodies), while others deem it impossible. The second theory is attributed to Aristotle and his followers, such as al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, who restrict place to the containing surface. The passage ends by noting that this is the detailed exposition of the doctrines on this topic.

Key Terms
المكان
الفضاء
الخلاء
السطح
المتكلمون
الفلاسفة
أفلاطون
أرسطاطاليس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All philosophers agree that place is space/void.
Correction: The passage shows that some philosophers (Aristotle and followers) say place is the containing surface, not space/void. Moreover, even among those who say place is space/void, there are disagreements about whether empty space is possible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the view of Aristotle and his followers on the reality of place?
A) Place is space and void.
B) Place is the containing surface only.
C) Place is dimensions existing by themselves.
D) Place has no existence.

Key Takeaway
The two main theories on the reality of place are space/void (with subdivisions among theologians and philosophers) and the containing surface (held by Aristotle and his school).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mutakallimun said the void has no existence at all. Have you ever felt an 'empty space' in your life—like loneliness or purposelessness? If the void is truly nothing, then what is that feeling actually pointing to? Could it be a call to fill your heart with Allah's remembrance? Take a moment to identify one such 'void' and commit to turning it into a space for dhikr tonight.
?
The philosophers split on whether empty space is possible. Think about your daily schedule: do you leave any 'empty spaces'—unfilled time for reflection and worship—or do you fill every moment with busyness? If empty space is impossible, what does that say about your constant need to occupy yourself? Resolve to create a 10-minute empty slot tomorrow just to sit still and be present with Allah.
?
Aristotle and his followers defined place as the containing surface. What are the 'surfaces' that contain you—your body, your home, your routines? Are they defining you more than they should? Imagine your soul as a place contained by something deeper: Allah's mercy. How can you shift your identity from being contained by the world to being contained by the divine? Write one practical change you can make today.

First Argument Against Place as Dimension: Interpenetration
Aristotle's first argument that if place is dimension then interpenetration follows.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Aristotle's conditional proof that if place is a dimension, interpenetration of dimensions necessarily follows, and why this is used to reject the view.

Core Idea
If place is a dimension, then objects would cause dimensions to interpenetrate—an impossibility.

This lesson presents the first of several arguments by Aristotle and his followers against the view that place (al-makan) is a self-subsistent dimension. The argument is a carefully structured conditional: if place is a dimension, then when an object occupies place, two dimensions must interpenetrate. But interpenetration is impossible, so place cannot be a dimension. This logical move is foundational for understanding later debates on the nature of space.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that this is a very formal logical argument.
2
Trace the conditional structure
The argument is a conditional: if place is dimension, then interpenetration follows. Identify the premise and the conclusion.
The conditional is the backbone.
3
Follow the case analysis
The proof examines what happens to the two dimensions (object's and place's) when an object occupies a place. It lists three possibilities and eliminates two.
Pay attention to the logical reasoning for elimination.
4
Analyze the union question
After establishing both dimensions exist, the argument considers whether they become united. It rejects union through three sub-cases.
Union is impossible because of the numerical distinctness.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Since union is impossible, the two dimensions remain distinct, leading to interpenetration. Thus the conditional is established.
The impossibility of interpenetration is assumed here; its proof comes later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث: في أن المكان هل يعقل أن يكون بعدا قائما بنفسه أم لا؟ احتج أرسطاطاليس وأتباعه على فساد هذا المذهب بوجوه ونحن نذكرها ونبحث فيها على سبيل الإنصاف: فالحجة الأولى: أنهم قالوا: إن كان المكان بعداً، لزم من حصول المتمكن في المكان تداخل البعدين، ولكن تداخل البعدين محال، فالقول بأن المكان هو البعد يجب أن يكون محالاً. أما بيان الشرطية: فهو أن المتمكن إذا حصل في المكان، ففي هذه الحالة إما أن يبقى البعدان معاً، أو يعدمان معاً، أو يبقى أحدهما ويعدم الآخر. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أنهما يعدمان معاً، فهذا باطل، وإلا لزم أن يكون المتمكن المعدوم حاصلاً في المكان المعدوم وأنه محال. وأما القسم الثالث: فهو أيضاً باطل، وإلا لزم أن يكون المتمكن المعدوم حاصلاً في مكان موجود أو بالعكس. وذلك أيضاً باطل. ولما بطل هذان القسمان، بقي القسم الأول وهو أن يكون كل واحد منهما موجوداً. ثم نقول: إما أن يقال: إنهما في هذه الحالة صارا متحدين، أو ليس الأمر كذلك. والأول باطل، لأنها حال الاتحاد، إن كانا موجودين فهما اثنان لا واحد. وإن عدما وحدث شيء ثالث، فلم يكن هذا اتحاداً للأولين، وحدوثاً للثالث. وإن بقي أحدهما وعدم الثاني، فالاتحاد ههنا أيضاً محال. لأن المعدوم والموجود لا يكونان شيئاً واحداً. ولما بطل القول بالاتحاد، ثبت أن كل واحد من ذينك البعدين، أعني بعد المتمكن وبعد المكان، يكون باقياً حال ذلك النفوذ. فصح ما ذكرنا: أنه لو كان المكان بعداً، لزم من حصول المتمكن في المكان تداخل البعدين.
Chapter Three: On whether place can be a self-subsistent dimension or not. Aristotle and his followers argued for the corruption of this view with proofs, and we will mention them and examine them fairly. The first proof: they said: If place is a dimension, then from the occurrence of the located object in place necessarily follows the interpenetration of two dimensions, but interpenetration of two dimensions is impossible, so the statement that place is dimension must be impossible. As for the explanation of the conditional: it is that when the located object occurs in place, in that case either both dimensions remain together, or both cease together, or one remains and the other ceases. As for the second division: that they both cease together, this is false, otherwise it would necessitate that the non-existent located object occurs in non-existent place, and that is impossible. As for the third division: it is also false, otherwise it would necessitate that the non-existent located object occurs in existing place or vice versa. That is also false. When these two divisions were invalidated, the first division remained, which is that each one of them exists. Then we say: Are they said to have become united in this case, or is it not so? The first is false, because in the state of union, if they both exist, they are two, not one. And if they both cease and a third thing occurs, then this is not a union of the two, but an occurrence of a third. And if one remains and the second ceases, then union here is also impossible, because the non-existent and the existent cannot be one thing. When the statement of union was invalidated, it is established that each one of these two dimensions — I mean the dimension of the located object and the dimension of place — remains during the state of penetration. So what we mentioned is correct: that if place were a dimension, then from the occurrence of the located object in place would necessarily follow the interpenetration of two dimensions.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument of Aristotle and his followers against the view that place (al-makan) is a self-subsistent dimension (bu'd). The argument is a conditional proof: if place is a dimension, then when something occupies place it causes the interpenetration (tadakhul) of two dimensions (the dimension of the object and the dimension of place); but interpenetration is impossible; therefore place cannot be a dimension. The passage carefully proves the conditional by enumerating and eliminating possibilities. When an object occupies a place, the two dimensions (object's and place's) must either (1) both remain, (2) both vanish, or (3) one remains and the other vanishes. Options (2) and (3) are rejected because they would entail that a non-existent object occupies a place (or vice versa), which is absurd. Only option (1) remains: both exist. Then the author asks whether the two dimensions become united (ittihad) or not. He rejects union because: if they both exist, they remain two; if both cease and a third arises, that is not union; if one ceases, the existent and non-existent cannot be one. Since union is impossible, the two dimensions must remain distinct, leading to their interpenetration—one dimension within the other. Thus the conditional stands: if place is a dimension, interpenetration follows. Since interpenetration is deemed impossible, the original claim is false.

Key Terms
مكان
بعد
تداخل
متمكن
اتحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes interpenetration is impossible without proof.
Correction: The argument first proves the conditional that interpenetration follows; the impossibility of interpenetration is argued separately in the subsequent sections.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument against the view that place is a dimension?
A) Place must be a substance, not an accident.
B) If place is a dimension, then a located object would cause interpenetration of two dimensions.
C) Dimensions are indivisible and cannot overlap.
D) Place is relative and not an absolute dimension.

Key Takeaway
This passage provides the first of several arguments against place as dimension, showing that if place were a dimension, interpenetration would necessarily occur—an objection that the author will later refute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine the logical possibilities in this argument, are you someone who exhausts all alternatives before forming a belief about your faith, or do you rely on comfortable assumptions? Specifically, in what area of your religious practice have you settled for an easy answer without thinking through the alternatives?
?
The argument declares interpenetration impossible—yet God's power is not constrained by physical laws. Does your understanding of God's attributes allow for the possibility of interpenetration? How does this challenge your mental image of God's relationship to creation?
?
This argument relies on reason to determine what is impossible. Where in your life do you trust reason over revelation, and where do you distrust it? Are you consistent? Let this lesson push you to examine whether you are truly honest in your intellectual pursuit of truth about God.

Proof of Impossibility of Interpenetration and an Objection
First proof and an objection about intuition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof against interpenetration of dimensions and a methodological objection regarding intuition.

Core Idea
If two dimensions interpenetrate, their sum is larger, but the sum equals each—a contradiction.

This passage presents the first of five proofs that the interpenetration of dimensions is impossible. The proof uses a reductio argument: if two dimensions interpenetrate, their sum must be greater than either alone, but since they occupy the same space, the sum equals each one—a contradiction. However, an objector questions the need for proof, suggesting that the impossibility should be intuitive. This raises a key methodological issue: can something be both intuitive and debated?

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the passage once to understand the reductio argument. Identify the premise, the inference, and the contradiction.
2
Examine the objection
After the proof, the objector challenges the necessity of the proof. Consider whether the impossibility is intuitive or requires demonstration.
3
Reflect on the methodological issue
Think about the objector's point: if the impossibility is intuitive, why give proofs? And if not, then the proof is needed, but opponents also claim intuition.
The text cuts off here, so the response is incomplete.

وأما بيان المقام الثاني وهو أن القول بتداخل البعدين محال. فقد احتجوا عليه بوجوه خمسة: الوجه الأول: قالوا: إذا كان البعدان موجودين فهما أزيد من البعد الواحد، وكل ما هو أزيد من الواحد، فهو أعظم، فذلك المجموع أعظم من الواحد، فيلزم أن يكون مجموع البعدين المتداخلين أعظم من الواحد، لكن ليس الأمر كذلك، لأن مجموع هذين البعدين ليس إلا الذي بين النهايات، وذلك هو بعينه قدر كل واحد منهما، فليس المجموع أعظم من الواحد. هذا خلف. ولقائل أن يقول: هل تزعمون أن القول بتداخل البعدين معلوم الامتناع بالبديهة، أو تزعمون أنه لا يعلم امتناع ذلك إلا بالدليل؟ فإن كان الحق هو الأول، فاتركوا ذكر هذه الدلائل، واقتصروا على ادعاء البديهة. ويرجع حاصل هذا الكلام إلى ادعاء أن مذهب القائلين بأن المكان هو البعد، مذهب معلوم البطلان ببديهة العقل، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل. لأن المعلومات البديهية لا يجوز وقوع الاختلاف فيها بين العقلاء. وأيضاً: فالقائلون بالبعد، يقولون: مذهبنا معلوم الصحة ببديهة العقل، وذلك لأنا نعلم بالضرورة أن بين طرفي الطاس...
And as for the explanation of the second position — that the statement of interpenetration of two dimensions is impossible — they argued for it by five proofs. The first proof: they said: If two dimensions exist, then they are greater than one dimension, and everything that is greater than one is larger, so that sum is larger than one. Thus it would necessarily follow that the sum of the interpenetrating two dimensions is larger than one. But it is not so, because the sum of these two dimensions is nothing but what is between the extremes, and that is exactly the measure of each one of them, so the sum is not larger than one. This is a contradiction. And someone may say: Do you claim that the statement of interpenetration of two dimensions is known to be impossible by intuition, or do you claim that its impossibility is not known except by proof? If the truth is the first, then abandon mentioning these proofs and suffice with the claim of intuition. The sum of this speech amounts to claiming that the view of those who say that place is dimension is a view known to be false by intuitive reason, and it is known that that is false. Because intuitive matters do not admit of disagreement among rational people. Also, those who say it is dimension say: our view is known to be true by intuitive reason, because we know necessarily that between the two ends of the cup...
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage begins the second part of the refutation: proving that interpenetration of two dimensions is impossible. The author states that the opponents have five proofs and presents the first one. The proof is a reductio ad absurdum: if two dimensions interpenetrate, their sum must be greater than either one alone (since each is one, and two is greater than one). But the sum of two interpenetrating dimensions is exactly the same as each dimension (since they occupy the same spatial interval between endpoints). Therefore the sum is not greater than one, which contradicts the initial inference. Hence interpenetration is impossible. The text then records an objection: 'Someone may say' — do you claim that the impossibility of interpenetration is known by intuition (badiha) or by proof? If by intuition, why bother giving proofs? And if by intuition, it should be universally acknowledged, but there is disagreement, so it cannot be intuitive. Moreover, the proponents of the dimension view also claim intuition for their position, giving the example of knowing necessarily that between the two ends of a cup... (the text cuts off here). The objection highlights a methodological issue: if the impossibility is intuitive, no proof is needed; but if it is not intuitive, then proof is required, yet the opponents also claim intuition. The response is incomplete, making this passage a presentation of the proof and the challenge.

Misconception: The objector is rejecting the argument itself.
Correction: The objector is not rejecting the argument but questioning its necessity if the impossibility is supposedly intuitive.

Could it be that you have not fully grasped the depth of an issue? What will you do to humbly test your certainty?
?
Notice that the opponents also claim intuition for their false view. This shows that sincere people can be sincerely wrong about what seems self-evident. Is there a belief you have always taken as obvious that might actually be a blind spot? Will you ask a trusted scholar to challenge your assumptions for the sake of your faith?

The Argument from Necessary Location
Presents first argument that location is necessary and not derivative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that every body necessarily has a location ('here' or 'there'), and that this location is not reducible to relations of contact or enclosure with other bodies.

Core Idea
Location is intrinsic to bodies, not a relation.

The author opens the fourth argument by observing a striking asymmetry: we can imagine a body that touches no other body and is not surrounded, but we cannot imagine a body without a 'here' or 'there'. This suggests that location is a fundamental, non-relational property of bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text of the fourth aspect and its literal translation. Focus on the contrast between what we can and cannot imagine.
The author uses the word 'مماس' (touching) and 'محیط' (surrounding).
2
Identify the key intuition
Notice that the author claims we can conceive a body without any contact or enclosure, but we cannot conceive it without a location.
This intuition is presented as self-evident.
3
Understand the conclusion
The necessary conclusion is that 'here' and 'there' point to something distinct from the body's relations to other bodies. This 'something' is space itself.
The argument does not prove space is a substance, only that it is a distinct dimension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : وهو أنه يمكننا أن نتصور جسماً لا يماسه جسم ، ولا يحيط به جسم، ولا يمكننا أن نتصور جسماً لا يصدق عليه أنه ههنا أو هناك ، فوجب أن يكون المعنى المشار إليه بقولنا ههنا وهناك : أمر مغاير لكون الجسم ممسوس جسم آخر ، أو كونه بحيث يحيط به جسم آخر .
الوجه الخامس : هو أنا نقول : بأن الماء في الكوز ، لا بمعنى كون الماء سارياً في جسم الكوز ، بل بمعنى كون الماء مماس بسطحه سطح الكوز . وصريح العقل حاكم بأن الماء حاصل في الفضاء المفترض بين طرفي الكوز بدليل : أن ذلك الفضاء باقي بعينه واحد بشخصه ثم تارة يحصل فيه الماء ، وأخرى يخرج منه الماء، وينتقل إليه الهواء ، وثالثاً يبقى الفضاء خالياً عن كل الأجسام عند من كانت الدلائل القطعية عنده على صحة القول بالخلاء ، وذلك يدل على أن ذلك الامتداد أمر مغاير لامتداد الأجسام الحاصلة في ذلك الفضاء
الوجه السادس : هو أن المشار إليه بقولنا ههنا وهناك : لا يقبل الانتقال والحركة. فإنه من المستحيل في بديهة العقل أن ينتقل ههنا إلى هناك ، وأن ينتقل هناك إلى ههنا ، أما الجسم الحاصل ههنا والجسم الحاصل هناك ، فإنه قابل للانتقال. فلما كان المعنى المشار إليه بقولنا ههنا وهناك : لا يقبل الانتقال البتة ، وكان الجسم الحاصل ههنا والجسم الحاصل هناك ، قابلاً للانتقال . علمنا : أن المشار إليه بقولنا ههنا وهناك : مغاير للجسم الذي يقبل الانتقال .
فهذه تنبيهات دالة على أن العلم الضروري حاصل بأن هذه الأحياز والجهات والمحاذيات أبعاد ممتدة ، وأنها مغايرة لأبعاد الأجسام المتحركة المتنقلة
ولو عرضت هذه الوجوه على جميع العقلاء لقضت عقولهم السليمة بأن الأمر كما ذكرناه ، والحصناه والله أعلم
The fourth aspect: that we can imagine a body that does not touch another body, nor is surrounded by a body, but we cannot imagine a body that does not apply to it that it is 'here' or 'there'. So it is necessary that the meaning indicated by our saying 'here' and 'there' is something different from the body being touched by another body, or from its being such that another body surrounds it.
The fifth aspect: that we say water is in the jug, not in the sense that water permeates the body of the jug, but in the sense that water touches its surface the surface of the jug. The clear intellect judges that water exists in the assumed space between the ends of the jug, evidenced by: that space remains identical, one in itself, then sometimes water occurs in it, and other times water exits from it, and air moves into it, and thirdly the space remains empty of all bodies according to one who has decisive proofs for the validity of the claim of void. And that indicates that that extension is something different from the extension of the bodies occurring in that space.
The sixth aspect: that what is indicated by our saying 'here' and 'there' does not admit transition and motion. For it is impossible in the immediacy of the intellect for 'here' to move to 'there', and for 'there' to move to 'here'. As for the body occurring here and the body occurring there, it is capable of transition. So since the meaning indicated by our saying 'here' and 'there' does not admit transition at all, and the body occurring here and the body occurring there is capable of transition, we know that what is indicated by our saying 'here' and 'there' is different from the body that admits transition.
These are indications that necessary knowledge exists that these spaces, directions, and opposites are extended dimensions, and that they are different from the dimensions of moving, transient bodies.
And if these aspects were presented to all rational people, their sound intellects would judge that the matter is as we have mentioned and enumerated. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments (wujuh) intended to show that space (al-makan) is a dimension distinct from bodies. The author begins with the fourth aspect: we can conceive of a body not touching or being surrounded by another body, but we cannot conceive of a body without a location 'here' or 'there'. Therefore, the meaning of 'here'/'there' must be something other than relations of contact or enclosure. The fifth aspect uses the example of water in a jug: the water does not permeate the jug's body but touches its inner surface. The space between the jug's sides remains the same even as water enters, leaves, and air replaces it, or even if it becomes empty. This shows that the extension (imtidad) of that space is different from the extension of the bodies within it. The sixth aspect notes that 'here' and 'there' themselves do not move or change, whereas the bodies located here or there can move. Thus, the referent of 'here' and 'there' is distinct from movable bodies. The author concludes that these are indications that necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri) tells us that spaces (ahyaz), directions (jihaat), and opposites (muḥadhiyat) are extended dimensions, different from the dimensions of moving bodies. He adds that any rational person would affirm this.

Key Terms
مكان (makan)
بعد (bu'd)
جهة (jiha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a body's location is nothing but its relations (distance, contact) to other bodies
Correction: The argument shows location persists even when all such relations are removed, proving it is a distinct dimension.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth argument claim we can imagine about a body?
A) A body without any location
B) A body without touching or being surrounded by another body
C) A body that is infinite
D) A body that is not in space

Key Takeaway
The necessity of location for every body, even in isolation, shows that location is a dimension distinct from bodies and their relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that you cannot imagine yourself without a location. Where are you right now, in this very moment? Are you aware that your body occupies a precise place that Allah has determined for you? What would change in your daily life if you truly lived with the certainty that your location is not accidental but part of divine decree?
?
The argument proves that location is not just a relation to other things but a real dimension of your body. Since you are always 'here' or 'there' until death, and after death you will be buried in a specific spot, does the location of your grave matter to you? Have you prepared yourself for the journey to that final, permanent location?

The Argument from the Jug Example
Second argument using the example of water in a jug.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the jug example demonstrates that the space inside the container remains constant and distinct from the bodies that occupy it.

Core Idea
The vessel's interior space is a stable dimension, not a property of the water.

The fifth argument uses a vivid everyday example: water in a jug. The author argues that the water does not permeate the jug's walls but merely touches them, and the same cavity successively contains water, air, or emptiness. This persistence shows that the extension of the cavity is independent of the bodies within.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English of the fifth aspect carefully. Note the three states of the jug: with water, with air, and possibly empty.
The phrase 'باقي بعينه واحد بشخصه' emphasizes identity over time.
2
Analyze the logical structure
The argument: (1) The same space persists as water is replaced by air or becomes empty. (2) Therefore, that space is something different from the bodies that occupy it.
This is an argument from permanence through change.
3
Connect to the broader claim
The extension (imtidad) of the jug's interior is a dimension that remains when bodies leave – proving it is not identical to any body's extension.
This supports the existence of space as an independent continuum.
