الوجه الرابع : وهو أنه يمكننا أن نتصور جسماً لا يماسه جسم ، ولا يحيط به جسم، ولا يمكننا أن نتصور جسماً لا يصدق عليه أنه ههنا أو هناك ، فوجب أن يكون المعنى المشار إليه بقولنا ههنا وهناك : أمر مغاير لكون الجسم ممسوس جسم آخر ، أو كونه بحيث يحيط به جسم آخر .
الوجه الخامس : هو أنا نقول : بأن الماء في الكوز ، لا بمعنى كون الماء سارياً في جسم الكوز ، بل بمعنى كون الماء مماس بسطحه سطح الكوز . وصريح العقل حاكم بأن الماء حاصل في الفضاء المفترض بين طرفي الكوز بدليل : أن ذلك الفضاء باقي بعينه واحد بشخصه ثم تارة يحصل فيه الماء ، وأخرى يخرج منه الماء، وينتقل إليه الهواء ، وثالثاً يبقى الفضاء خالياً عن كل الأجسام عند من كانت الدلائل القطعية عنده على صحة القول بالخلاء ، وذلك يدل على أن ذلك الامتداد أمر مغاير لامتداد الأجسام الحاصلة في ذلك الفضاء
الوجه السادس : هو أن المشار إليه بقولنا ههنا وهناك : لا يقبل الانتقال والحركة. فإنه من المستحيل في بديهة العقل أن ينتقل ههنا إلى هناك ، وأن ينتقل هناك إلى ههنا ، أما الجسم الحاصل ههنا والجسم الحاصل هناك ، فإنه قابل للانتقال. فلما كان المعنى المشار إليه بقولنا ههنا وهناك : لا يقبل الانتقال البتة ، وكان الجسم الحاصل ههنا والجسم الحاصل هناك ، قابلاً للانتقال . علمنا : أن المشار إليه بقولنا ههنا وهناك : مغاير للجسم الذي يقبل الانتقال .
فهذه تنبيهات دالة على أن العلم الضروري حاصل بأن هذه الأحياز والجهات والمحاذيات أبعاد ممتدة ، وأنها مغايرة لأبعاد الأجسام المتحركة المتنقلة
ولو عرضت هذه الوجوه على جميع العقلاء لقضت عقولهم السليمة بأن الأمر كما ذكرناه ، والحصناه والله أعلم
The fourth aspect: that we can imagine a body that does not touch another body, nor is surrounded by a body, but we cannot imagine a body that does not apply to it that it is 'here' or 'there'. So it is necessary that the meaning indicated by our saying 'here' and 'there' is something different from the body being touched by another body, or from its being such that another body surrounds it.
The fifth aspect: that we say water is in the jug, not in the sense that water permeates the body of the jug, but in the sense that water touches its surface the surface of the jug. The clear intellect judges that water exists in the assumed space between the ends of the jug, evidenced by: that space remains identical, one in itself, then sometimes water occurs in it, and other times water exits from it, and air moves into it, and thirdly the space remains empty of all bodies according to one who has decisive proofs for the validity of the claim of void. And that indicates that that extension is something different from the extension of the bodies occurring in that space.
The sixth aspect: that what is indicated by our saying 'here' and 'there' does not admit transition and motion. For it is impossible in the immediacy of the intellect for 'here' to move to 'there', and for 'there' to move to 'here'. As for the body occurring here and the body occurring there, it is capable of transition. So since the meaning indicated by our saying 'here' and 'there' does not admit transition at all, and the body occurring here and the body occurring there is capable of transition, we know that what is indicated by our saying 'here' and 'there' is different from the body that admits transition.
These are indications that necessary knowledge exists that these spaces, directions, and opposites are extended dimensions, and that they are different from the dimensions of moving, transient bodies.
And if these aspects were presented to all rational people, their sound intellects would judge that the matter is as we have mentioned and enumerated. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments (wujuh) intended to show that space (al-makan) is a dimension distinct from bodies. The author begins with the fourth aspect: we can conceive of a body not touching or being surrounded by another body, but we cannot conceive of a body without a location 'here' or 'there'. Therefore, the meaning of 'here'/'there' must be something other than relations of contact or enclosure. The fifth aspect uses the example of water in a jug: the water does not permeate the jug's body but touches its inner surface. The space between the jug's sides remains the same even as water enters, leaves, and air replaces it, or even if it becomes empty. This shows that the extension (imtidad) of that space is different from the extension of the bodies within it. The sixth aspect notes that 'here' and 'there' themselves do not move or change, whereas the bodies located here or there can move. Thus, the referent of 'here' and 'there' is distinct from movable bodies. The author concludes that these are indications that necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri) tells us that spaces (ahyaz), directions (jihaat), and opposites (muḥadhiyat) are extended dimensions, different from the dimensions of moving bodies. He adds that any rational person would affirm this.

Key Terms
امتداد (imtidad)
خلاء (khala')
مماس (mumass)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What evidence does the fifth argument use to show that the space in the jug is distinct from the water?
A) The water changes color
B) The same space remains while water leaves and air enters
C) The jug is made of clay
D) The water touches the jug's surface

Key Takeaway
The persistence of the same space despite changing occupants demonstrates that space is an independent dimension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the space inside a jug remains the same whether it holds water, air, or nothing, what does that reveal about the Creator's ability to sustain independent dimensions—and how does that humble your sense of control over your own 'space'?
?
The jug's interior is unchanged by what fills it. What does that teach you about your own soul? Is your core identity anchored in something that remains when worldly 'contents' come and go?
?
This argument shows that space is a real creation, distinct from the bodies in it. If Allah can create such a subtle reality, what else might He have created that you overlook? Will you today reflect on one invisible creation that deserves your gratitude?

The Argument from the Immobility of Place
Third argument contrasting mobility of place vs. bodies, plus overall conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'here' and 'there' themselves do not move, whereas bodies do, proving that location is distinct from movable bodies.

Core Idea
Place is fixed; bodies move through it.

The sixth argument points to a simple but profound fact: the referent of 'here' and 'there' never moves. We can say 'here' moves to 'there' only in a metaphorical sense; in reality, 'here' always stays here, and 'there' stays there. Bodies, however, change location. This difference in mobility reveals that location is not a property of bodies but an independent dimension.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English of the sixth aspect. Pay attention to the contrast between the immobility of 'here'/'there' and the mobility of bodies.
The author uses 'بديهة العقل' (immediacy of the intellect).
2
Identify the two premises
Premise 1: 'Here' and 'there' cannot move. Premise 2: Bodies located here and there can move. Conclusion: 'Here' and 'there' are not identical to the bodies.
This is a simple syllogism.
3
Understand the conclusion paragraph
The author concludes that these three arguments (fourth, fifth, sixth) together establish necessary knowledge that spaces, directions, and opposites are extended dimensions distinct from bodies.
The conclusion is universal: all rational people would affirm this.

The First Proof That Place is the Void Dimension
The author proves that place must be the void dimension, using the case of a stationary bird in moving air.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that place must be the void dimension rather than the encompassing surface, and why the alternative leads to contradiction.

Core Idea
If place were surface, a stationary bird in moving air would be moving—an absurdity.

This lesson explores the first of several proofs that place is the void dimension, not the surface. The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum: assuming place is surface leads to contradictory judgments about motion and rest. By examining the case of a bird standing in flowing air, the author demonstrates that the surface theory forces us to call a stationary object moving, which is impossible. Therefore, place must be the dimension itself.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The second group argues that knowledge of dimensions is inferential. Their first proof is a conditional argument against place being surface.
2
Identify the conditional statement
Read the passage and locate the if-then structure: 'If place were the surface, then a stationary object would be judged moving, and a moving object stationary.'
3
Examine the example
Consider the bird standing in air or stone in water. Note that they change contact surfaces while remaining stationary. This shows the absurd consequence.
Focus on the transfer from surface to surface.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the consequence is false, the premise that place is surface must be false. Therefore, place is the self-subsistent dimension.
5
Consider the alternative definition
If place is the void dimension, no absurdity arises because the body is stationary within that void.
The passage also includes an objection, but focus on the core proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفريق الثاني وهم القائلون بأن العلم بوجود هذه الأبعاد علم استدلالي :
فقد احتجوا عليه من وجوه :
الحجة الأولى : لولم يكن المكان عبارة عن البعد ، بل كان عبارة عن السطح ، لزم الحكم على الساكن حال كونه ساكناً بأنه متحرك ، ولزم الحكم على المتحرك حال كونه متحركاً، بأنه ساكن . ولما كان ذلك باطلاً ، وجب أن يكون المكان : عبارة عن البعد القائم بنفسه ، لا عن السطح الحاوي .
أما بيان أنه يلزم الحكم على الساكن حال كونه ساكناً بأنه متحرك . فتقريره : أنا لو فرضنا طائراً واقفاً في الهواء ، والهواء يجري عليه ، أو حجراً واقفاً في الماء ، والماء يجري عليه . فههنا لا شك أن هذا الطير واقف في الهواء ، وهذا الحجر واقف في الماء ، مع أنه في كل آن ، ينتقل من مماسة سطح إلى سطح آخر . فلو كان المكان عبارة عن السطح الحاوي ، لكانت الحركة عبارة عن الانتقال من سطح إلى سطح آخر ، ولما حصل هذا الانتقال في هذه الصورة من سطح إلى سطح آخر ، لزم أن يكون هذا الجسم متحركاً مع أنه ساكن في الحقيقة . مع ما ذكرنا : أنه لو كان المكان عبارة عن السطح ، لزم الحكم على الجسم الساكن ، حال كونه ساكناً بأنه متحرك . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . فعلمنا : أن المكان لا يجوز أن يكون عبارة عن السطح . أما إذا قلنا : إن المكان عبارة عن البعد الخالي ، والفضاء الصرف، لم يلزم ذلك . لأن ذلك الجسم لما كان واقفاً في ذلك الفضاء ، وفي ذلك الخلاء ، لا جرم كان ساكناً لا متحركاً . فإن قيل : إن عنيت بكون ذلك الطير ساكناً في الهواء ، وكون ذلك الحجر واقفاً في الماء : أن مسامتته مع الأجسام الساكنة باقية . فالأمر كذلك
As for the second group, who say that knowledge of the existence of these dimensions is inferential knowledge:
They have argued for it with aspects:
The first proof: If place were not the dimension, but rather the surface, then it would necessarily follow that the stationary object while being stationary is judged as moving, and the moving object while being moving is judged as stationary. And since that is false, place must be the self-subsistent dimension, not the encompassing surface.
As for the explanation that it would follow that the stationary object while being stationary is judged as moving: its exposition is that if we suppose a bird standing in the air, and the air flows over it, or a stone standing in water, and the water flows over it, then here there is no doubt that this bird is standing in the air, and this stone is standing in the water, despite the fact that at every moment it transfers from touching one surface to another surface. So if place were the encompassing surface, then motion would be the transfer from surface to another surface, and since this transfer occurs in this case from surface to another surface, it would necessarily follow that this body is moving while it is in reality stationary. Added to what we mentioned: that if place were the surface, it would necessarily follow that a stationary body while being stationary is judged as moving. And it is known that this is false. So we know that place cannot be the surface. As for if we say that place is the empty dimension, the pure void, that does not follow. Because that body, since it is standing in that void, in that vacuum, is necessarily stationary, not moving. If it is said: if you mean by that bird being stationary in the air, and that stone being stationary in water, that its coincidence with stationary bodies remains, the matter is so.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-hujjah al-ula) from the second group, who claim that knowledge of these dimensions is inferential (istidlali). The proof is a conditional argument: if place (al-makan) were the surrounding surface (al-sath al-hawi) rather than the dimension (al-bu'd), then absurd consequences follow. Specifically, a stationary object in a moving medium (like a bird in flowing air or a stone in flowing water) would constantly change the surfaces it touches, and if motion is defined as change of surface (since place is surface), then the stationary object would be judged as moving, and conversely a moving object could be considered stationary if it stays on the same surface. Since these consequences are false, place cannot be surface; it must be the self-subsistent dimension (al-bu'd al-qaim bi nafsihi). The author then elaborates the case: a bird standing in air changes contact surfaces every moment, so if motion is transfer between surfaces, the bird would be moving, but it is actually stationary. The absurdity is clear. The conclusion is that place is the void dimension (al-bu'd al-khali) and pure space (al-fada' al-sirf), and under that definition, no absurdity follows because the bird is stationary within that void, not changing place. The passage ends with an objection (fa-in qila) that perhaps 'stationary' means constant coincidence with stationary bodies, but the text cuts off mid-answer. The argument as given effectively refutes the surface theory by showing its contradiction.

Key Terms
مكان (makan)
بعد (bu'd)
سطح (sath)
حركة (haraka)
سكون (sukun)
خلاء (khala')
فضاء (fada')
مماسة (mumassa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that motion is defined as change of contact surfaces.
Correction: The proof does not assume that definition; it shows that if place were surface, then motion would have to be defined as change of surfaces, leading to absurdity. The definition of motion itself is not assumed but derived from the definition of place.

Checkpoint
What absurdity follows from defining place as the surrounding surface, according to the first proof?
A) A moving body would be considered stationary
B) A stationary body would be considered moving
C) Both A and B
D) Neither A nor B

Key Takeaway
Place cannot be the encompassing surface because that would force us to call a stationary body moving, which is impossible; place must be the void dimension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof challenges you to reconsider beliefs that lead to logical contradictions. Are there areas in your life—your understanding of God, yourself, or your purpose—where you are holding onto an idea that, if followed consistently, leads to absurdity? Will you surrender comfort for truth and seek the correct definition, even if it feels unfamiliar?
?
The author shows that a wrong definition of 'place' leads to misjudging reality. Reflect: In your actions and intentions, are you operating under a false definition of what truly matters—like defining success by worldly standards? When you realize your definition leads to contradiction (e.g., chasing worldly gain yet claiming contentment), what change must you make today?
?
The bird in flowing air is called stationary, yet the air moves around it. Are you like that bird—unchanged inside while everything around you shifts? Or are you being moved by the currents of your time, mistaking activity for progress? Define your 'place' before God: Are you standing still in His service, or are you drifting without realizing it?

6 min
The First Proof That Place Is Void
Razi presents the first proof that place is the void, using a reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that place must be the void dimension rather than the containing surface, using the example of a stationary object in a moving medium.

Core Idea
If place were a surface, a stationary bird in moving air would be moving — an absurdity.

Razi begins his first proof by considering what would follow if place were defined as the containing surface. He shows that this leads to a contradiction: a stationary object in a moving medium would have to be considered moving because it constantly changes the surfaces it contacts. This absurdity forces us to conclude that place is not surface but the void dimension.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the opening conditional: if place were surface, then stationary would be judged moving. Notice the logical structure: if P then Q, Q false, so not P.
2
Examine the example
Consider the bird in air and stone in water. They are stationary yet change surfaces. This illustrates the absurd consequence.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the consequence is false, place cannot be surface; it must be the self-subsistent dimension/void. This is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفريق الثاني وهم القائلون بأن العلم بوجود هذه الأبعاد علم استدلالي : فقد احتجوا عليه من وجوه : الحجة الأولى : لولم يكن المكان عبارة عن البعد ، بل كان عبارة عن السطح ، لزم الحكم على الساكن حال كونه ساكناً بأنه متحرك ، ولزم الحكم على المتحرك حال كونه متحركاً، بأنه ساكن . ولما كان ذلك باطلاً ، وجب أن يكون المكان : عبارة عن البعد القائم بنفسه ، لا عن السطح الحاوي . أما بيان أنه يلزم الحكم على الساكن حال كونه ساكناً بأنه متحرك . فتقريره : أنا لو فرضنا طائراً واقفاً في الهواء ، والهواء يجري عليه ، أو حجراً واقفاً في الماء ، والماء يجري عليه . فههنا لا شك أن هذا الطير واقف في الهواء ، وهذا الحجر واقف في الماء ، مع أنه في كل آن ، ينتقل من مماسة سطح إلى سطح آخر . فلو كان المكان عبارة عن السطح الحاوي ، لكانت الحركة عبارة عن الانتقال من سطح إلى سطح آخر ، ولما حصل هذا الانتقال في هذه الصورة من سطح إلى سطح آخر ، لزم أن يكون هذا الجسم متحركاً مع أنه ساكن في الحقيقة . مع ما ذكرنا : أنه لو كان المكان عبارة عن السطح ، لزم الحكم على الجسم الساكن ، حال كونه ساكناً بأنه متحرك . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . فعلمنا : أن المكان لا يجوز أن يكون عبارة عن السطح . أما إذا قلنا : إن المكان عبارة عن البعد الخالي ، والفضاء الصرف، لم يلزم ذلك . لأن ذلك الجسم لما كان واقفاً في ذلك الفضاء ، وفي ذلك الخلاء ، لا جرم كان ساكناً لا متحركاً . فإن قيل : إن عنيت بكون ذلك الطير ساكناً في الهواء ، وكون ذلك الحجر واقفاً في الماء : أن مسامتته مع الأجسام الساكنة باقية . فالأمر كذلك إلا أن السكون بهذا التفسير غير مشروط بكونه في نفسه ساكناً . إلا ترى أن الصندوق إذا وضع فيه جسم، وفرضنا هوى ذلك الصندوق مع كل ما فيه ، فإن مسامتة تلك الأجسام الموضوعة في الصندوق مع جوانب ذلك الصندوق باقية غير متغيرة ، مع أن ذلك الصندوق، وكل ما فيه متحرك . فعلمنا : أن مسامتة ذلك الطير، وذلك الحجر مع سائر الأجسام ، لا يمنع من كونه في نفسه متحركاً . وإن عنيت بكونه ساكناً أنه بقي في ذلك الفضاء الذي هو حاصل فيه ومداخل له . فهذا الكلام إنما يتم بعد ثبوت القول بالفضاء ، فلو أثبتنا القول بالفضاء بهذه المقدمة لزم الدوروهو محال . والجواب : أما أن الطير قد يقف في الهواء ، مع أن الهواء يمر عليه ، والحجر قد يقف في الماء مع أن الماء يجري عليه . فذلك معلوم بالضرورة ، والمنع من كونه ساكناً في هذه الحالة مكابرة في البديهيات . وأما تفسير السكون ببقاء المسامنات فهو باطل . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن الجسمين المتساويين في الثقل والحجم والشكل ، إذا ابتدأ بالنزول من رأس الجبل في هواء صاف لا تفاوت فيه البتة ، فههنا يبقى سمت كل واحد منهما مع الآخر ، من أول هذه الحركة إلى أخرها . فههنا المسامتة باقية مع كون كل واحد منها متحركاً ، فعلمنا : أنه لا يمكن تفسير كون الجسم ساكناً ببقاء مسامنته مع غيره . الثاني : إن الفلك الحاوي، والفلك المحوي، إذا كانا يتممان الدورة في مدة واحدة ، فهناك لا يتغير شيء من المسامات الحاصلة بين الأجزاء المفترضة في كل واحد منهما ، مع كون كل واحد منهما متحركاً في نفسه . الثالث : إن الجسمين من بقيا في ذاتيهما على السكون ، كان سكونها في ذاتيهما علة لبقاء المسامتة الحاصلة بينهما ، وذلك لأن سكونه في ذاته أمر حاصل له في ذاته ، وأما مسامته مع غيره فهي أمر يحصل له بالنسبة إلى غيره
As for the second group—those who say that knowledge of the existence of these dimensions is inferential knowledge—they have argued for it from [several] aspects. The first proof: If place (al-makan) were not an expression for the dimension (al-bu‘d), but rather an expression for the surface (al-sath), then it would necessarily follow that a stationary thing be judged, while being stationary, as moving, and that a moving thing be judged, while being moving, as stationary. And since that is false, place must be an expression for the self-subsistent dimension, not for the encompassing surface. As for the explanation that it necessarily follows that a stationary thing be judged, while being stationary, as moving: its exposition is that if we suppose a bird standing in the air while the air flows over it, or a stone standing in water while the water flows over it, then here there is no doubt that this bird is standing in the air and this stone is standing in the water, even though in every instant it transfers from contact with one surface to another surface. So if place were the encompassing surface, then motion would be the transfer from surface to surface, and since this transfer occurs in this case from surface to surface, it would necessarily follow that this body be moving when in reality it is stationary. Along with what we mentioned: that if place were the surface, it would necessarily follow that a stationary body be judged, while being stationary, as moving. And it is known that that is false. So we know that place cannot be an expression for the surface. But if we say that place is an expression for the empty dimension (al-bu‘d al-khālī) and pure space (al-faḍā’ al-ṣirf), that does not follow. Because that body, when it is standing in that space and in that void, necessarily is stationary, not moving. And if it is said: If you mean by that bird being stationary in the air and that stone standing in water that its contiguity (musāmatatuhu) with stationary bodies persists—the matter is so, except that stillness (al-sukūn) according to this interpretation is not conditioned by its being in itself stationary. Do you not see that if a body is placed in a chest, and we suppose the falling of that chest with everything in it, then the contiguity of those bodies placed in the chest with the sides of that chest persists unchanged, even though that chest and everything in it is moving? So we know that the contiguity of that bird and that stone with the rest of the bodies does not prevent it from being in itself moving. And if you mean by its being stationary that it remains in that space which it occupies and which encompasses it, then this statement is only valid after establishing the doctrine of space (al-faḍā’). So if we establish the doctrine of space by this premise, circularity (al-dawr) would follow, and it is impossible. The answer: As for the bird standing in the air while the air passes over it, and the stone standing in water while the water flows over it—that is known necessarily, and denying that it is stationary in this state is stubbornness against self-evident truths. As for the interpretation of stillness as persistence of contiguities (baqā’ al-musāmanāt), it is false. Evidence for it [comes from] [several] aspects: First: If two bodies equal in weight, volume, and shape begin to descend from the top of a mountain in pure air with no difference whatsoever, then here the alignment (samt) of each one with the other remains from the beginning of this motion to its end. Here contiguity persists even though each one is moving. So we know that it is impossible to interpret a body’s being stationary by the persistence of its contiguity with another. Second: If the encompassing sphere (al-falak al-ḥāwī) and the encompassed sphere (al-falak al-maḥwī) complete a rotation in the same period, then there no change occurs in any of the contiguities occurring between the hypothetical parts in each of them, even though each one is moving in itself. Third: If two bodies remain in their essences upon stillness, their stillness in their essences is a cause for the persistence of the contiguity occurring between them; and that is because its stillness in itself is a thing that occurs to it in itself, whereas its contiguity with another is a thing that occurs to it in relation to another.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-ḥujjah al-ūlā) of those who claim that knowledge of the existence of dimensions is inferential (ʿilm istidlālī). The author first states the conclusion of the proof: if place were a surface, then a stationary object would be judged moving and a moving object stationary; since that is absurd, place must be the self-subsistent dimension (al-buʿd al-qāʾim bi-nafsihi), i.e., the void. The proof is then explained with an example: a bird standing in moving air or a stone in flowing water—these objects are stationary in reality, yet they constantly change the surfaces they contact. If motion were transfer from surface to surface, they would be moving, which contradicts their perceived stillness. Therefore, place cannot be surface but must be the empty dimension/space. The author then anticipates an objection: perhaps 'stillness' here means persistence of contiguity (musāmata) with stationary bodies. The reply distinguishes between the object's own state and its relation to others. Using the example of a chest falling with a body inside, the contiguity between the body and the chest persists even though both are moving. Thus, persistence of contiguity does not entail stillness. The objection continues: perhaps stillness means remaining in the same space (faḍāʾ). The author responds that this would require assuming the existence of space (faḍāʾ) to prove space, which is circular (dawr). The answer then refutes the interpretation of stillness as persistence of contiguity with three arguments. First: two identical bodies falling together maintain alignment and contiguity yet are moving. Second: concentric spheres rotating at the same speed maintain contiguity between parts yet move. Third: stillness in itself is a cause of contiguity, not the other way around—contiguity is a relational accident, not constitutive of stillness. This refutation solidifies the original proof that place is the void.

Key Terms
مكان (makan)
بعد (bu'd)
سطح (sath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence follows if place were a surface, according to the first proof?
A) A moving object would be judged stationary.
B) A stationary object in a moving medium would be judged moving.
C) Place would be void.
D) Motion would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that place cannot be the containing surface because that would contradict the observable difference between motion and stillness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What assumption in your own life do you hold so firmly that you have never questioned it, even though it may lead to contradictions? Will you test it with the same honesty Razi uses here?
?
Razi shows that our immediate intuitions about reality can be mistaken. What is one belief you have about your own 'place' in the world that might be based on changing surfaces rather than an unchanging truth? How can you ground yourself in the latter?
?
If you were to apply the same rigorous reasoning to your spiritual state, what premise about yourself would you need to examine first, even if it feels uncomfortable? Commit to examining it this week.

Objection and Refutation: Stillness as Contiguity
Razi answers the objection that stillness is persistence of contiguity, using three arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that stillness might be persistence of contiguity, and Razi's three arguments refuting that interpretation.

Core Idea
Stillness is not the same as persistence of contiguity with other bodies.

After presenting the first proof, Razi anticipates an objection: perhaps 'stillness' means persistence of contiguity with stationary bodies. He shows this interpretation is false through three arguments, using examples of falling bodies and rotating spheres. This strengthens the original conclusion that place is the void.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector suggests that the bird is stationary because its contiguity with stationary bodies persists. Note the two interpretations offered: contiguity persistence or remaining in same space.
2
See the first argument
Two identical bodies falling together maintain contiguity yet move — contiguity does not entail stillness. This is a direct counterexample.
3
See the second argument
Concentric spheres rotating together maintain contiguity yet move. Another counterexample.
4
See the third argument
Stillness in itself is the cause of contiguity, not the other way around. This establishes the correct order.

As for the second group—those who say that knowledge of the existence of these dimensions is inferential knowledge—they have argued for it from [several] aspects. The first proof: If place (al-makan) were not an expression for the dimension (al-bu‘d), but rather an expression for the surface (al-sath), then it would necessarily follow that a stationary thing be judged, while being stationary, as moving, and that a moving thing be judged, while being moving, as stationary. And since that is false, place must be an expression for the self-subsistent dimension, not for the encompassing surface. As for the explanation that it necessarily follows that a stationary thing be judged, while being stationary, as moving: its exposition is that if we suppose a bird standing in the air while the air flows over it, or a stone standing in water while the water flows over it, then here there is no doubt that this bird is standing in the air and this stone is standing in the water, even though in every instant it transfers from contact with one surface to another surface. So if place were the encompassing surface, then motion would be the transfer from surface to surface, and since this transfer occurs in this case from surface to surface, it would necessarily follow that this body be moving when in reality it is stationary. Along with what we mentioned: that if place were the surface, it would necessarily follow that a stationary body be judged, while being stationary, as moving. And it is known that that is false. But if we say that place is an expression for the empty dimension (al-bu‘d al-khālī) and pure space (al-faḍā’ al-ṣirf), that does not follow. Because that body, when it is standing in that space and in that void, necessarily is stationary, not moving. And if it is said: If you mean by that bird being stationary in the air and that stone standing in water that its contiguity (musāmatatuhu) with stationary bodies persists—the matter is so, except that stillness (al-sukūn) according to this interpretation is not conditioned by its being in itself stationary. So if we establish the doctrine of space by this premise, circularity (al-dawr) would follow, and it is impossible. The answer: As for the bird standing in the air while the air passes over it, and the stone standing in water while the water flows over it—that is known necessarily, and denying that it is stationary in this state is stubbornness against self-evident truths. Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-ḥujjah al-ūlā) of those who claim that knowledge of the existence of dimensions is inferential (ʿilm istidlālī). The author first states the conclusion of the proof: if place were a surface, then a stationary object would be judged moving and a moving object stationary; since that is absurd, place must be the self-subsistent dimension (al-buʿd al-qāʾim bi-nafsihi), i.e., the void. The proof is then explained with an example: a bird standing in moving air or a stone in flowing water—these objects are stationary in reality, yet they constantly change the surfaces they contact. If motion were transfer from surface to surface, they would be moving, which contradicts their perceived stillness. Therefore, place cannot be surface but must be the empty dimension/space. The author then anticipates an objection: perhaps 'stillness' here means persistence of contiguity (musāmata) with stationary bodies. The reply distinguishes between the object's own state and its relation to others. Using the example of a chest falling with a body inside, the contiguity between the body and the chest persists even though both are moving. Thus, persistence of contiguity does not entail stillness. The objection continues: perhaps stillness means remaining in the same space (faḍāʾ). The author responds that this would require assuming the existence of space (faḍāʾ) to prove space, which is circular (dawr). The answer then refutes the interpretation of stillness as persistence of contiguity with three arguments. First: two identical bodies falling together maintain alignment and contiguity yet are moving. Second: concentric spheres rotating at the same speed maintain contiguity between parts yet move. Third: stillness in itself is a cause of contiguity, not the other way around—contiguity is a relational accident, not constitutive of stillness. This refutation solidifies the original proof that place is the void.

Key Terms
سكون (sukun)
مسامتة (musamata)
دور (dawr)
فضاء (fada')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Stillness is the same as persistence of contiguity with stationary bodies.
Correction: Persistence of contiguity can occur while an object is moving, as shown by falling bodies and rotating spheres; thus contiguity does not define stillness.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three arguments Razi gives against interpreting stillness as persistence of contiguity?
A) Two identical bodies falling together maintain contiguity yet move.
B) A stone in water is stationary even though water flows.
C) Concentric spheres rotating together maintain contiguity yet move.
D) Stillness in itself is the cause of contiguity.

Key Takeaway
Stillness is not defined by contiguity with other bodies; rather, stillness in itself causes contiguity, not the reverse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you define your identity or worth based on external relations (e.g., status, opinions of others) rather than an intrinsic state? How might you shift to the intrinsic stillness that Razi defends?
?
Razi refutes an objection by showing it leads to circular reasoning. Where in your beliefs or habits do you use circular arguments that prevent you from seeing the truth? Will you confront one today?
?
The objector stubbornly denied the self-evident fact that a bird can be stationary while air flows past. Are there self-evident truths about your own soul—like your need for God, your mortality, or your accountability—that you stubbornly resist? What would it take to surrender to that clear proof?

Proof that the Void Cannot Be Annihilated
The author argues that void cannot cease to exist, using two proofs, and rejects an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that the void cannot cease to exist, based on the necessity of spatial distinctions.

Core Idea
The void is necessary for spatial distinctions; if it vanished, left and right would become meaningless.

In this passage, the author of Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a rational proof that the void cannot be annihilated. The argument relies on the idea that spatial differentiation (right/left, above/below) depends on the existence of void. This lesson walks through that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Note that the passage is part of a larger work and may cut off at the end.
The argument is a priori, not empirical.
2
Identify the two proofs presented
The author states the majority of ancients agreed that void does not admit nonexistence, and they argued in two ways. Find the first proof (hypothetical about spatial distinction) and the second proof (dismissed as invalid by intuition).
The second proof is not fully stated; only its rejection is given.
3
Understand the objection and its refutation
Notice that someone might object that the spatial distinction is merely a product of wahm (estimation) or imagination. The author claims this has already been refuted earlier in the text.
This objection is dismissed quickly.
4
Grasp the conclusion about empty dimensions
The conclusion is that empty dimensions (void) cannot cease to exist because assuming their nonexistence forces the impossibility of losing all spatial differentiation.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع في فروع القول بالظاء البتة وهي فروع: الفرع الأول: اتفق جمهور القدماء على أن الخلاء لا يقبل العدم واحتجوا عليه بوجهين: الأول: إن كل ما كان ممكناً، فإنا لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، فلنفرض أن الخلاء قد عدم، فنقول عند وقوع هذا الفرض: هل يتميز جانب اليمين عن جانب اليسار، وجانب الفوق عن جانب التحت، أو لا يتميز؟ والثاني باطل بالبديهة، لأن القول بحصول حالة، وفرض أخرى لا يبقى معها هذا الامتياز: أمر لا يقبله العقل، ولا يتصوره الخيال [وقول من يقول: إنه من حكم الوهم، ومن عمل الخيال: فقد أبطلناه فيما تقدم] ولما بطل هذا القسم ثبت أن الحق هو القسم الأول، وهو أن عند فرض عدم هذا الخلاء، لا يكون امتياز اليمين عن اليسار، والفوق عن التحت: حاصلاً. وإذا كان كذلك، فهذه الأبعاد الخالية أمور يلزم من فرض عدمها، فرض
Chapter Seven: On the Branches of the Statement on al-Dhad (الظاء). Among its branches: The first branch: The majority of the ancients agreed that the void (الخلاء) does not permit nonexistence (العدم). They argued for it in two respects: The first: That everything that is possible, from assuming its occurrence it is not necessary that something impossible follows. So let us assume that the void has become nonexistent. Then we say, at the occurrence of this assumption: Is the right side distinguished from the left side, and the above side from the below side, or is it not distinguished? The second is invalid by intuition, because the statement that a state occurs and another assumption with which this distinction does not remain is something the intellect does not accept and the imagination cannot conceive. [And the saying of one who says: 'It is from the ruling of wahm (estimation) and from the act of imagination' — we have already refuted it earlier.] And when this part became invalid, it is established that the truth is the first part, which is that when the nonexistence of this void is assumed, the distinction of right from left and above from below does not occur. And if that is so, then these empty dimensions (الأبعاد الخالية) are things whose assumption of nonexistence necessitates the assumption of
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the void cannot cease to exist. It begins by stating the consensus of the majority of the ancients that the void does not admit nonexistence. Two proofs are given. The first proof uses a hypothetical: if the void were to cease to exist, we ask whether spatial distinctions (right/left, above/below) would remain. If they do not remain, that distinction would be lost, which is impossible by intuition. The second proof is declared invalid by intuition because it posits a state where such distinctions vanish, which the mind cannot accept. The author then refutes an objection that this might be a product of wahm (estimation) or imagination, claiming it was refuted earlier. Therefore the correct view is that the void cannot cease to exist, because its nonexistence would imply the absence of all spatial differentiation. The argument concludes that empty dimensions are such that assuming their nonexistence forces an impossible consequence.

Key Terms
الخَلَاء (al-khalāʼ, void)
العَدَم (al-ʿadam, nonexistence)
الِامْتِيَاز (al-imtiyāz, distinction)
الوَهْم (al-wahm, estimation/fancy)
الأَبْعَاد الخَالِيَة (al-abʿād al-khāliyah, empty dimensions)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument relies on empirical observation or physical experiment.
Correction: The argument is a priori and rational; it does not depend on sensory experience but on the logical necessity of spatial distinctions.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that the void cannot cease to exist?
A) That assuming its nonexistence would necessitate a contradiction in spatial distinctions.
B) That the void is eternal and has no beginning.
C) That the void is an imaginary concept and not real.
D) That the ancients all agreed it is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The void is necessary for spatial distinctions, so it cannot cease to exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the void is necessary for you to distinguish your right from your left, could it be that you also depend on something necessary that you neglect? What if your very existence is contingent upon a Necessary Being? This proof shows that even emptiness has a kind of necessity. How does that challenge your sense of self-sufficiency? Will you spend time today acknowledging your absolute dependence on the Creator?
?
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum to show that denying the void leads to an impossibility. Is there any assumption in your life that, if logically pursued, would lead to an absurd spiritual conclusion? For example, if you live as if this world is permanent, what absurdity does that lead to? This lesson calls you to examine the logical foundations of your worldview. Are you willing to follow the reasoning to its end, even if it disturbs your comfort?
?
The proof relies on the intuition that spatial distinctions cannot be absent. Your intuition about right and wrong is also a God-given gift. Have you suppressed your innate intuition (fitra) about the existence of God? This lesson on the necessity of void reminds you that the most obvious things are often the most important. What would it take for you to listen to that inner witness that points to the truth?

The Infinity of the Void Outside the World
Razi presents the third branch on infinite void and Aristotle's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate between proponents of an infinite void and Aristotle's objection that infinite dimensions are impossible.

Core Idea
The void outside the world is argued to be infinite, not finite.

This passage presents a key debate about the nature of the void beyond the cosmos. Proponents of the void argue it must be infinite, while Aristotle and his followers deem this impossible. The arguments hinge on whether a finite void would allow distinction between directions, and whether infinite dimensions can exist at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: statement of agreement, Aristotle's claim, proponents' argument, Aristotle's proof, and a note about further detail.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two opposing positions
Identify who holds each view: the proponents of void say it's infinite; Aristotle says that's impossible. Then note the reasoning each side gives.
The proponents' reasoning is based on the impossibility of a finite void having no side-distinction.
3
Examine the proponents' argument
Reflect on why a finite void would lack distinction between sides. If the void is finite, beyond its boundary there is nothing, so all directions beyond become identical. That is deemed impossible by the proponents.
This argument presupposes that space must have some differentiating feature.
4
Consider Aristotle's counter-proof
Aristotle's proof is simple: infinite dimensions are impossible. Note that the passage does not elaborate why; it promises a detailed discussion later.
This suggests the debate is ongoing and complex.
5
Connect to the broader discussion
The passage ends by signaling that a thorough treatment of the proofs will follow. This lesson is just an introduction to the debate.
Keep in mind that this is part of a larger work on theology and philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثالث : اتفق القائلون بإثبات هذا الخلاء ، على أن حصول هذا الخلاء خارج العالم غير متناه . وزعم « أرسطاطاليس » وأصحابه أن ذلك محال . واحتج الأولون بأنه لو تناهى هذا الخلاء ، لما بقي في الخارج عنه امتياز طرف من طرف ، وجانب عن جانب . وذلك محال . وحجة أرسطاطاليس : أن القول بوجود أبعاد غير متناهية : محال . واعلم أن الكلام في تلك الدلائل سيأتي بالاستقصاء.
The third branch: Those who affirm the existence of this void agreed that obtaining this void outside the world is infinite. Aristotle and his followers claimed that this is impossible. The first group argued that if this void were finite, there would not remain outside it a distinction of one side from another, and one direction from another—and that is impossible. Aristotle's proof: that affirming infinite dimensions is impossible. And know that the discussion of those proofs will come in detail.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents the third branch of a larger discussion about void (al-khala'). It begins by stating the agreed position of those who affirm void: that the void outside the world is infinite (ghayr mutanāhin). It then introduces Aristotle's objection that this is impossible (muḥāl). The proponents of infinite void argue that if the void were finite, there would be no way to distinguish one external side from another—all directions would be identical, which they consider absurd. Aristotle's counter-proof is simply that infinite dimensions are impossible. The passage concludes with a note that a full discussion of these proofs will come later in detail (bi'l-istiqṣā').

Key Terms
الخلاء (al-khala')
غير متناه (ghayr mutanāhin)
أرسطاطاليس (Aristotle)
امتياز (imtiyāz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the void must be finite because the world is finite.
Correction: The passage shows that proponents of the void argue it is infinite, based on the impossibility of distinguishing sides if it were finite. The debate is not settled by the world's finitude.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of those who affirm the void?
A) The void must be finite because the world is finite.
B) If the void were finite, there would be no distinction between sides outside it.
C) Aristotle proved that infinite dimensions are possible.
D) The void does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The void outside the world is argued by its proponents to be infinite, based on the necessity of side-distinction; Aristotle rejects this, asserting that infinite dimensions are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate the vastness of the void beyond this world, does it increase your awe of Allah's power, or does it feel distant? What keeps you from feeling that awe in your daily life?
?
The supporters argued that a finite void would lead to no distinction between sides, which they deemed impossible. Do you ever treat matters of faith as if they were 'finite' and neatly contained, avoiding the uncomfortable infinity of Allah's attributes? How can you embrace that infinity in your worship?
?
If the void is infinite, then creation is not enclosed; does that challenge your mental image of the universe? How does this reshape your understanding of Allah's statement that He is 'with you wherever you are'? What step will you take this week to live with that reality?

The Theory of Void Mixture and Density
Razi reports the theory that void mixture causes differences in density.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ancient theory that differences in density are caused by varying degrees of void mixture within bodies.

Core Idea
Density is determined by how much void is mixed into a body.

In this passage, Razi presents a physical theory explaining why some bodies are subtle (light) and others dense (heavy). According to this view, the key factor is the presence of void (empty space) mixed with the body's parts. The more void, the subtler the body. This theory was one attempt to explain material differences before modern atomic theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the main claim.
The passage is a report of a theory, not necessarily Razi's own view.
2
Examine the examples
Notice the examples of earth, water, air, fire, and celestial spheres. See how each fits the pattern of void mixture.
3
Reflect on the theory's scope
Consider that the theory extends from earthly elements to celestial spheres, showing its universal ambition.
The passage cuts off at the spheres; the next page might continue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الخامس : من الناس من قال : امتياز اللطيف عن الكثيف ، إنما حصل بسبب مخالطة الخلاء. فالأجزاء التي يتولد منها الجسم المخصوص ، إن كانت بحيث تخالطها أجزاء الخلاء ، فهو الجسم اللطيف، وإن كان لا يخالطها أجزاء الخلاء فهو الكثيف . ثم كلما كانت مخالطة الخلاء أكثر ، كانت اللطافة أكثر . والأرض لم تخالطها أجزاء الخلاء ، فلا جرم كانت في غاية الكثافة والماء يخالطه أجزاء الخلاء ، فلا جرم حصلت اللطافة فيه . وأما الأجزاء الهوائية فمخالطة الهواء لها أكثر ، فلا جرم كان الهواء الطف من الماء . وكذلك القول في النار . ثم في الأفلاك .
The fifth branch: Some people said: The distinction between the subtle and the dense only occurs due to mixture with void. The parts from which a particular body is generated, if they are such that parts of void mix with them, then it is the subtle body, and if parts of void do not mix with them, then it is the dense. Then, whenever the mixture of void is more, the subtlety is more. Earth: parts of void did not mix with it, so it was at the utmost density. Water: parts of void mix with it, so subtlety occurred in it. As for airy parts, the mixture of air with them is more, so air is subtler than water. And likewise for fire. Then for the celestial spheres.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a physical theory reported by Razi to explain the distinction between subtle (laṭīf) and dense (kathīf) bodies by the degree of mixture (mukhālata) with void (al-khalā'). According to this theory, if the parts of a body are mixed with void parts, the body is subtle; if not, it is dense. The more void mixture, the greater the subtlety. Examples are given: earth has no void mixture, so it is utmost density; water has some, making it subtler; air has even more, making it subtler than water; fire is similar. The passage begins to mention celestial spheres but is cut off.

Key Terms
اللطيف (al-laṭīf)
الكثيف (al-kathīf)
مخالطة الخلاء (mukhālatal al-khalā')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Density is solely determined by the amount of matter packed into a body.
Correction: This theory proposes that density is determined by the mixture of void (empty space) within the body's parts, not just mass.

Checkpoint
According to the theory presented, what makes earth extremely dense?
A) High mixture of void parts.
B) Lack of mixture with void.
C) Low temperature.
D) Its large size.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between subtle and dense bodies, according to this theory, hinges on the presence and amount of void mixed with the body's parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This theory says that being 'subtle' depends on allowing void into your composition. In your own life, what 'void' of humility or openness have you been resisting, making you dense to truth? Will you let that void in today?
?
If earth with no void is 'utmost density', consider areas where you are so certain or rigid that no new thought can enter. What would it take for you to become 'subtler'—more receptive and light? What specific step will you take this week?
?
The passage presents a spectrum from dense to subtle. Where do you place yourself on a spiritual spectrum of density to subtlety? What is one void—one empty space of surrender—that you can allow to mix into your heart right now to move toward subtlety?
